THE RED PAGE.
Nietzsche.
REYNOLDS’S NEWSPAPER, in announc-
ing a cheap translation of one of Nietz-
sche’s books, says
Nietzsche is the most daring of individualists, yet with
the tenderest of hearts. Originality marks every line of
his writings. One may not agj-ee with half of what he
says, but his utterances are so profound and so striking
that they force thought. For mental stimulus, this is
the man in a worn-out and Mammon-worshipping a«e.
... Undoubtedly the most refreshing breeze of philo-
sophical thought that has blown across Europe in the
last half-century has come from the great German
thinker, I riedrich Nietzsche, the first part of whose great
prose epic, “Thus Spake Zarathustra,” translated by
Thomas Common, has just appeared as a Is. 6d. volume.
An eighteen-penny edition (in a translated form)
of a great philosophical and abstract work is
indeed, fame! James Thomson, when he found
in the ingle of a remote hostelry a well-thumbed
and dogs’-eared copy of his “Seasons,” was filled
with glee. “And yet the scamps say lam not
famous, ’ he shouted, and ordered free punch for
whomsoever liked to ask for it. When Nietzsche’s
death was announced he was written about very
much as if lie had been a Herr Most, shaggy, un-
washed, filthy, half-drowned in beer, half-suffo-
cated with the fumes of bad tobacco, writing
treatises on dynamite and the destruction of
society. As a matter of fact, Nietzsche was the
most fastidious and dainty of the enemies of
conventional civilisation. Condemning the German
intellect (as did Heme before him) for indulgence,
he said : “There is too much beer in the German
intellect. Nowhere else has there been so vicious
a misuse of the two great European narcotics—
Alcohol and Christianity.”
Nietzsche was a transplanted Pole. Two other
St Germans owed little to the “ Fatherland
ipenhauer was a Dutchman ; Heine was a
Jew. The philosopher of aristocratic individualism
was born in a small Saxon village near Lutzen in
1844. But he was descended from a noble Polish
family named Nietzky, and was proud of his
Sclav origin. One admirer has found points of
resemblance between his weird prose-poem,
Zarathustra*” and Chopin’s B-minor Scherzo.
He was certainly the least Germanic of all
German writers.. His style, in its brevity, verve ,
and directness is rather Gallic than German.
This protagonist of Christianity was, strangely
enough, descended from a long line of Christian
pastors who gave up their countrv rather than
abjure their faith. On both sides the stock of his
family was vigor us, both mentally and physi-
cally. His aunt had a fine saying, which showed
the moral robustness of the race, “We
Nietzsches hate lies.”
To Nietzsche, Greece and France were the
ancient and modern examples of intellectual
supremacy. Six French writers he named whom
he considered to be nearer to Greek antiquity than
any other group of modem authors, having minds
which contained more real thought than all the
books of all the German philosophers put together.
These six were Montaigne, La Rochefoucauld, La
Bruyhre, Fontenelle (“Dialogues of the Dead”),
Chamfort, and Vauvenarges. Stendhal was the
single French writer of the present century (with
the exception of Merimee) whom he considered to
possess some of the characteristics of the earlier
group. For ancient Greece the iconoclast had abso-
lute admiration, albeit he expelled some Hellenic
prophets from his temple. In 1871 he published
his first work, a treatise on the origin of music
and the drama, in which he embodied his famous
doctrine of the “ dithyrambic theory of life.”
Here he looked into primitive human nature as he
found it in early Greece, and discovered two
impulses or inspirations which led to art-produc-
tion : in other words, which led mortals out of
the routine task of satisfying merely their physical
needs, into the upper region of spiritual and
.aesthetic movement. The first inspiration is
through dreams ; for the dreamer is straightway
lifted above the beaten, sordid track of daily life.
This, in Nietzschean phrase, is the Apollonian
impulse. The other impulse comes from the
gladness that men feel when they are in touch
with the overflowing and intoxicating power of
Nature. This he called the Dionysiac impulse,
coining his word from Dionysos, the god who
presides oyer the springing corn, the opening bud,
the breaking blossom, and all the mysteries of
Nature. Along such lines as these Nietzsche
works out the doctrine cl. “ the will to live ” into a
doctrine of “ the will to enjoy life.” His starting-
point is essentially Hellenic:
Live in the Whole and the Full. Let us imagine a
generation growing up with this fearlessness of glance,
with this heroic bent towards the terrible, let us imagine
the bold step of these dragon-slayers, and the proud daring
with which they turn their backs upon all the old
optimism, and its doctrines bred of feebleness, in order to
live resolutely in the Whole and the Full.
I will learn always more and more to look upon the
necessary as the beautiful; so shall Ibe one of those who
make things beautiful.
Die proudly when it is no more possible to live proudly.
Nietzsche preached with Heine the Greek
doctrine of “ the rehabilitation of the flesh ” :
Christianity is mainly responsible for the deterioration
of t.he European rare.
To attack the passions at the root means to attack life
itself at the root.
Chastity and sensuality are not necessarily antithetical:
every true marriage is beyond such antithesis.
The spiritualisation of sensuousness is called love : it is
a grand triumph over Christianity.
The world has been Orientalised long enough, and men
now seek to be Ilellenised.
Most of what we know personally of the last of
German philosophers is gleaned from the books
published by his sister, in which his life is brought
down to 1889, the year in which the brain of
Neitzsche was paralysed at Turin. When her
husband, Dr. Forster, died, she devoted herself to
the care and the fame of “one of the greatest
spiritual forces which have appeared since
Gcethe.”
As a student, Nietzsche wrote during bis
gymnasium days :
The study of the Grecian and the Latin languages
should be put in the front rank, and thoir point of view
compared with that of the Germans. History should not
be separated from geography, nor mathematics from
physics and music. Only by this method will the tree erf
knowledge bear excellent fruit.
At the age of fourteen (in 1858) Nietzsche
was sent to the militarily conducted school of
Pforta. His progress was astoundingly rapid,
Earticularly in philology. Before he had taken
is doctor’s degree, and in his twenty-fifth year of
age, he was offered and accepted the philological
chair at Basle. About a year before lie took up
his professorship he met Wagner in Leipzig, and
absolutely succumbed to the great musician’s
magic influence. It is curious to reflect that to
IS letzsche, Schopenhauer and Tolstoi, three great
philosophical forces, Wagner was’ a factor in
development. In 1871 he published his first work,
wtt l - 6 der Tragodie,” etc., dedicated to
‘His Sublime Fore-runner, Wagner.” He after-
wards changed his views and spoke of Wagner as
the Cagliostro of Music, the Prince of Decadents,
and said that to listen to “His Sublime Fore-
runner” one needed Pastilles Gerauclels , followed
by twenty performances of “ Carmen” as an anti-
dote.
The boyish professor, the preacher in the
abstract of sensuousness, was very shy. Women
held no attraction for him. Even while showing
sympathy towards those with whom the course of
ran by no means smoothly, he would
shake his head perplexedly and murmur :
And all that over a little girl !
He makes Zarathustra say :
• I? better to fall into the hands of a murderer than
into the dreams of an ardent woman.
Between 1873-6 he wrote four essays : “David
Strauss, ’ “ The Use and Abuse of History in Re-
lation to Life,” “ Schopenhauer as an Educator,”
and ‘ Richard Wagner.” Bizet was not then en-
throned on Nietzsche’s music-stool, and “Car-
men ’ was not yet a cure for the Teuton cycle,
lhe spell of Wagner still worked, albeit he had
written that foremost among Wagner’s partizans
we T e audiences who were bored by the old masters,
and who found in the new master a stimulant for
their faded nerves.
One day Nietzsche almost unconsciously earned
home a volume by Schopenhauer. His sister said
of it that—
Schopenhauer was not for him a book but a friend.
Schopenhauer set the Nietzschean wheels re-
yolvmg. The evolution oi Attic tragedy, with
its Apollonian and Dionysiao impulses, was the
first-fruits. In time the younger man shook off
i'i , ln^uence °f the elder, and called Schopenhauer
‘ the old pessimistic false-coiner—merely an
heir to the Christian interpretation of life.” Like
Mephistopheles, Nietzsche considers “Morality
itself as a problem ” :
. The individual in his antecedents and his consequents
is a piece of fate : To say to him, “Alter thyself,” is to
require everything to alter itself.
Men must become both better and wickeder.
Only the simpleton says, “ Men ought to be like this or
like that”
Do what you will, but first be one of those who sn able
to will.
Love vour neighbor as yourself—but first be one of
those who are able to love themselves.
He called Paul the inventor of Christianity, and
described him as a neurotic, sensual, refined
“Jewish Pascal.” Historical Christianity was
purely Pauline:—
Paul the incarnated genius inspired Chandala-hatred
against Rome, against the world—the Jew, the eternal
Jew par excellence.
The Anarchist and the Christian both wish to revenge
themselves on Life. Their motto is, “J am worth
nothing.” The Decadent says, “ Life is worth nothing.”
In the whole New Testament there is only one figure
we can genuinely honour—that of Pilate.
With its contempt for the body Christianity was the
greatest misfortune that ever befel Humanity.
A being capable of purely altruistic actions alone is
more fabulous than the Phoenix. Never has a man done
anything solely for others, and without a personal
motive ; how could the Ego act without Ego ?
In order to subsist, the highest morality must posi-
tively enforce the existence of immorality.
Nietzsche compares the last cry of the Redeemer
with the disillusionment of the dying Don
Quixote; but for Him he had no sneer. He
called Him sublime, morbid, child-like. And
again:—
Strictly speaking, there has been only one Christian,
and He died on the cross. The Gospel died on the cross.
Opposition to Christianity and its theory of self-
denial and asceticism represents the final balance
of Nietzsche’s mind. His four cardinal virtues
were sincerity, courage, generosity and courtesy.
Humility and self-sacrifice he regarded as ab-
horrent. His aphorisms on life *
Profound suffering makes noble ; it separates.
For folly’s sake wisdom is ming ed with all things.
At bottom every man well knows he can only live one
single life in the world, and that never again will so
strange a chance shake together into unitv such singu-
larly varied elements as he holds : he knows that, but he
hides it like a bad conscience.
In 1880 Nietzsche’s health broke down, and ho
resigned his chair at Basle, and was liberally
pensioned. From 1877 to 1882 may be regarded
as the period of his maturity, when he produced
his best work. His third period began in 1882,
when he produced “Thus Spake Zarathustra.”
and other books. The work of his life ended in
1889, at Turin, when his brain became irretriev-
ably paralysed.
The great idea of the Zarathustra book was the
theory of master-morality versus slave-morality.
The motto of the volume was “ Break in pieces—
break in pieces the old tables.” Nietzsche stood
forth absolutely now as the apostle of aristocracy,
individualism, loneliness, and warfare. He was,
indeed, the modern incarnation of that image of
intellectual pride which Marlowe created in
Faustus:
Then are only three respects in which the masses
appear to me to deserve a glance—first, as blurred copies
of great men, executed on bad paper and from worn-out
plates ; secondly, as opposition to the great; and, lastly,
as instruments of the great; for the rest, let them go to
the devil and to statistics.
A people is the circuit which Nature makes to arrive at
six or seven great men.
Morality is the mob-instinct working in the individual.
To be customary is to be novel.
To be individual is to be wicked.
Every kind of originality involves a bad conscience.
The artist, the actor, the merchant, the freethinker, the
discoverer, were once all criminals.
The shadow that Fate had for Nietzsohe was
the shadow that fell on Scott and on Swift ; on
Rousseau and on Pascal, and Southey. The brain
before its fall had done a work of stupendous
nature. Havelock Ellis says of Nietzsche :
A man, who has certainly stood at the finest summit of
modern culture, who has thence made the most deter-
mined effort ever made to destroy modern morals, and
who led a life as near to death as any life outside the
grave can be, must needs be a tragic figure.
Nietzsche’s pioture has been drawn by M. Sohupfi
(during the Bayreuth festival) in the following
words:—
I was struck both by the superiority of bis intellect
and the strangeness of his face. A broad forehead, short
hair brushed back, the prominent cheek-bones of th*
Sclav. The heavy moustache and the bold outline of tba
face would have given him the aspect of a cavalry officer
if it had not been for his timid and haughty air. The
musical voice and slow speech indicated the artist’s
organisation, while the circumspect meditative carriage
was that of a philosopher. Nothing more deceptive than
the apparent calm of his expression. The fixed eye re-
vealed the painful travail of thought. It was at once the
eye of an acute observer and a fanatical visionary. • The
double character of this gaze produced a disquieted and
disquieting impression, all,the more so since it seemed to
be always fixed on a single point. In moments of effusion
this gaze was softened to a dream-like sweetness, but
soon became hostile again.

Paul Leicester Ford, author of “The Honorable
Peter Stirling” and other fine American political
novels, has been killed by his temporarily-mad
brother.

Melbourne Pastor Rentoul, at a Trades Hall
Pleasant Sunday Afternoon, the other day, re-
called to the workers’ memory a neglected genius,
William Langland, a great English democrat,
whose lengthy poem of “ Piers Plowman ” was a
protest against war and war taxation, under
Edward 111. and Richard 11.
Some correspondents:—
A.P.: He “F.M.’s” article on Blake and Whitman,
no genius is absolutely original, and it would be interest-
ing to know how much Blake learned from the Christian
mystics—Behmen, Paracelsus, Swedenborg. The latter
died in London in 1772, and it is probable that Blake saw
him. Blake’s love of animals and birds might have come
from Francis of Assisi. And was he not largely indebted to
the Elizabethan poets, notably to Spenser and the “ Epi-
thalamion”? Blake’s statement, “I assert for myself
that I do not behold the outward creation,” is
the Buddhist doctrine of the illusion of the objec-
tive universe. Yet, if we agree with Emerson’s
definition of originality—“lt is being, being one’s
self, and reporting accurately what we see, and are ”
then Blake was original. Emerson relates that Words-
worth said Blake’s abnormal genius interested him more
than the conversation of Scott or of Byron. Nietzsche,
in “ Also sprach Zarathustra,” evidently borrowed largely
from Blake, but he had not Blake’s sane physiological
basis, so landed himself in a lunatic-asylum. J. A.
Symonds’ book on Whitman is the most interesting
analysis of the “ good, grey poet.” It is, surely, a mis-
take to say that Whitman “fought" in the American
war. Edward Carpenter is a mild English Whitman.
But there is no adequate poet of the Anglo-Saxon race
to-day.
[Whitman went through the American Civil War, not as
a soldier, but as a nurse.]
Wilgar: You are unreasonable in condemning the
prize-poem. The bad poetry might scrape like a hatpin
on a slate, but if the £5O would fix up a bard’s finances,
and do only moderate injury to Art, wherefore the pro-
test of the aesthetic ? Sydney University can afford the
money, and, seeing that verse-writers are thick enough
locally to compete with zest and industry, surely they
deserve all the surplus encouragement possible ?
P. Pollard (New York) : Yes, it’s a great deal too close
to be pleasant, but Whitney at this end swears that he
never saw Burgess’ poem; and we’ve got to take hie
word.
C. Crisp : Publish next week.
Old l>r. Johnson was prophetically right in
locating his wise prince in Abyssinia. King
Memlik oi that country, having given orders to
some intruding German missionaries to move
away and not disturb the faith of the people, they
protested. Then Menelik asked them if they had
no sinners in Germany. They had to say “yes,”
and the Prince told them to return to Germany,
and give their countrymen the benefit of their
advice, which was not wanted in his country.
A “Push” Novel.
“ Great Push Experiment,” by Ambrose
I Pratt (from George Robertson and Co.),
JL is a book the reading of which must
suggest regrets to the critic, for it shows
unmistakably a talent for romance far above the
ordinary, and the, raw material, moreover, of a
bright and vivid style ; and yet, withal, contains
such mistakes and crudities as sadly mar it
as a work. The effect is similar to that
of a painter of distinguished natural talent
venturing on big pictures before he has
learned his technique thoroughly. Pratt might
take a place not at all humble among Aus-
tralian writers if he would learn to, or choose to,
cook his dishes before giving them to the public.
This particular novel has rather an interesting
history. Some months ago, in the sober
pages of Blackwood, Ambrose Pratt printed
as solemn truth a delightful fairy-tale about
Sydney pushes. N.S. Wales Agent-General
Henry Copeland, foolish man, stumbled into
print to deny the truth of the yarn, and Pratt’s
name was cabled for a whole week from continent
to continent Now that fairy-tale, with some
woman-interest added, and some more fictional
details, becomes an original and exciting novel—
“ The Great Push Experiment.” The main fault
is that the atmosphere of romance is at the outset
sought to be smothered by a preface solemnly
claiming truth for the main facts, and then is
rudely disturbed at intervals by the introduction
of real personages. It is as if Anthony Hope dis-
turbed the illusion of the “Prisoner of Zenda”
by dragging in now and again Lord Salisbury or
Mr. _ Chamberlain. A novel should never give
a hint to its readers to stop and ask, “la
this true ? ” Beside this mistake, others of fact, of
construction, of actual grammar, of too “luscious”
treatment of love passages are but trifling. It is,
after all, from the ordinary reader’s standpoint,
not very important that on page 1 a grand piano
is made to occupy the moiety of “a large and
stately, chamber, ’’ and that on many other pages
there is some extraordinary lapse of the sama
character; but that the fictional atmosphere
should be every now and again broken into, is a
more serious blemish. But, passing all that, the
book is a vivid and exciting one, original in its
main conception, carried out, in the Main, to a
natural and intensely exciting climax.

WARNER’S SAFE CURE.


Persistent Mr. Priestman still pegs away
with his demand for the release of poor old Cress-
well, the nnn who probably, if not certainly, isn’t
the real, original, long-lost, and no longer desir-
able Roger Tichborne. Priestman, the other day,
took a reporter along to interview his protege at
1 ark (S. W.) Asylum, but the expedition
vas a dismal failure. The lunatic revealed nothing,
jave a vague desire to hang one of the warders.
He didn t seem to know his faithful barracker,
Priestman, what time he was accepting oranges,
•tobacco, bananas, and cigars from. the visitor.
As for Cresswell being reasonably interviewed
any more on this earth, the idea seems absurd.
He refuses to talk, even with his brother patients.
H there was any glimmer of light in the man’s
mind before his real Sir Rogerness was forced
upon his notice, the strain of wondering what it
all means must have “ dowsed his glim ” for ever.
He now expresses _ some sense of mystery by
“holding up a warning hand,” as though he sus-
pected a plot to carry him off somewhere. Under
the circumstances, Cresswell’s release would pro-
bably be a cruel farce, but cruel farces are so
common in cases of lunacy that any stern medical
objection to bis capture is unaccountable. The
N.S.W. Govt, is not so devotedly attached to its
lunatics that it can’t bear to part with old Bill
Cresswell.
“ Malle’s ” theory :
It has been proposed to fix a maximum expenditure for
Parliamentary candidates, it being held that vital
questions are often drowned in a sea of beer. Under
present conditions the heaviest item of expense is litera-
ture, tons of this heavy substance being unnecessarily
dumped everywhere. The writer once witnessed a heated
discussion in China, between tars and Chows. The one
whose arguments carried most weight, the sort of senior
wrangler, was a brawny person with about 18in. of thin
bar iron in his fist. He appeared to convince his
opponents every time, and not one of them continued the
debate. Since then I’ve been impressed with the
necessity for condensing one’s blows. It’s useless trying
to belt at man’s whole anatomy with one whack, and
election literature, to be any good, should only contain a
few crisp sentences that will hang in the memory and,
like Twain’s “ pink trip slip for a three cent, fare,” won’t
be shaken off until the elector has either voted for, or
violently assaulted the author. One boiled-down whoop
of 10 words which conveyed the alleged essence of its
side’s policy and held the other crowd up to derision
would fill the bill and win every time.
The trouble is that sometimes the name of the
creek that you are promising to get the bridge
over runs to three words itself. That leaves you
four words in which to promise the bridge and
three in which to heap an avalanche of scorn on
the adversary.

while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page

Eight Years’ Dyspepsia.
A LONG PERIOD OF SUFFERING.
A WELCOME FRIEND IN TIME OF NEED.
The Case of tyrs. S. BRQ.VN.
(by our reporter.)
If you are imbued with any desire to gain
an insight into the methods adopted by
people whose efforts are attended with suc-
cess, you cannot do better than read the
following narrative, which was related by
Mrs. Selina Brown, of No. 5 Little Edward-
street, Pyrmont.
“ One day,” said Mrs. Brown, “ as I was
talking to a young girl at the front door, a
strange and overpowering sensation came
over me, which caused me to have a fit, and
I was taken indoors and put to bed, where
I was lying unconscious for three days. A
doctor was called in, but he said that he
could not account for the sudden turn I
had taken. He prescribed some medicine,
which did not have any beneficial effect,
as three weeks later I took another fit in my
sleep, after which I was so feeble that I had
to keep to my bed for several days, and
again the doctor was brought to my bed-
side.”
“ Previous to taking the fits were you in
good health ? ” asked the reporter.
“ I was not at all well, as you may gather
from the fact that for eight years I suff red
from indigestion,” answered Mrs. Brown.
“ For a long time I seemed as if I had no
use in my body to work at all. I felt
languid and depressed in spirits, and did
not care whether I lived or died; neither
coul i I deep well. It was a perfect misery
for me to go to bed, as my mind was so un-
settled that I felt as if I wanted to walk
about all night. Just as Ith ught I was
falling asleep I commenced to think about
some troublesome subject which generally
occupied my mind till morning, and when I
got up I was too exhausted to do anything,
and with a taste in my mouth that made
everything seem bitter. The only sleep I
got was always accompanied by dreadful
dreams, so that I never got any proper rest.
The sense of hunger w r as a stranger to me
entirely, but in spite of that I took a little
food occasionally, or else I should have
starved.”
“ Hid the food do you any good ? ”
“ I am sure it did not do me much good,
if any, for I wa< continually getting weaker
and thinner, and the worst of it was that
eating always gave me such awful pains in
the stomach, where windy spasms made me
feel as if I wanted to tear my clothes off, no
matter how loose they were. A heaviness
in my chest also troubled me greatly, for as
each breath was drawn a smothering sensa-
tion came over me, and at the same time I
suffered acutely from pains under my
shoulder blades and about my back. Every-
thing possible seemed to go against me. at
once, as I was often incapacitated from
going about the house owing to my feet
swelling and my limbs aching, across the
loins especially Unless I was very careful
and slow in my movements I became so
giddy that I could not stand, while extreme
nervousness was another torture, I was
afraid to move in the dark lest something
would happen to me, and if anything gave
me the least excitement my nerves shook to
such an extent that the vibration of my
limbs was quite ' isible. Little did I think
that something even worse than these ail-
ments was in store for me, but my experi-
ence goes to show how a person’s constitu-
tion is gradually wrecked, and w'hat fright-
ful results ar > likely to follow. After my
sudden seizures I was in a more hopeless
slate than ever- My head pains were so
bad that I could not bear anybody to speak
to me, and any food that I took w r as always
vomited. The medicines and powders which
were given to me were of no avail, and at
length I crawled out of bed and went to
another doctor, who told me I ought to go
to a hospital.* Having three young children
I did not like doing so, and instead of 'going
tc a h’lßpi'al I came home ”
“ Were you wise in going against the
doctor’s advice ? ”
“It turned out to be the best thing I
could have possibly done, as a neighbour
shortly afterwards came in to see me lor the
pu pose of reading a cure performed by
Clements Tonic. When she had finished
the story she exclaimed : ‘That is just how
you are. Why don’t you try it?’ I said,
‘I believe I will.’ When my husband
came home he thought I was going
to the hospital, but I said, ‘ I’m not
g ing. Bring me home a bottle of
Clements Tonic, and I will try it. ’ On
my word of honor, I felt a slight im-
provement when I had drank only half a
bottle. Just imagine my delight after the
way I had been disappointed with all other
medicines, especially as I had long since
thought that I was doomed to die, because
I could not get relief, but Clements Tonic
soon eased the terrific pains in my head
(which extended right down my neck), and
when, in a few days, they were gone alto-
gether I could get about a little, for I also
felt more vigorous than I had been for a long
time. The first good night’s rest I had had
for many a month was obtained through the
comforting influences of Clement Tonic, and
when this grand experience had been re-
peated several nights in succession I felt
quite new, for I was happy in the belief
that I m'ght yet recover m healtli. Xverv
tissue in my bod seemed to be strong! honed
bv the wonderful med cine I was taking
and for sharpening my appetite it was just
the t 1 ing. before long 1 sat down lo m
meals like everybody else in the house, and
I could always take a fair share of whatever
was placed on the table. The neighbours
were intensely surpr sed to see me up a id
about once more, as they had entertained
little hope of ever see ng me well aga n. I
had no trouble in keeping my food down,
and every day I found greater relief from
tie symptoms of indigest on. The pans
finally left my stomach and shoulders, an 1
I could breathe without gasping as I used
to. for the oppression had disappeared from
my chest, and w th returning strength my
limbs acquired freedom from all aches.
When I was taking my doctor’s medic nes I
had no desire for any thing, but Clements
Tonic gave me a light-hearted inclination to
see everything. By its cont'nued use I
forgot what nervousness was, as I knew no
fear, and my nerves were as steady as a
rock. I was never a stout woman, yet my
diseases had dragged me down considerably,
but Cements Tonic was my sole medicine
till my lost weight was regained, and, re-
markable as it may seem, that is all I
required, for I was then quite cured of
everything. None of my former ailments
were left behind by Clements Tonic, and I
am only speaking the truth when I say that
my life was saved by that medicine. If you
like you can publish my story in any way
you deem fit, as itmay alleviate the sufferings
of many more.”

Athlete’s Cough
“I must have contracted it while
Training for Athletic Meetings ”
Winter athletics cause many colds. In
the enthusiasms of football not much care
is taken of the health. Yet even while
training and competing it is easy to guard
against coughs and colds—here is the tip *
“ Mona House, Isleworth,
“ Middlesex, England,
“ February Bth, 1901.
“Dear Sirs,—For some time I have been
suffering from a severe cold and hacking
cough, which I think I must have contracted
while training to compete at athletic meet-
ings. One day a friend of mine said I ought
to try your Scott’s Emulsion as he knew it
PERCY GOURLAY.
(From a photograph.)
would do me good. I took his advice and
soon found I was deriving benefit from the
treatment. After taking Scott’s Emulsion
for three weeks I had entirely lost my cold
and no longer had any cough. I am now
feeling in splendid health, and giva Scott’s
Emulsion all the credit for same.
“ Yours truly,
“ (Signed) Percy Gourlay.”
Every athlete knows that training makes
extra demands upon his strength—competi-
tion still greater demands. Football,
running, “ cross country” are all hard work
and must be met by extra effort. Coughs
and colds are not permissible if the athlete
is to keep in the pink of condition. Scott’s
Emulsion maintains for the athlete a per-
fect condition of health.
Mr. Gourlay got himself and kept himself
in condition by using Scott’s Emulsion.
Athletes should try it—preventive treat-
ment is better for them than curative
treatment. Scott’s Emulsion supplies
exactly what they need—pure cod-liver oil
with hypophosphites of lime and soda. The
cod-liver oil is treated by our process so that
all the virtue of it remains, while all the
disagreeable taste and smell are taken away.
Cod-liver oil is the finest strengthener
ever known: hypophosphites of lime and
soda the best tissue, muscle and bone
builders. There can be no question of the
value of these things to the athlete.
He should, however, remember that the
high value of these ingredients is obtained
only in* Scott’s Emulsion—the scientific
blending of which is the result of thirty
years’ experience. Cheap imitations give
only cheap results. No substitutes possess
the unique value of cod-liver oil.
To get the athletic value that Mr. Gourlay
obtained, take Scott’s Emulsion the
wrapper of every bottle has a picture of a
man carrying a big codfish. *
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpenceto Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
483 Kent-street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All ohemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A strange story of how she got a lost feel-
ing in her legs was told recently in Sydney by a
lady who was found guilty of bigamy, but only
imprisoned till the rising of the Court. She
married very young and had ten children, but had
got out of the habit of seeing her husband. The
loss of this habit lasted for about ten years.
At the end of that period she chanced to
go once or twice into the office of an
agent to see about a lease, and he asked
her to marry him. She explained about the
previous husband, who might still possibly be
alive, whereupon the agent gave her a glass of
wine, and went away to get legal advice on the
matter, leaving her locked in the office. While he
was away a lost feeling in her knees came over
her. Whether the wine or something in the wine
was the cause or not can’t be ascertained now,
but, anyhow; she was deprived of all force of
character in her legs, and when the agent came
back and said it was all right, she was led away
uncomplainingly in a cab and married. Imme-
diately after the ceremony her new husband bor-
rowed" £1 off her. Later on he borrowed £l5, and
also suggested that her property should be made
over to him. He brought Ins four children to live
with her, and afterwards he got into gaol. Then the
first husband transpired again, and there was
trouble, but the law mercifully took account of the
fact that the act of bigamy was committed in a
weak moment when the lady had no strength in
her knees, and gave her “ the rising of the Court,”
as already mentioned.
Australian Autumn in Drought Time.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Desolate, bleak and depressing, O Autumn, is thy
gradual waning,
But blue are thy skies with a keenness no glittering
steel could surpass;
Oh ! for the murmur of water, in lieu of thine
arid disdaining,
That this dusty, bleached, thirst-cracking land-
scape might be swathed in the greening of
grass.
When matched with this pitiless picture of lank
sheep and skeleton cattle,
Gasping with gouged bleeding sockets amid beak-
plunging, ravenous crows,
The deep-billow-roaring of cannon and the scenic
distractions of battle
Are something devout and inspiring, especially to
one that has foes.
Rivers are shrinking to frog-holes and choking
with carrion, green-livid;
Deserts like granite invade the once meadow-
smooth stretches of plain;
And yet we cannot but marvel at colors so various
and vivid—
But oh ! for its long swinging columns fraying
off into sombres of rain !
N.S.W.
ARTHUR A.D. BAYLDON.
Where Dreadful Drought is King!
In helpless woe the land is gaping wide,
Its river courses glisten lone and sere,
White bones betoken where its flocks have died,
Its wild mirages mock you far and near ;
The sky’s a brazen vault, so deadly clear;
The breezes sweep you by and scorch and sting :
It gleams and glares, a place of torrid fear :
This is the Land where Dreadful Drought is King !
.Across its baking plains ye may not ride,
For deadly Thirst and Desolation sheer
Encompass, as ye go, on every side,
And gibb’ring phantoms in the hazes leer;
At night the twinkling starlights peep and peer
(Just as they peer where laughing phantoms sing)
And tell a tale of dew-wet meadows dear,
Afar from lands where Dreadful Drought is King 1
For He hath won the Desert as a bride,
To Him alone she lendeth eye and ear,
For Him her shrunken veins are slowly dried,
To Him alone she bares her bosom drear;
Her ruthless Lord denies a trembling tear
To cool the parched bird on drooping wing,
Her sons knownaught of runnel sweetand mere—
This is the Land where Dreadful Drought is King !
In vain—in vain ! No gentle voice of eheer !
No clouds above where hope may climb and cling—
The breath of Death in all the atmosphere;—
This is the Land where Dreadful Drought is King I
Q.
P.L.
The Bush.
Oh, lonely sadness of the drear grey bush 1
So still, so void of every living thing !
Oh, sleeping tent-hills ! deathlike is the hush —
No voice to ripple and no bird to sing.
Dawn’s rosy red, nor evening’s gentle close,
Can tinge thy solemn wastes, thy soulless calm;
Thy irresponsive dumbness, dead repose,
May kindle to no note of Life’s glad psalm.
The winds but voice the mulga’s nightwatch dirge,
Gay noon but lights thy long unending sleep
—From Time’s first beat till endless hours shall
merge
To the last echoing deep’s reply to deep.
Pitying stars shine o’er thy ghost-grey plains !
The dingo wails his dismal weird lament—
The sense of tragic melancholy strains
Ere yet the eerie hopeless glooms are spent.
Oh, sombre Bush ! Upon my heart there groweth
Changeless and voiceless thy eternal moan :
Profound mysterious life of death in death !
Silent and endless, tortured and alone !
And yet I love thee-—infinitely vast!
My spirit revels in thy freedom wild,
The empty world drops from me, till at last,
I may be for a season Nature’s Child.
S.A.
J.O.G.

SATURDAY. JULY 5, 1902.
The Exodus.
Emigration from Australia is becoming an
established institution nowadays. Both in
New South Wales and Victoria the applica-
tions for permits to go to South Africa are
pouring in by hundreds, and as only persons
with at least tjlOO each in cash, over and
above their passage-money, are eligible, and
the emigrants mostly possess just about that
sum, this is mainly an exodus of the better-
off among the working classes, and of that
section of the people which used to go on
the land, in the days when the land hadn't
been so completely found out as it is now.
It is a curious thing that people should be
flying, from sheer want of work, from a
country which has a population of only Jive
persons to every four square miles ! —a
country, too, whose “ magnificent natural
resources ” form the wheeze and platitude of
every dull politician ; and that even South
Africa, ruined by war and swarming with
cheap nigger labour, should occur to them
as a place that it is good to go to if
only they can get away from the land
of M‘Milt,an, Bruce Smith and co. It
suggests that Australia has somehow “missed
the ’bus,” and that it has lived in vain
and fooled away a great opportunity.. In
N.S. Wales alone persons with money were
recently said to be applying at the rate of
60 a week for permission to go to the burnt
lands of South Africa, and N.S. Wales is
better off just now than some of its neighbors
—mostly because it is floating larger loans.
Such a state of things suggests that Australia
has “ missed the ’bus ” in an aggravated
form.
# • • •
In N.S. Wales Assembly last week the
Owb’ Sullivan arose as usual, and explained
what measures were going to be taken out of
loan money to “afford relief to the unem-
ployed ’’ this winter. Works Ministers
in Australia rise about four times a year
on an average to explain what measures are
to be taken out of loan money to afford
relief to the unemployed, who are in a parlous
state owing to the approach of a cold winter,
or a dry spring, or a hot summer, or a late
autumn, as the case may be. In every case
the relief takes the form of public works—
and even the kind of public works that are
supposed to yield direct revenue are only
valuable when there are people to use them,
and unless there are more genuine industries
to support these people it is hopeless to sup-
pose that these people will be founci here
permanently to any great extent. There is
a constant making of roads which lead, not to
big factories and other permanent indus-
tries, but to some place where the bones of
dead animals lie on a plain. There is a
perpetual making of bridges to connect some
dying western town with some other place
that is practically dead, or to join a wilder-
ness and a waste together, and of railways to
“ afford access ” to places which no sane
person wants access to, until there is some-
thing better to do there than wrest five-
bushel crops from a barren soil, and then
pay 300 miles of land carriage and 16,000
miles of sea carriage to convey them to a
glutted foreign market. There is no serious
effort to provide any industry worth going
to at the end of these roads and railways,
and though roads are good when they lead to
any visible means of support, and bridges
are valuable when they enable a man
to get across to a permanent job on the
other side, yet merely as places for de-
mented and unemployed swagmen to walk on,
with corks hanging from their hats and no
definite prospect of getting anywhere in
particular, they are not worth having. Aus-
tralia doesn’t require new roads just at
present nearly so much as it requires to build
industries along the roads italready possesses,
so that a self-supporting population may use
them in getting to its remunerative employ-
ment in the morning and oack at night.
One of the most depressing facts imaginable
is to walk or ride 40 miles along a well-made
highway, and meet one unemployed swag-
man, and a dray bringing up imported goods
to the store m some far township, and two
men mending the road out of loan funds,
and ten men building a bridge out of loan
funds, and the mail-coach, supported by a
Government subsidy, carrying a solitary
passenger, who is a Government inspector of
something or other.
v * *
Australia imports more foreign goods, in
proportion to population, for its own use—
not for transhipment to other States—
than any other country on earth. Its
imports are principally manufactured goods.
Therefore it has more opportunities
than other countries to find permanent
and profitable employment for its
people—the people who are now leaving it
in disgust and making for Africa—by
stopping some of these imports and manu-
facturing for itself. Unfortunately, Aus-
tralia has hitherto been too much addicted
to Revenue Tariffism, or to a weak and in-
efficient form of Protection, for its manufac-
turing industries to get a fair hold. In many
lines this country has to compete with vastly
more expert foreigners, who, in lands where
food and rent are cheap, can work and be
comparatively affluent for 3s. 6d. a day. The
Australian would become equally expert in
time, but he can’t expect to be so at the
outset. And the Australian manufacturer is
expected to pay ss. to 7s. or more per day—
if he can’t there is generally some O’Sullivan
about, who is willing to pay that much out
of loan funds for cutting scrub at some place
where the scrub will grow again next year,
or digging a hole that will silt up again next
month. The Foreign-trade party, and a large
section of the Labor party, profess to believe
that it is utterly wrong to subsidise, by
bonuses, or by Protective duties which will
bring in a certain amount of revenue, some
permanent producing industry, which may
save the country £1,000,000 a year in imports
and enable that amount to be distributed in
wages. It never sees anything wrong in
subsidising, on a far larger scale, out of loan
funds, some temporary scrub-cutting or
comparatively useless road-making job which
brings in no revenue, but costs a large
amount in interest, and possibly a good deal
in maintenance. And if the non-Pro-
tected or insufficiently Protected manu-
facturer can’t compete, in point of wages,
with the enormously-subsidised scrub-cutting
job—the job on which every penny of the
wages is a bonus—he has to go.
* * * ♦
N.S.W. professed, for many years, to be
run on Freetrade principles, and though it
protected some industries very heavily, and
thereby kept its Freetrade theory out of the
Benevolent Asylum under false pretences, it
did practise Freetrade as regards many in-
dustries. With regard to these industries,
the would-be manufacturer was simply told :
“In many foreign lands, where rents
and food are cheap, your industries are
run by expert artisans at 3s. 6d. a day.
Setting the Protection you gain by sea-
freights to Australia against the fact that you
must begin work with less experienced
artisans, you will probably be able to do the
same work if you can find able-bodied men at
3s. 6d. a day.” And if the Freetrade Govern-
ment had insisted on having all its public
works performed similarly at European rates
the position would have been logical. Then,'
either there would never have been any
public works or any manufactures run on
Freetrade principles, or else the rate of
wages would have fallen to 3s. 6d. a day all
round, and there would have been a corres-
ponding fall in rents and other things, and
everybody would have had a fair deal. But,
while it recognised the necessity of paying a
higher wage than the European rate on
public works, it didn’t recognise, except in a
few instances, the necessity for paying a
higher wage in manufacturing enterprises.
It laid down the law that this country
mustn’t 'make calico unless it could do
it at European cost, while the equally
logical law that it shouldn’t make roads
and railways unless at European wages,
was ignored. It refused to make £lOOO
worth of spades and picks unless they could
be made at German cost, but it didn’t mind
buying them and then paying £2OOO in wages
(out of loan moneys) in uselessly shifting
sand with them, in order to find work for
men who might have been better employed
making them.. It held that it was wrong to
make rails if they could be made more
cheaply in Europe, and it had no scruples
about paying men (out of loans) nearly twice
the European rate to turn these rails into a
railway which wouldn’t pay axle-grease. It
wouldn t pay, under a Protective tariff, the
amount extra for paint which would repre-
sent the difference between Australian wages
(artificially fixed, to some extent at least, by
the rate paid out of loan money on public
works) and (say) Italian wages, but it had no
scruple about paying the men whom this
policy left unemployed, a high wage for
daubing that paint on the railings of Hyde
Park, Sydney.
* * » •
Even in the protected States the Protec-
tion was, in many cases, wholly inadequate.
All over Australia the manufacturer, in many
lines, was put in a position to pay European
rates, or perhaps as much as 50 per cent,
over European rates, while the State paid in
many cases twice these rates to its great
mass of public employees. In many cases
the manufacturer was left to compete, with
no Protection at all, against skilled European
artisans in countries where a man can be
reasonably well off at 3s. 6d. a day, while
the Government paid ss. a day for what was
practically pauper relief. Asa result, manu-
factures never flourished as they should have
done. N.S.W., the worst sample of the
Freetrade policy, produces less value per
head of manufactured goods than even poor
bankrupt Spain ; less than Austria, Scandi-
navia, or Holland ; not half as much as
Switzerland, Canada, France, or Germany ;
about a-quarter as much per head as the
United States. And by reason of this, some
O’Sullivan or other has been rising for
many years with new loan schemes for the
relief of the unemployed. If there had
never been any O’Sullivan, either the
public would have demanded Protection, or
else there would have been a general fall in
wages, with a corresponding fall in rents,
and manufactures might have made some
headway. But the Man with the Owe was
always in the road.
♦ ♦ *
The exodus to South Africa and the new
rising of O’Sullivan with a scheme for the
relief of the unemployed (O’Sullivan, having
no control of the country’s fiscal policy, is,
of course, not now wholly responsible for the
situation) are two results of this system. The
people who have higher ambitions than to
toil at agriculture in a drear land, where
agriculture is worth about 15s. a week, and
who don’t want pauper relief out of the
recent £7,000,000 loans, are going away.
The Labor members in the Federal Parlia-
ment have just refused to vote some
£300,000 or £400,000 to start works which,
it was expected, would give permanent
employment at good wages to 3000 men
and probably prevent £1,000,000 a year
going out of N.S.W. alone to pay for
imports. The Labor members in N.S.W.
State Parliament are understood to cordially
approve of schemes to spend more than that
in relief jobs which will involve a large
annual outgoing in interest, and merely
afford temporary aid to about the same num-
ber of men. Sanuford, of Lithgow, accord-
ing to appearances at time of writing, must
either find men who will work at such a rate
as will enable him to compete with English
and other outside rivals, or else abandon his
proposed new industry. But the rule doesn't
apply all round ; the unemployed who are
to be assisted by the new loan scheme won’t
be expected, by any means, to compete
against Austrian or Italian pick-and-sliovel
rates. And by reason of this refusal to put
the permanent industry on anything like the
same wage level as the relief job, the rising
of O’Sullivan and his kind to propound
loan schemes in aid of the unemployed has
become a permanent Australian rising, and
if M'Millan and co. have their way much
longer with the Federal tariff, it is likely to
continue so.
N.S.W. Arbitration Act.
It is natural that there should be a feeling of
uneasiness, even of grave discontent, among the
trades-unions at the first important decision of
the N.S W. Arbitration Court, which is adverse to
their claims. Compulsory Arbitration has been
always put forward as a “labor measure,” as a
means by which the worker could more readily
secure that fairer distribution of the fruits of
labor which the justice of modern sociology sug-
gests. It is, therefore, disquieting to the workers
to find that compulsory arbitration can cut both
ways, and penalise trades-unions acting unfairly,
as well as capitalists acting unfairly. The volun-
tary Conciliation and Arbitration Act in N.S.W.,
it will be remembered, began its operations sorne-
what similarly. A Southern coal-mining case
having been referred to it for decision, the verdict
was, in the main, unfavorable to the trades-union
and excited serious discontent, though its justice
was not ser’ously questioned. The voluntary act,
partly on account of that decision, was practically
a dead letter afterwards. The present Act cannot
become a dead letter, since any person, employer
or employee, may bring his grievance to its
arbitrament, whether the other party to the cause
is willing or not. Trades-unions must, therefore,
whether they care to or not, make the best of it.
That should not be a-difficult task.
♦ * • *
What has to be remembered is that the Arbi-
tration Court is bound by its oath to administer
justice to both sides in an industrial case--not the
theoretic-justice represented by “ Socialism in our •
time,” or communism, but what is practical fair
play under existing conditions of society. It is
empowered—indeed, ordered—to give preference
to members of trades-unions as against non-
unionists, and, consequently, as a matter
of fair play, it must take care that
every working-man is given a reasonable
chance of becoming a trades-unionist. It would
be intolerable tyranny otherwise. So far,
the decisions of the Court have held an even
balance. In the Newcastle case the employers
were ordered to employ: union labor, and the
decision generally was in favor of the workers.
In the Australian Workers’ -Union case, the
application of the Union to cancel the registration
of a rival, seemingly promoted by thB pastoralist
employers for the purpose of combatting the
A.W.U., was refused, with a strong inferential
suggestion that the A.W.U. might be deprived of
its status as a registered union if formal applica-
tion were made. This decision was all in favor of
the employers, and was arrived at by a majority
verdict —Mr. Smith, the workers’ representative
on the Bench, dissenting.
* * *
But it is impossible to allege that the decision was
anything but just. The A. W.U., through its own
mistake, was debarred from bringing forward any
material evidence to support its allegation that
the Machine Shearers’ Union was a “ bogus ”
organisation. This was on a “ technicality,” but
a very necessary technicality. No court of jus-
tice could carry on its business unless there was a
preliminary process of defining the issues for trial
beforehand, and unless it subsequently confined the
proceedings strictly within those issues. Further,
the Court proceedings showed that in the A.W.U.
code there were rules which it was impossible for
any court of justice in a free country to compel the
workers in the pastoral industry to subscribe to.
These rules were designed with the object of com-
pelling every A.W.U. member to vote ‘‘the
Labor ticket ” at all elections, and provided
stringent penalties for any A.W.U. member
who spoke, worked, or voted against a Labor
candidate. It would be utterly undemocratic
for the State to attempt to compel a man
into certain political convictions —however ad-
mirable in theory—on penalty of losing or
seriously prejudicing his chances of employ-
ment. The A.W.U. should recognise this, and
promptly amend its rules. There will be no
reason then why members, if they choose to do so
should not form a parallel political organisation
on the lines of a Political Labor League.
* * * *
The Arbitration Court, it will be noted, took
the broad view that a trades-union may spend its
funds on printing a newspaper for propaganda
purposes : and that shows a liberal idea of the
rights and functions of trades-unionism. But it
is obvious that trades-unions must recognise that
the system of compulsory arbitration has its
penalties as well as its benefits. The price to be
paid for a legal enforcement of trades-unionism,
and for the establishment of a Court of Justice to
regulate hours of labor and wages, is that the union
in each trade and calling must be open to every
decent, skilful workman, and must impose no
political, religious, or other unfair restriction on
its members. Possibly other organisations besides
the A.W.U. will find it necessary to make altera-
tions in their rules. But the benefit to be gained
—the almost absolute insurance against strikes
and lock-outs —will be worth the sacrifice.
■-
A Lost Joss.
The debate on the Federal Bonus Bill aroused
William M‘Millan to state firmly (according to
the report in Sydney Telegraph) that he would
not be a party to the “ creation of a new industry
by means of Protection and a bonus, for which, so
far, the people had given no mandate.” This
arouses curious recollections of the time when
this same unflinching M‘Millan, and the
equally unflinching Bruce Smith, were suc-
cessively Treasurers in a N.S.W. Foreign-
trade Ministry, which held office on a man-
date from the people to rule the State on
Foreign-trade principles. And that Ministry
heavily protected, among other things, sugar,
tobacco, cigars, wine, beer, apples, bacon, bis ;i
cuits, butter, candles, cement, cheese, confec ;
tionery, cornflour, galvanised iron, iron wire, wire
■ netting, lead pipes, hams, tongues, jams, jellies,
preserved milk, molasses, treacle, nails, 15 different
kinds of oil, paint, salt, shot, timber of almost
every kind, and newspapers. It sacrificed a vast
amountof moneyin protecting many of these things
—if it had levied an excise on local beer, sugar
tobacco, cigars, and wine equal to the "duty
charged on the imported goods, the Treasury
would have been much better off and its sacred
principles would have been saved. While it could
hold on to its sordid portfolios and its unclean
salaries (for money obtained by preaching one
thing and practising another is unclean) the
“ mandate of the people,” about which M ‘Millan
is now so agitated in his soul, didn’t matter two-
pence. And the same soiled proceeding can be
charged, in exactly the same degree, against
Gould, and Pumpkin Smith, and in a less degree
against Reid and Joseph Cook. Also the rank
and file which supported the old Parkes and Reid
Ministries, and a good part of which is now in the
Federal Parliament preaching the creed it didn’t
follow, is included in the same condemnation.
• * * •
These men, but principally M ‘Millan, Bruce
Smith, Gould, and the Pumpkin Smith person,
shamefully disregarded the distinct mandate oi
the people—which was bad ; and in the light of
that proceeding their present high moral attitude
of snuffle is outrageous hypocrisy and, politically
speaking, worthy of the late T. Oates. And they
did worse than disregard the popular mandate.
They created, or maintained, a considerable num-
ber of protected industries in New South Wales,
and they systematically pointed to these in-
dustries as a proof of the prosperity that
came of Freetrade. Their Freetrade was a lie and
a degrading imposture all through, and there
was neither truth, honor, nor public decency
in it. To practise Protection in a very large
degree while they declared that Protection was
injurious, was either gross treachery or something
equally bad. It was also a selling of their own
brass deity, and the work of a gang of political


Iscariots. To maintain industries by Protection,
and then, directly or indirectly, to ascribe the
prosperity of Protected industries to the Freetrade
system was political false pretences. Likewise, it
was bearing false witness against their neighbor,
and stealing the credit which belonged to a system
they reviled. It was preaching teetotalism with
a red nose and a shaky hand and a strong whisky
breath. It was a low-down process of living, and
having their job and their salary, by means of a
thing they said they abhorred. M ‘MiI LAN, at all
events, had his reward. The Government he
helped to disfigure didn’t levy a protective tax on
the goods he imported, and thereby create a
local industry that might haye damaged his
business and supplied the public with employ-
ment. By protecting, without a mandate from
the people, many other things, his party
was able, for very many years, to keep in
office, and keep out of office the real Protectionist
party which would have taxed M ‘Millan’s goods.
If the various apostate Governments with which
he was connected, or which he supported, had
practised real Freetrade the chances are that the
feneral ruin which would haye followed would
ave brought in a real Protectionist Government
—a calico-taxing Government —in very short order.
* * * *
Every time any member of the old Parkes and
Reid Governments addresses the Federal Parlia-
ment on the advantages of Freetrade he should be
asked to explain, first of all, why he never dared to
practise it even when lie had a mandate from the
people to do so, and pretended to be doing so,
and was in office for the express purpose of doing
so. That question wants answering before all
others. A creed which is so dirty that its own
advocates dare not touch it even now except by
instalments, is no creed for Australia. Not one of
the Freetrade leaders has dared to suggest putting
the excise on beer, for instance, on a level with
the import duty, and letting the blessings of com-
mercial freedom and increased public revenue
blow on the muddy beverage ; and that is only
one case out of many. The alleged prosperity of
N.S.W. under Freetrade is a lie, for N.S.W. never
had Freetrade. In N.S.W. that doctrine has
always been a fraud, a job, and an imposture, and
the alleged Freetrade Ministers of N.S.W. have
always been either impostors holding office under
false pretence, or traitors deliberately doing what
they believed to be injury to their own State and
their own people—being either hired to do so by
foreign enemies who are still at large, or doing it
out of their own natural wickedness. They
always denied their own brass deity in prac-
tice, and they preached him and rejected
him at the same time. Their callous, brutal
system wouldn’t have lasted a year if it had been
really put into force ; the best proof of that fact
is that M'Millan and his party knew a great deal
better than to ever really put it into force. If they
were offered Ministerial office now on condition
that they protected no industry whatever—that
every duty levied for revenue purposes on im-
ported goods should be accompanied by an equiva-
lent excise duty on the local product, and that
there should be no surreptitious Protection by
the faking of railway rates or otherwise, it
is quite safe to wager, on their past re-
cords, that they wouldn’t take office, badly as
they want it. The Freetrade creed only lives in
Australia by never being put to the test. Its
apostles only kept on top by telling a gullible
people persistently for many years that they
were practising it, while to a great extent
they practised the other thing. It has been the
longest-lived hoax, and the most impudent fraud,
and the cheekiest three-card, trick in Australia’s
history, and it is time Australia realised the fact.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Dead Premier’s Send-Off.
Just before poor George Leake died and left the
Westralian Premiership to Ktngsmill (who hasn’t
been able to hold it, and is turning the task over
to that amiable lawyer, James), Perth Morning
Herald again spoke to him very severely about
the wickedness of having a surplus and proposing
to use it for railway construction, instead of
floating the usual loan. It said :
There is one aspect of the case against the sliding scale
that does not seem to be fully understood by the general
public, and that is its absolutely unconstitutional charac-
ter. Mr. Nanson, who is one of the most vigorous advo-
cates of the sliding scale, in his speech at Queen’s Hall,
took exception to the proposals of the Government in
reference to the expenditure of the surplus revenue. He
touched on the question very lightly, merely indicating
that the works proposed to be carrried out might more
properly be undertaken out of loan money. Mr. Nanson,
however, was unable to suggest a means of spending an
annual surplus of £300,000 without either wasting it
or else devoting it to national undertakings fair It/ charge-
able to loans. We have supported the proposals of the
Government in regard to the surplus revenue simply on
the ground that they offer almost the only alternative
to the frittering away of the money, but, at the
time, we are bound to endorse to some extent the
objections raised by Mr. Nanson. The bare fact of the
matter is that there is no proper and constitutional
method of spending a surplus of £300,000 per annum, for
the simple reason that there is no constitutional method
by which such a surplus can be obtained. May, in his
Parliamentary practice, puts the position very clearly
when he says:
“ The Commons do not vote money unless it be required
by the Crown, nor do they impose or augment taxes
unless such taxes be necessary for the public service, as
declared by the Crown through its constitutional
advisers.”
The practice of raising unnecessary revenue by taxation
is quite unconstitutional, and that the revenue from the
sliding scale is unnecessary is demonstrated by the
existence of the surplus. No supporter of constitutional
government can record his vote in favor of the Leake
Administration.
There is a magnificent ignorance, or else a
fine solid capacity for misrepresentation in
quoting May, the great English Parliamentary
authority,as a proof that it is “unconstitutional”
to build public works out of revenue. In Great
Britain all the public works done by the national
Government are paid out of revenue (the item
runs to £8,000,000 or £10,000,000 in a year, in-
cluding barracks, &c.), likewise all new telegraphs,
post-offices, &c. ; and in addition, in ordinary
years, about £6,000,000 is applied in reduction of
the public debt. The English Parliamentary
authority is solid enough on the fact that, after
all public works have been paid for out of taxa-
tion, no needless taxes should be levied, but he
doesn’t consider the £6.000,000 a year raised in
repayment of the public debt needless taxation by
any means. Perth Morning Herald’s demand
that the dead Premier should be thrown out
because he proposed, in a State which has already
the appalling debt of £67 per head, to build two
small railways out of revenue ; and its declaration
that it is “unconstitutional” not to run into
debt; and that the sin of trying to keep down
the public liabilities in the boom days, so that the
burden may be less disastrous in the burst days
that must follow just as surely as night follows
day, called for his ejection from office—all these
things are a sorry example of the mental dry-rot
which has come upon Australia. At the time of
his death George Leake was the only Australian
Premier who hadn’t a big shortage to' add to the
public liabilities. Probably he didn’t fully realise
the sin of not having a shortage till his miserably
stupid newspaper adviser drew his attention to it.

The Transcontinental Land-drab.
The party which seeks to negotiate with a syndi-
cate to complete the transcontinental line from
Port Darwin to Adelaide on the land swindle
principle is confident of success. Premier Jen-
kins Iscariot has announced that he is with it,
just as Jenkins Iscariot was with the Adelaide
tram syndicate job; and the Tory party in both
Houses is with it. Also, a great many other
people who would, under different circumstances,
be against it, favor the idea as a way of defeating
what they regard as a mean conspiracy on the
part of Queensland and N.S.W. to secure the
Territory’s trade. These latter people have cer-
tainly some show of a reasonable grievance.
Adelaide is nearer to Port Darwin than is either
Brisbane, Sydney, Melbourne, or Perth. The
S.A. people have constructed a great part of the
railway already, and would have constructed it
all had money been available. They have ad-
ministered the Territory, and carried the burden
of its ill-luck and its deficit. Now a small party
of shameless grabsters has arisen with a proposal
that,. if the Federal Government takes over the
Territory, instead of carrying the Territory rail-
way straight south to connect with the Adelaide
line at Oodnadatta, it should be deflected away to
the east to bring the business over a much longer
route to Sydney, via a proposed Federal capital
at Canoblas, all the trade of which would also go
to Sydney. The scheme includes an arrangement
for buying the support of Queensland by connect-
ing the new line with the Brisbane and Rockhamp-
ton railways in passing. This, of course, sacrifices
the idea of a transcontinental railway altogether ;
the proposed line would be merely a line going
diagonally across a comer of Australia. It would
be longer, and it would make a great part of the
688 miles of transcontinental line already con-
structed from Adelaide to Oodnadatta a lost
enterprise, or nearly so. But it would bring the
trade to Sydney, and, in the view of a certain
small but loud and pernicious gang, that is the
beginning, middle, and end of all things
in this country. A great quantity of
literature has been circulated, with maps
showing the Federal capital at Sydney’s
back door, and the great Diagonal Railway which
is to bring the products of the Northern Territory
through that capital to Circular Quay. Ade-
laide has taken this unprincipled and enormously
insolent grab-scheme more seriously than it
deserves. It hasn’t the money to go on with the
transcontinental at once. It has a fear, carefully
fostered by the Tory party and sedulously culti-
vated by large land-grabbers, that if it hands over
the Territory to the Federal authority without
first committing that body in some way to complete
the existing line, the party which wants to bring
everything to Sydney, regardless of cost, will get
the existing Port Darwin-Pine Creek railway de-
flected into N.S.W., and Adelaide will be left. A
considerable number of the people are almost
ready to job away the whole Territory as a land-
grant to any syndicate that will build the line, and
thus make the defeat of the Sydney grab idea cer-
tain. S.A. is willing to give the Territory to the
Commonweath —that is to say, it has offered it to
the Federal Parliament, and got no answer. It
reckons that it will cost it no more to give the great
province to some private jobbers, and, if it there-
by secures the Territory trade, it may even
cost it a little less. Under these conditions
it is more than ever desirable that the Federal
Parliament should take over the Territory at an
early date, and put its foot on the nefarious land-
grant scheme. It is equally desirable that in
taking over the Territory it should make it clear
that the insolent grab scheme of the Canoblas
Federal Capital push, for bringing the Territory’s
trade by a sidelong railway to Port Jackson, and
dragging it up and down the Blue Mountains on
the way, has no Federal sympathy whatever. The
trade, by the way, would probably, in any case,
do Sydney much less good than is supposed.
Half-a-dozen new boot-factories, to make the
boots that are now imported, might be of much
more permanent value. Certainly, the trade
would do Sydney far less good than it would
Adelaide; every extra mile of distance diminishes
the value of commerce.

. An Order of Merit 1
There is bitter sarcasm on the whole “title”
business in the establishment of an Order of
Merit by King Edward, with a first list of
recipients who decidedly merit honor, if honor
can be conferred by a mere enthroned man. The
theory used to be that all the kingly Orders
were for “merit”—the Garter, the Bath, the
Thistle, the Cross of Michael and George
and all the other knighthoods, some with
their suggestions of immorality, others of the
servile abasement of the Middle Ages. That
theory can now hardly be longer tenable when
there is one “order” for people of merit, as dis-
tinguished from the people who crowd into the
orders which commemorate -King’s courtesans and
other incidents of Royal life. There is now more
hope for a general recognition of the truth that
titles are only the business devices intended to
prop up the anachronistic institution of a monarchy
by recruiting champions for it among the most sue
cessful of the bourgeoisie. A great seller of beer,
a pirate of the tea-trade, a notable captain of
industry, a successfully dishonest politician, pro-
gresses from a K.C.M.G. to a baronetcy or a peer-
age, because the monarchy sees the need of
attaching to itself by bonds of self-interest those
who have shown great capacity for exploiting their
fellow-men. Merit —not even the merit of the
beer, the tea, or the politics dealt in—has no rela-
tion at all to the title. In fact, within certain limits
bad beer, bad tea, and mischievous politics are-
more sure steps to the peerage than good articles
of those descriptions, for by dealing in inferior
articles successfully there is more money to be
made in a certain time, more sure capacity shown
for exploiting the people. The system of royal
honors has succeeded so well in corrupting poli-
tics, in attaching to the monarchical interest most
of the energy of. the bourgeois party, that it is
matter for surprise that King Edward, by his
Order of Merit, should throw upon it a grave
reflection. A monarchy would be saved from such
mistakes if it had a sense of humor.

The Question of Water Supply.
The “most important question in Australia.’
dragged into N.S.W. Assembly by a motion for’
adjournment, produced an inconsequential debate.
Ministers talked vaguely about light railways,
water conservation, and artesian wells. Ex-
Ministers pleaded guilty to past neglect, and mem-
bers generally did little more than air vapid
imaginings. The proposal was that £200,000 a
year should be expended on water conservation.
Where the money was to come from, or how it
should be expended, did not seem to concern any-
one. The mischief is that it has not concerned
the present or any other Govt, since the days when
a beginning was made in selling the land and
squandering the proceeds. There is no inten-
tion of providing the money save by borrowing,
and there are no plans ready for even such
works as would serve the country with the
first good rain. Yet much might be done.
Dams might be constructed across the supply
channels of the great backwaters, with sluice-
gates to let the flood in, and hold the water
when the rivers begin to fall. Also £100,009
might be profitably expended in sinking artesian
wells within a year. The first of these measures
would put a few of the unemployed to profitable
use, the second would render a few thousand
acres available for fodder-growing. But neither
the one nor the other would afford adequate.relief
in the present, nor safeguard the State against a
possibly disastrous future. N.S. Wales is suffer-
ing now for the unthrift, the negligence, the folly
of half a century. The State is like a farmer who,
coming into a mixed estate, sold all the best
patches, mortgaged the balance and had a good
time while spending the proceeds. A bad
time following such a course is apt to be
bitter; and bitter is the lot, and blue
the outlook of N.S.W. at the present time.
Neither academic discussion nor apologetic ad-
missions will mend it. Reform must begin on the
inner edge and stretch right out. The condition
of the county of Cumberland is more disgraceful
than that of the Western Division. Large
volumes of the Hawkesbury flood-waters could be
turned to profit in every year. Dr. Lang dis-
cerned that 60 years back. And if the watershed
of the tributaries of that river were intelligently
handled, N.S.W. would have a full lake at Pros-
pect and cultivated green fields right up to the
mountain feet in any drought. The same applies
to a dozen districts along the coast, and in a less
degree to a good many big patches about the
middle courses of the westward flowing rivers.
Further out, surface storage and artesian supply
have possibilities which have never yet been esti-
mated, and which certainly will not, under any
circumstances, be fully realised by this genera-
tion. Still, there might be a beginning, and, so
far as all information available shows, there is no
prospect of that m the minds of any responsible
people. Water famine is menacing Sydney now
as directly as starvation menaces any Western
bullock dragging out life on a dry plain, yet
the Water Commissioners have not got be-
yond telling the people to be careful,
and the Government is “considering.” The
only irrigation plant ever put down beside the
Hawkesbury waters is dismantled and advertised
for sale. Now it would pay its cost in a single
season, and neither Gov rnment nor Parliament
has any ideas of new and practical developments
there. Further out, what is there but talk and
froth ? What is the outcome of the Western
Districts Commission and of half-a-dozen State
trips across the desert ? Is there any place ready,
or getting ready, which would justify Parliament
in voting a million of money ? Is there anybody
in charge fit for the occasion ? A negative reply
may not carry us much further, but if the country
evinced a determination that fitness for Minis-
terial position should be proved by adequate
dealing with the water conservation business,
something might be tabled which would merit
serious discussion. But what can be hoped for
from a State whose Parliament devotes a fort-
night to a battle for office over a reduction-of-
members question, and a futile night to a matter
which involves the stock, the credit, the very
lives of a large percentage of its people?

The Cry of Vaile.
A person named Vaile has arisen in Auckland
(M.L.) Chamber of Commerce and moved “ That
in the opinion of this meeting the time has
arrived when Great Britain, her colonies and de-
pendencies, should seriously consider what means
can be devised to attract the overflow of popula-
tion of the United Kingdom to said colonies and
dependencies; also that the foregoing resolution
be forwarded to London Chamber of Commerce
with a request that it would help to carry out the
idea.” Vaile appears to be threatened with sense.
For many, many years about 70 per cent, of the
overflow of the United Kingdom has streamed
away to the U.S.A., Argentina and other foreign
places, and this proportion is increasing. Mean-
while, the map have taken to marking a very
large area of Britain as “unpopulated,” despite the
increase of city population. Canada holds out all
sorts of inducements to immigrants, and yet
it gets only a fraction compared with the
United States. Maoriland has respectable land
laws, but Maoriland is a long way off.
Australia has no land laws, speaking in a civilised
manner; and it, too, is a long way off, and, in
addition, is a poor land cursed with droughts,
ticks, Philp Governments and other plagues.
S’Africa is only fit for capitalists ; the ever-
increasing supply of cheap black labor scares away
the industrious white man with little capital but
strong arms and pluck. A few years ago the only
trades affected by the blacks were the navvies’and
blacksmiths’. To-day Hindus run shops, Malays
do the carpentering, Cingalese run tailoring estab-
lishments, Chinamen do the gardening and laun-
drying, and at least one firm has taken to
employing Babu clerks. Of course, there is no
reason, except misgovernment, why the English-
man should want to emigrate at all. There are a
few enormously congested localities in the United
Kingdom where the bulk of the ever-inereasing
population is centred, but more than half the
country averages about 40 persons to the sq. mile.
But the heavy rents demanded by aristocratic land-
lords, and the enormous taxes levied on land to
make up for the want of customs revenue (sacri-
ficed in the interests of Freetrade) make it pay
better to grow wheat for the London market in
the middle United States, where they don’t
crush the local agriculturist with taxes to avoid
taxing the foreigner, and where there are no
lordly or ducal landlords on the English scale,
than in England itself. So the farmer who wants
to grow wheat for London leaves the vicinity of
London, and goes to America from motives of
economy. His fixed resolution to make for a place
with a high, reliable Protective tariff is quite
remarkable.

Mr. H. V. Wigg, an alderman, or something, of
Grenfell (N.S.W.), has just issued a pamphlet re-
printing a speech made by him on Feb. 27 in
regard to the Boer war. The speech is a wild
denunciation of the Anti-War League, and its
publication is just now most opportune. From it
we learn that the Boers started the late war from
“racial hatred ” ; that they opened it by “sack-
ing, burning, or horribly and filthily desecrating”
British homes in Cape Colony; that they were
guilty of white-flag treachery; that they had
incurred “a huge and terrible debt of vengeance ”
to the natives, whom they had oppressed ; that
their character was marked by “invincible un-
cleanliness, dense and appalling ignorance and
callous indifference”; that the Boers “arequite in-
capable of understanding a generous act in war ” ;
and finally this :
And remember, if England gives way now in South
Africa she must lose prestige. It is a “ fight Co the
finish ” for one or the other. Unconditional surrender of
the Boers means that the Old Flag’ is victorious, and
England the conqueror dictating her own terms. Any
other terms, if granted, would mean that she stands
beaten, submitting to terms instead of dictating them.
For the honor of our race this must not be.
This was the language of every Jingo from Cham-
berlain downwards until Feb., 1902. But isn’t
it almost high treason now ? Does Mr. Wigg
dare say now that England “stands beaten,” and
that the people to which she has pledged herself
to give full self-government, and to whom she
sends soft messages welcoming them as worthy
fellow - citizens, are full of racial hatred, of
treachery, of cruelty, of appalling ignorance,
and so on ?
