One of the unscrupulous dodges by which the
Philp Government, and the mendacious news-
papers which pimp for it, have endeavored to
raise public wrath against the Federal Govern-
ment is the tale of the civilised kanaka who is to
be compulsorily returned to his island by the
process of a callous Federal law. He is to be cast
back into savagery—probably killed and eaten by
a tribe which has forgotten him. At last Acting-
Premier Deakin has grown weary of all this, and
has in effect called the whole Philp crowd an
Ananias. He has written to the dreadful
Queensland person, pointing out that the
only new element in the Federal anti-Kanaka law
is that it limits, and finally abolishes, the import-
ation of new Kanakas. The return of the
civilised Queensland nigger to his old savagery
among the islands is provided for under a (pre-
sumably brutal and callous) Queensland State
law, passed by the (presumably savage and un-
feeling) party to which Philp belongs, and which
he now leads. Deakin’s surprise that Philp and
co. should have originated and maintained laws
which they declare to be so grossly inhuman is
expressed m a way that should pierce the hide of
a rhinoceros.

“F.”: Melbourne Women’s Hospital is now living
above its income to the extent of about .£2OOO a
year, and the managerial dames and spinsters are
talking of a special effort to put the maintenance
fund in credit. If the special effort results in a
conspicuous failure, as it probably will, some of
the wards will have to be closed. The writer,
who doesn’t believe in smacking lovely woman
when she is down, begins to feel rather sorry for
the so-called “catmittee” of this discredited in-
stitution, whose want of funds will be attribu ed
to their want of fitness to run the show. As a
matter of fact increased expenditure accounts for
one-third of the £2OOO a year shortage in the
Women’s Hospital income, whilst the falling off in
the subscription is, perhaps, partly due to the
hospital's loss of prestige through the publication
of scandalous wrangles between the “catmittee”
and the male medical staff. The man in the street
who talks as though he were a liberal subscriber
to hospitals (which he isn’t), and a well-informed
critic of their management (which he isn’t), has
already jumped to the conclusion that the
“catmittee” are exasperating meddlers, one
and all, whereas the male medical professionals
are a crowd of noble, unselfish toilers, now engaged
in “ boycotting” the institution because they have
only its welfare at heart. This seems to be the
popular view of the situation among the multitude
whodon’thelpto keep thecharity going, rather than
among the few who do, and whose subscriptions
show a falling off on account of bad times and the
recent fashionable exodus to London. The popular
view is altogether wrong as regards most of the
“ cats ”on the Women’s Hospital executive. Two
or three of the “ cats ” may be almost as bad as all
of them are painted, and these two or three sour
eross-examinersof “fallen” girls, these distempered
autocrats of the maternity ward, have brought
the general management into disrepute. But
the Hospital is something more than a place for the
production of babies. It has wards for the treat-
ment of other than maternity complaints, and on
the whole it gets more praise from outcoming
patients than is given to other Melbourne hospitals
that have escaped scandal. The trouble between
the catmittee and the medical men is mainly a
sex-fight, arising from grievances on both sides.
Interference which should have been countered
with manly tact has been resented with unpardon-
able insolence. When the dogs cease barking at
them the cats will purr again, and that’s all about
it, or nearly all about it.
“Chass.” to The Bulletin re the position in the
Victorian Msdlee: —
“ Glenara’s ” (8./5/’O2) S.YV. Vic. tenant system was
too optimistic. The present season promises to be one of
the worst ever known. The provision made for water
on the plains is wholly inadequate to the needs of the
heavy increase of population, and most of the new
settlers have to take up the old weary game of water-
carting. At present one small council dam is supplying
most of them, and if that gives out 1 The land
around here, until recently rented at less than 2s. 3d. per
acre for sheep raising, was, in one case, sold at 525. fid.
per acre because it wouldn’t return even that small
amount at grazing. During three good years, with mild,
dry winters and opportune spring rains, the old
farmers obtained good crops by the use of heavy
and expensive dressings of superphosphates, and
realised good prices owing to the Boer war and the
drought further north. These things, with a general
eye by interested parties to pickings, commissions, and
big rents, created a boom and a general rush for laud
here. But the end is not yet. With chaff about £5 a
ton, on a bleak wind-swept piain, without firewood, with
miserable kipsies for themselves, and worse shelter for
their horses—the last state of the mallee-farmer is worse
than the first. What the drought has done for them
up north, landlords, machine-agents, storekeepers,
and low prices (certain to come shortly) will do for
them here. The price fixed by the landlords leaves
no chance of them ever being anything but human
slaves. Land that old fanners bought for £3 per acre,
and struggled for years to clear of debt, has been run up
to double that figure. In fact the tenant-system here is
the straw at which the drowning man catches. The
landlord is as merciless as the drought-fiend in the north,
and as certain in his workings. Yet some of the big
estates were offered to Govt, at 50s. and 525. 6d. an acre.
At that price a crust could be made, but Peacock and
co. were then busy over the Boer-farm-burning business ;
and the Age was yelling wildly about watering the mallee,
which will never grow wheat. By the time water
reaches there the farmer will be gone, and Lascellbs and
Co. will scoop the benefit of the huge expenditure of
ratepayers’ money,; and when the landlord works his
ruin here, about the best and toughest material to build
up a nation any country on God’s earth ever had will
have drifted away for ever.

“W.S.T.”: lam one of many who would like to
know if there is any probability of the N.S.W.
Resumption of Estates, or Closer Settlement Act,
coming into practical force. The weak point of
the measure is that the State can only buy where
tlie owner offers his land for sale, and as this
would entail the naming of a price far beyond the
existing valuation for land-taxation purposes, it is
not likely many estates will be offered under the
Act. To make this legislation of any value we
should have to adopt the Maoriland system under
which the State chooses what land it requires to
place people on farms. Many would-be cultiva-
tors would then jump at the chance of some of the
rich lands upon the Western slopes of the Blue
Mountains, for there is practically no good land
open for selection in N.S.W. convenient to a
market, or in fact anywhere but in the Great
Western Desert, otherwise the Western Division.

♦
Bland’s “Great Ruby,” at Sydney
Lyceum, is a refreshing detective drama, with no
lost woman in it, but instead a much-mislaid
jewel. The characters spend their time chasing
after that ornament and grouping themselves
around it admiringly. There are the usual society
ladies, in Worth costumes, talking bitterly of
their poverty, and everything happens in the
midst of a high-class cricket match, or a military
tournament, or something equally gorgeous. Even
“ the village street,” which might be excused for
being plain and humble, has a bright and decora-
tive appearance about it. The Ruby goes through
many vicissitudes. It is stolen from the glittering
shop of Sir John Garnett in a rather simple and
sudden manner ; is lost in a chocolace-box, strug-
gled for in a portmanteau (which burst open on
Saturday night when it shouldn’t and put the actors
to shame), and also journeys up in a balloon in com-
pany with a Hindu prince and an English thief
(who escapes from pursuit by this simple process).
It is much desired by Frances Ross, Detective
Bland Holt, and the members of a gang who
concoct deep designs behind newspapers in the
parks. A virtuous woman may be far above
rubies, but she is seldom so much run after. And
unless a balloon with a virtuous woman in it went
to an extraordinary height the proverb was all
wrong anyhow.
» * * *
Mrs. Bland Holt, who suspects all her
friends of not being above stealing jewels, and
does so in a public manner and on small provoca-
tion, acts naturally and without exaggeration.
She is a nice, amiable vulgarian, and when she
discovers that she has called numerous well-
dressed ladies and gentlemen Barabbases with-
out cause, she repents at once, and beseeches
her husband to “ smack her hard.” The scene
blots itself out just then, so that one cannot tell if
the lady was slapped hard or not. There is no
audible slap, anyhow. Miss Frances Ross, as a
well-bred lady thief, wears the costly garments of
this class of sin with grace. She is to be com-
mended for all of them except the yellow robe
that harmonises with the purple room, said robe
being rather long in the tail for all the work she
has to do in it. She walks up and down the
purple room pouring her soul out to Baker, but
has to stop every now and then to bring the
yellow robe to its bearings. Sometimes her maid
wanders in and arranges it for her ; a fine scene
is spoilt by the vagaries of the otherwise
pleasing garment. Baker is a melancholy
Hindu Prince who walks through his
part pleasantly and dies in an agricultural
tournament, mainly because one of the Ruby-
stealers makes derogatory rem arks about Frances
Ross when clinging to another man’s balloon in
the clouds. Bland Holt is necessarily heavier
than usual as a somewhat serious detective ; and
it is to be feared that Miss Harrie Ireland made
some trouble before consenting to take the thank-
less part of an unloved lady- ecretary, on whom
Bland Holt resolves to take pity—after he has
settled about the Ruby. Numerous other charac-
ters wander in and out of the scenes, and if some
of these other characters, also some of the prin-
cipal characters, took the trouble to talk more
distinctly, the audience would feel better satis-
fied.
* * * »
The still-life scenery of this drama is ex-
cellent, especially the glittering shop and the
lounge at Oatland’s Park Hotel. Some exception
must be taken to the scene wherein the balloon
ascends half way to the flies, and numerous clouds
fall down from the top and are pushed, out from
the wings. The clouds are all right so far as they
go, but they don’t go far enough, and the hard
wooden floor spoils the illusion of height. If the
management would sell the tail of Miss Ross’s
dress and buy more clouds with the proceeds, so
that that floor which now appears in the sky could
be disguised, the “Great Ruby” would be an
artistic as well as a financial success.
** * #
“ Facing the Music,” after inspiring laugh-
ter for five weeks, is shortly to be withdrawn from
Sydney Palace, and will be replaced by a comedy,
new to Sydney, of a more sentimental and less
farcical type.
** * *
Charles 11. and his dog are now guiding
the destinies of England in their third week at
Sydney Royal. The dog is one of the few stage
animals who seem to know their parts perfectly.
His business is principally to sit on a table —in the
royal apartment from which the stairway opens, so
that the draught may blow direct on the royal
ear—and be ruminated to by the king about the
difficulty of steering the troubled State. The
small dog rises when the king asks his opinion,
and offers his cold nose to Charles by way of
counsel. Then the cares of State burst in
on Charles, and sweep the dog away—at
all events the public loses sight of him in the
rush. He doesn’t take a front place in the drama
after that. Presumably when Charles died he
left the dog to his successor, and doubtless he was
trodden on by the rush of the invading Dutch
army a few years later, and obliterated from
history. Charles apparently was faithful to four
things—three women and a dog. These four lend
a touch of kindliness to the stage character
of the Frisky King. He lobks like a man who
would have carried his pup out of burning London
regardless of risks, even if he had to leave his wife
behind to do it.
** * #
The inconsequential Judith still wails over
brother Iscariot at Sydney Criterion, though, it
seems to the writer, not at such length as formerly.
The fact that Barabbas may have been a high-
class, well-dressed robber, as Miss Williamson
depicts him, is just beginning to displace that old
idea of him as a low person who said the Hebrew
for blanky, and belonged to the push that leaned
against Herod the Great’s back fence. Some
traditions give out that Barabbas was in prison
for political offences —that he was one of the
partly patriotic, partly villainous, partly fanatical
and partly mad organisation of the Robbers which
defended Jerusalem with its last tooth against
Titus and his Romans. And the distinction
drawn between the robber who wasn’t executed
and the two thieves who were is worth noting.
Miss Maud Williamson might do worse than
revise Barabbas, aud make him another Wallace
or Bolivar, stating through the keyhole of his
dungeon that Judah will yet be free, and will
grind the skull of Caesar on the 144 steps of the
temple.
** * *
Be “Barabbas” at Sydney Criterion, it
may be casually mentioned that Miss Maud
Williamson, who plays the part of Miss Iscariot,
is a lady who has passed through much theatrical
tribulation. Asa blind heroine, supposed to fall
into the river, she has been suspended in the air
by the invisible wires that ought to have been
cut; she has had her foot pinned to the floor
by the unblunted sword of a stage duellist; and
once she was nearly drowned in a tank, because
the three men sent in to rescue her lost their
heads, and spent the time rescuing one another.
She was brought to with restoratives, and
comforted with a medal. Maud’s second-last
grief was the Plague Scare which helped to spoil
the first part of her Criterion season, and her
very latest sorrow is caused by the new concrete
flooring in the Criterion, which has caused an
epidemic of severe colds ever since the company
has had to stand about the wings in the light,
ancient Judean garments of “Barabbas.” The
coughing of the Criterion company as it waits in
the wings has grown so frequent that if
“Barabbas” remains on the boards much
longer Peter will some evening have to
save the situation by remarking to Judas that
the groans of the early Christians in the adjacent
dungeons are much louder this afternoon. As they
wait about for their turn to go on the members of the
co. read to each other ecclesiastical letters lament-
ing their appearance in religious and desecrating
&, and warning them of the soul-peril to come.
he company buttons its twentieth century
ulster round its first century toga, and dances on
its ancient and sandalled feet to warm them, and
is but little impressed by the threat that it will be
warmer by - and - bye. Peter, who can keep
tolerably warm even on the boards in the large,
voluminous dressing-gown, which seems to have
been the uniform of the first disciples, is much
envied. This is a cold season for a play which is
dressed in sandals and the long pale cotton night-
shirt of classic days.

There was no alteration in the strong

** * *
“My Jack,” the latest deliverance at
Melb. Royal, strikes one as having been gathered
together from the wreckage of several bygone
melodramas. There’s a lighthouse and consider-
able rolling sea in the first act ; then somebody
gets murdered on account of missing papers, and
the wrongly-suspected Inman can’t prove his in-
nocence until he is stranded on the old Egyptian
desert together with another thirsty castaway,
who confesses everything in a villainous foreign
accent. The desert hysterics of the parched
foreigner are followed by the discovery of a
missing Flag, which involves the honor of the
British Empire and is produced with brilliant
success from underneath Inman’s shirt, where the
hero has been saving it from the Arabs. It is
only the sort of Flag that a stockbroker waves on
Mafeking Nights under the influence of drink,
but it reflects great credit on Inman and probably
leads to his being made a peer when he has come
into his property and taken over a baronetcy from
H. O. Willard. The trouble about the property
is so very old and mysterious that in a legal-
minded melodrama Inman would stand a poor
show to get any property at all, notwithstanding
the Papers, but “My Jack” is not a
legal-minded drama, though it might be
more wildly insane than it is. A few extra
touches of lunacy would improve the narra ive
rather than otherwise, especially in the fourth
act, where the two men converse together con-
cerning their exhausted condition and other little
matters until the audience would be glad to see
a thu derbolt come down from Heaven and kill
them both. For that matter, a fatal thunderbolt
would be a pleasing novelty in any drama. As a
destructive agent and a thrilling mechanical effect
it has hitherto been overlooked by authors and
managers.
* » » *
Willard, Inman, Bert Bailey, and Miss
Eugenie Duggan are practically the same people
in this case as in a dozen previous cases, where
Willard met with retribution. Temple Harrison
is more seriously wicked than usual, and it doesn’t
suit him. T.H.’s spurious Cockney nasallsm,
which sounds like an abortive attempt to imitate
Bland Holt, grows very trying when it is sup-
posed to express the penitence and despair of a
bloo 1-stained ruffian named Panitza. Several
small characters are well filled in by Geo. Carey,
Walter Rivers and others. If “My Jack” were
not limited to two weeks, The Bulletin would
lodge a particular reproof against the scenic artist
for painting a marquee on the margin of an ocean,
as though it were a canvas bathing-machine.
** * *
At Her Majesty’s the “Florodora” re-
vival is a genuine boom as revivals go, and to-
morrow night . (Friday), when Grace Palotta
makes her farewell appearance as the Widow who
“can’t pretend that she doesn’t know things,” the
big house in Exhibition-street will be crowded
more or less. Assisted by sundry new “gags”
and stage arrangements “ Florodora ” goes fas all
the time. Whereas Brownlow used to take the
whole stage to himself when he was slowly asking
an invisible Dolores to meet him under the palms,
his successor, Harold Thorley, addresses the same
song to the lady in person, which is a
rational way of making an appointment.
Also, he warbles with comparative briskness, so
the difference between Brownlow and Thorley is
not always deplored. Some tableaux of a Jingo
illustrated-newspaper character were hitched on
to the entertainment last Saturday night, but this
scribe did not stay to see “Britannia and her
Daughters,” or the representations of Britain’s
Friendship, Justice, and Love in Boerland, or
the spectacle of King Edward Ban took and Queen
Alexandra Palotta being crowned by Archbishops
Hugh Ward and Howard Vernon at Westminster
Abbey. The man who didn’t stop to witness
Britain’s Love for the Boer must have missed an
amazing treat, anyhow.

old fairy tale will be preceded, or followed, by
“ Trial by Jury,”
* * *
The first performance of “A Chinese
Honeymoon,” at Melbourne Princess’s on Mon-
day night, showed a piece very promising in some
places and very weak in others. It lasted for
three and a-half hours, and kept the prompter
always on the alert. The play is so full of easy
opportunities for improvement, and so likely to
lead to alterations in the cast before the week is
out, that The Bulletin will defer judgment till
next issue. “A Chinese Honeymoon ” seems
to have drawn its ideas from “The Mikado”
and “The Geisha,” and in some of its. hap-
piest moments the suggestion of Gilbert and
Sullivan is decidedly clever. Probably Gilbert
could convert it into a sparkling little operatic
comedy without much trouble, but if that bilious
expert were to take control of Musgrove’s patchy
production, he would start proceedings by en-
gaging new artists for at least three of the parts.
On Monday night Miss May Beatty saved the
situation frequently by her high-spirited perform-
ance of a “ low comedy” girl. May Beatty has
only a crude conception of Cockney character, but
her vigor, good humor and intelligence enabled
her to get away with the honors in this case.
For the rest Edward Lauri was not so successful
as he presently will be. Baritone Piddock sang
and whistled sweetly, and cut rather a serious
figure in an unthankful part as the lover of Miss
Josephine Stanton. Miss Cissie Niel made
a feeble first appearance, and tuneful Henry
Hallam showed a lack of detail in his act-
ing as the Emperor of China. Other people
were not too satisfactory, but they got little
chance to score until the clock had struck 11 and
matters were being hurried. The strength of “ A
Chinese Honeymoon ” lies in the second act, and
as all previous shows of the same class, except
“ Florodora,” have been weak where this is strong,
the management should make a desperate .effort to
remedy the first-night defects ; the thing is worth
a desperate effort.

At the Opera House the latest arrivals are
two Brothers —a plain, gymnastic brother and a
monkeyfied ditto—who perform on horizontal
bars, and bound upwards from an elastic table
without striking any new ideas to speak of.
The Concertina King has played himself into the
affections of a generous public, and the Nawns are
appearing in a new sketch. Tom Nawn goes
to sleep and dreams the dream of a broguey Irish-
man who has raised a spook in much the same
way that Petramos Dunn brought Niobe to life.
Nawn’s dream introduces beer and domestic diffi-
culties. Also it introduces Miss Katherine Nawn,
a young lady who takes after Mrs. Nawn as
regards the pain in her voice. “Pat and the
Genie ” is a pleasant little joke that will show to
more advantage when the Sidney Drews have
gone away. The Drews, and their dawg, likewise
the oppressed inaid-servant and her miserable hat
are leaving the Opera House this week.

Donald Macdonald’s beautiful calm
audacity is not the least of his qualifications for
public lecturing. At Melb. Athenaeum t’other
night Mac took the platform as per advt. to
discourse on “War and Peace” in aid of the
Women Writers’ Club. There was a well-crowded
house, about 90 per cent, of the audience being old
admirers of the journalist in kharkee, who had come
expecting to hear something quite new and up-to-
date concerning the subjects mentioned in the
ad. Mac’s latest entertainment, however, proved
to be mainly a selection of items from his
original Ladysmith yarn. When he had
talked for about 40min. he called for some
pictures of English buildings and places
historic and otherwise to be put upon the screen,
and these represented the “Peace” portion of
his lecture. Then came lOmin. interval, after
which Mac. went on with some more of his
S’African experiences. The fact that they were
worth hearing once again testifies to the merit of
the raconteur and his reminiscent matter; but
what chiefly impressed one was his unassuming
“front.” Only a born showman could casually
introduce a series of irrelevant limelight views
into the middle of a well-worn War lecture and
call them “Peace.” Donald Mac. will make an
effective M.P one of these days if he yields to
the awful temptation.
*# * *
English papers have been publishing as a
recent item of news the old, old story that “an
Australian manager” (who is, of course, the
irrepressible Dan Barry) hitches the names of
deceased notorieties—Rev. Spurgeon, Parnell and
W. E. Gladstone —to three of the melodramas
which he produces. A Bulletin correspondent
wants to know “ In what backblock townships are
these fabrications practised?” Well, Geelong was
not so many years ago stirred to its religious core
by Dan’s audacious presentment of “ The Parson’s
Oath, by Rev. C. H. Spurgeon,” and Dan did very
well out of it. A local clergyman, Rev. Webb,
(he died quite recently), wrote to the Geelong
papers protesting that his distinguished brother-
in-the-Lord could never have been guilty of
writing any drama for the stage, much less “The
Parson’s Oath.” The result of Rev. Webb’s in-
dignation was crowded houses for Dan Barry, who
used to come before the curtain with a roll of
paper in his hand and invite anyone in the
audience to step forward and examine The
Contract, signed by Mr. Spurgeon’s son.” No-
body in the gentle Geelong crowd ventured upon
the stage to examine Dan’s bluffsome document,
so the “drama by Spurgeon” was accepted on its
producer’s pretensions. All the same the Rev. Webb
remained sceptical. At the end of a phenomenally
-successful season he sought out Dan and said
“Sir, I am unable to believe that your play was
really written by Mr. Spurgeon.” Dan dug the
good' man in the ribs and replied, “I know it
wasn’t, and so do you.” “ Then,” said the shep-
herd, “ I regret, I deeply regret having written
my letter to the press, for I fear it served to
attract people to the theatre, even members of
my own congregation.” “Fell in ! chirped
Daniel Barry. His Rev. friend gazed upon him
with a sad twinkle in his eye, and murmured
“ Yes, indeed—indeed— they fell in”
At Adelaide Royal, the Waldorf season
ended with a few nights of “The Royal Divorce,”
which took the place of several contemplated
revivals. H. H. Vincent scored a success as
Napoleon. The general feeling of the public
seems to be one of surprise at the advertising of
one lady as a star, and the other members of the
co. merely as support, whereas the others turn out
to be a very strong all-round crowd. The No. 2
Comics have returned from the West, reviving
“ A Runaway Girl ” and “ Belle of New York.”
Brownlow (who had a large farewell concert at
the Town Hall) dropped out, and Arthur Crane
(late baritone at the Tivoli) dropped in. “The
Wrong Mr. Wright” filled the little Tivoli for
three solid weeks, but has at last given way to
Smith and the Cook-Ladies’ Union.
Printed in S.M. Herald of June 28 :
Burrowa, Friday.
There is profound sorrow for the King’s illness. The
holiday was kept up, 300 school children and 500 adn ts
were entertained oti the recreation reserve at a picnic
and sports, and there were fireworks and a bonfire at
night.
Democrats in all the States will regret to
hear of the serious illness of Q. Labor-leader
Browne, just now prostrated with his old trouble,
asthma. Browne is a great sufferer, but is sus-
tained by a big heart. His absence from the
Assembly at the beginning of a most important
session will be a great loss to his party.
Melb. magistrate Panton, wishing to clear
his court, the other day, of women and boys,
thundered out : “All those who are not criminals
leave the Court! ” In the scramble that ensued
the chiefs of the Rats’ Push, who had dropped in
for an hour’s diversion, were the first to reach the
street.
Walter James, whose accession to the Wes-
tralian Premiership is announced as The Bulletin
goes to press, is 38 years of age—so he ranks among
the youngest Premiers on record in these States.
Australasia has had such an appalling number of
Premiers that it is difficult to find out who got to
the front earliest ; but Boucaut, of S.A., who
arrived at the age of 35, and Vogel, Byrnes, and
James at 38 were among the youngest.
Leo (that sounds British, anyhow) Wein-
thal, in the Jewish World, on Jews in S’Africa,
says there are over 20,000 of them, and there
“The most, solid and tangible results have been
attained by Jewish pioneers in all branches of legislative,
administrative, legal, financial, and commercial develop-
ment.”
The tribe of Moses can afford to let a little of the
cat out of the bag now.
Mr. Louis Grist is the athletic young
Australian composer who spends the intervals
between repairing the punctured tyres of his
bicycle in writing roundelays and barcarolles.
He is a troubadour knight of modern pattern,
with a laugh that would assure him a pass to any
of the alleged funny shows when someone is
wanted to start the smile. Like most cheerful-
natured persons his genius runs seriously, bursting
into “ Ave Marias ” and “Farewells.” “Stars to
Guide Me ” is said to be his most efficacious pro-
duction. Among other athletic feats Mr. Grist
lately warbled £5O profit out pf a suburban
audience towards a local place of worship.
After a two months' pursuit, the Kenniffs
have been captured in Queensland, simply, it
would seem, because they were sick of the. bush-
ranging life and camping out without a fire with
the thermometer below freezing-point. They
have cost the Queensland Government about £5OOO.
A few more Kenniffs would just about empty the
Queensland Treasury. Could not the Government
send them before trial on a lecturing tou” to cover
expenses ?
The swamp at the back of the boat-sheds,
near Princes Bridge, which was about the last
place in Melbourne proper where the reed-birds
made music, is now being filled up. The local
authorities only know why-. They might have
easily turned the little bit of ancient swamp into
a picturesque lakelet, fringed with reeds—a
souvenir of early Melbourne. But then it
wouldn’t have been an Improvement, as the
aldermanic mind understands the term.
The unexpected acceptance by Edmund
Barton of a knighthood strengthens the public
belief that the Prime Minister will shortly retire
from the Ministry to the Federal High Court. It
is no secret that Barton is poor enough to look
askance at the added responsibility of keeping up
a title on the uncertain income of a politician. A
P.C.-ship, and now a G.C.M G.-ship, are as much
as he is likely to secure from the hurly-burly of
politics, and he will indeed be a foolish man not
to shortly provide a more stable position for him-
self than the Prime Minister’s chair.
What has become of the ukase issued a
few years ago that in future all appointments to
the Order of St. Michael and St. George were to
be made at the bottom of the ladder, promotions
thereafter to be made from C.M.G. to K.C.M.G.,
and thence to G.C.M.G. ? The coronation list of
honors has shown that this scheme has been
abandoned. And the pity of it ! Had Premier
See and others had to start as plain Companions
of the Order they would not have accepted the
“distinction,” and extinction of the whole crowd
of grand crosses and knights might have followed
in time.
There is" just now a very strong anti-im-
perial movement in Canada, the essence of which
was put by ex-Minister G. A. Nantel in a recent
speech :
Canada does not find itself in the same position as the
other great colonies which are isolated, such as S. Africa
and Australia. She dwells in the midst of a powerful
world, I may say from many points more puissant than
Great Britain herself— the American world. Is it reason-
able that if we renounce the formation of an independent
nation, a Canadian Empire by ourselves, we will go and
precipitate ourselves into the whirlpool of pan-Britannism,
instead of marching with a neighbor as rich and for that
matter as liberal and as generous as our suzerain, who
lives 3000 miles away from us, and under conditions of
existence to which we are strangers, when they are not
directly opposed and hostile to our proper means of
existence ? If Canada must live by Imperialism of some
kind, let us speak of American Imperialism, and not o
British Imperialism ; let us have nothing to say of that.
N.S.W. Public Service Gazette, dealing
with the appeals of 832 public officers, dating
from Ist January, 1901, was published on June 27,
two days before the vote lapsed, and there was a
great scatter to pay the long-deferred increments
before the Treasury closed down on the financial
year on June 30. Only 163 (or a bare fifth of those
who appealed) received anything at all, and most
of these but small advancements of £5 and
£lO a year. Owing to the absurdly small, sum
that the P.S.B. requested Parliament to vote to
satisfy the just demands of officers dissatisfied
with their last grading, the Board could not have
done otherwise. But to the great number of
appellants who were met with the remark, that
the Board had not enough money to go round, the
knowledge that they were refused on this ground
was extremely galling.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Sydney Morning Herald (25/6/’O2) again
preaches to Australia the Freetrade paper’s favorite doc-
trine of cowardice and helplessness, thus:—“ The
Treasurer is often told that he should pay for all his new
works out of revenue. This might be possible if he could
save up for the purpose. The Constitution, however,
prevents this, and he cannot have a surplus even of one
penny.” Moral, of course: The Federal Government
must borrow. The anxiety of the Sydney dailies to prove
that the Federal Government must borrow is something
pathetic. And yet, strange as it may seem in the case of
such a solemnly respectable paper as the Herald, the
above yarn is utterly and absolutely false—a poor, bald,
unconvincing tarradiddle invented in support of the doc-
trine of cowardice and helplessness. The Federal
Government can retain one-fourth of the customs and
excise revenue, and so far as that goes it can pay
for public works out of revenue. It can levy any other
taxes it pleases, and retain all of them, and pay for public
works out of the proceeds. It has the same power of
paying for works out of revenue as any other Govern-
ment has. As for the theory that the Constitution
doesn’t allow the Federal Treasurer to have a penny sur-
plus, he doesn’t want a penny surplus. He only wants
enough to pay his legitimate outgoings, which are the
cost of Federal Government in its various branches,
public works expenditure, and, when the Commonwealth
takes over the State debts, provision for interest and
sinking fund thereon. Any surplus would be as useless
to him as five legs to a hen. But if the Herald means to
say that he can’t pay for public works, except out of a
surplus, it might as well say that he can’t pay the
Governor-General’s salary except out of a surplus. In a
well-ordered State public works expenditure is as much
a part of the regular expenditure as anything else. In
England, for instance, public works are as distinctly a
regular charge on the revenue as the wages of the Civil
Service—but perhaps the S.M. Herald never heard of
England. If that paper of weak apologies and helpless
remarks about the impossibility of things had only a
tenth of the English grit and honesty in financial affairs,
instead of being English in its war policy 7 and a sort of
flabby, half-bred dish-cloth in public money matters, it
would teach a much more valuable lesson. The gospel of
put-it-off and give-it-up and let-it-slide is the Herald’s
special gospel, and it is as undermining as drink, and as
demoralising as the habit of being dead.
• • • »
There is one point which the anti-loan party in
the Federal Parliament needs to keep in mind if it is
going to be of any use whatever as a party 7. The country
needs public works—it is impossible to have civilisation
without them. Therefore, it is no earthly good refusing
to borrow for public works unless the party is willing to
find the money for them otherwise. It is essential to
have a separate Public Works Fund, into which the pro-
ceeds of certain taxes, services, or steals—into which
some reliable revenues of our own—will be paid, so that
works can go on, and the anti-loan party should demand
that such sources of revenue be provided and so applied.
Every attempt at economy in loans so far has merely con-
sisted in leaving necessary works undone and existing
works unrepaired, and when the public grew tired of
leaving things in that state the big loans commenced
again. They always will commence again under like
conditions, for the country can’t fall back on aboriginal
principles. Nothing can be effected by relying on the
surplus—this country never has a surplus.

The Maoriland Government Life Insurance Department
has now an accumulated life fund of £3,285,416. Out of
the various sums set apart at different times to provide
for depreciation, there remains £36,297 at credit of in-
vestment fluctuation reserve. Likewise there is £5228 at
credit of the Tontine Savings Fund, but this is kept
separate from the general fund till the tontine matures,
bo it is not included in the above.
The Department still keeps far ahead, in point
of business in force, of all competitors in its own State.
Also it is still the most cheaply managed of Australasian
offices, the A.M.P. only excepted, and among offices of
anything like its own size and age, it is the cheapest bar
none. The expense rate during the last nine years has
been as follows:

1897 14 17 2 „
This year’s expense rate has dropped with unusual sud-
denness through the transference of the funds of No. 1
Tontine, now matured, to the general fund.
There is, as usual, a slight drop in interest
earnings. There is a slight drop in interest earnings
with all assurance institutions nowaday's, and wheie the
process will end it is not easy to say, though there are
hopes that the worst is over. The record of the Maori-
land Government Life Insurance Department is as
follows :

The drop this year doesn’t amount to very much, and it
begins to seem probable that the fall in interest earnings
is nearly over, for the time at all events. In every 7
respect the Maoriland Government Life Insurance De-
partment continues its career of prosperity, and, con-
sidering the exceeding smallness of the community 7 to
which its business is confined, its progress is something
altog ;t her unusual. Its assets look solid • beyond
the average. They include no spectral or dubious
items such as ‘ ‘ Deferred Instalments of Annual
Premiums,” or the like. Out of over £3.000,000 of assets,
foreclosed properties represent the infinitesimal sum of
£5152, and overdue interest is only £472. Both these
items speak well for the safety of the investments—when
interest is difficult to collect and the mortgagors largely
develop the habit of abandoning th’eir properties to the
society, it is evidence, on the .other hand, of grievous in-
security in the assets. Taking everything into account,
the Maoriland Government Life Insurance Department is
more absolutely free of points to which objection can be
taken than any other Australasian office in its line of
business.

Officially announced that the public debt of
N.S.W. is now £71,044,734 but this does not include the
amounts still due to the property-holders in the resumed
area, so N.S.W. probably owes £75,000,000 or a little over
in one way and another. The day when the debt reaches
£100,000,000 should be declared a public holiday.
* » * •
One notable point about the Audit Bill, now
before N.S.W. Assembly, is that it gives the Auditor-
General power to audit and report on the loan accounts.
Hitherto, astounding as it may seem, the loan expendi-
ture has never been audited. This is almost as astound-
ing as the fact that in no Australasian State is there any
law defining what works may be charged to loans and
what may not.
* • • •
Maoriland grew 13,000,000 bushels of wheat in
18S9 ; this year it has grown 4,000,000, a diminution of
9,000,000 bushels (in Australia the decline was only
3,000,000 bushels). And for this contirigenting is largely
responsible, for there was no drought in Maoriland as in
Australia. Seddon’s blood-drunk islands sent a larger
proportion of their population farm-burning than
any other State, and its incendiaries were mostly taken
away from the plough, and the plough stood still in their
absence. And Maoriland gets no British grant, as the
Boers do, to repair its farms.
• • • *
The Bulletin has before it a circular purport-
ing to be issued by the City Mutual Life Assurance
Society (Sydney), in which it compares its bonuses and
results with those of the A.M.P. Society, and shouts con-
siderably over the results. It states that an A.M.P.
policy for £lOOO issued at age 30 cost £24 Is. Bd. a year
premium, and the bonuses for 1899-1900-1901 were £4B, or
£l6 a year. And it goes on to state that the City
Mutual charged only £23 10s. lOd. per year for £lOOO at
age 30, and in these same years gave bonuses amounting
to £93 6s. Bd., equal to £3l 2s. 2d. per year. This means
that the City Mutual charged 10s. lOd. less premium and
§ave £l5 a year more bonus. And if, in each case, the
onus went on at this rate for life, the City Mutual would
have some right to whoop. But whereas the £24 Is. Bd. is
the rate charged by the A.M.P. on an ordinary whole life
policy whereon, bar unforeseen accidents, the bonuses
may go on at this rate for life, the City Mutual’s charge
of £23 10s. lOd. seems to be for a special policy (Table F.)
on which an annual bonus of £3 per cent, is guaranteed
for three years only. —After that period it takes its chance
with the rest. The City Mutual’s ordinary whole life
policies apparently received £3 per cent, for the three
years ended 1894 (say, £lO a year on a £lOOO policy) ; £2
per cent, for the three years to 1897 (say, £6 13s. 4d. per
year on £1000); £1 for the three years to 1900 (say,
£3 6s. Bd. per year on £1000). Moreover, the City Mutual
seems to have given up issuing this sensational policy It
doesn’t appear at all in the latest table of rates
that writer has seen. There is a lamentable
want of candour about a document which allows the
public to infer that the bonus of £3O a year for a premium
of £23 10s. lOd is, or is likely to be, a regular thing, and
that its ordinary policies are built on these lines, or any-
thing dimly resembling them. An office which, in 1901,
had a surplus of only £9766 to divide among it policy-
holders as the result of three years’ work, isn’t con-
structed that way.
» » * *
It is reported that Melbourne Board of Works,
which looks after the water and sewerage business of
Fitzgibbon’s city, reckons that its revenue for next year
will be £477,402, and expenditure £859,705, Jeaving a
shortage of £382,303 to be made good by loan. Even
allowing for the fact that the Melbourne Board of Sewers
and Shirks is still spending money on construction, its
affairs are drifting into a shocking state, and increased
taxation, or bankruptcy, or an appeal to the Govern-
ment to come to the rescue, after the fashion of
Sydney in bygone years, begins to loom among
the events of the near future. Melbourne’s water-
rate is 6d. in the £, and it has a splendid supply—
miles ahead of the average Euglish city. This country
always expects to get the best of everything for the
lowest charge. In Great Britain, Aberdeen pays BJd.
in the £ for water; Aberystwith, 2s. ; Ayr, lOd. ;
Bacup, Is. to Is. 6d. ; Barrow, Is. ; Bedford, 9d. ;
Belfast, Is. ; Birmingham, about Is. 2d. and baths
extra ; Blackburn, Is. 7d.; Bolton, about Is. 6d.; Bootle,
Is. lid. ; Bradford, Is. 6d., baths extra; Burnley, Is. ;
Gloucester, lOd. ; Huddersfield, about Is. 6d.; Liverpool,
Is. lid.; Manchester, 9d., baths extra, and increased rate
in the suburbs ; Macclesfield, Is. to Is. 4|d. ; Northamp-
ton, Is. to Is. 6d. ; Sheffield, about Is. 2d., baths and
water-closets both charged extra; Wolverhampton, Is.
3d. in the £, and so on. Where the rates are as low as in
Melbourne it is generally because, by a long course of
high rates, the debt has been greatly reduced or almost
extinguished, and a period of relief is setting in. In some
British towns, the rate, including all extras, runs to more
than 2s. in the £ for water alone. But in all these places
the debt incurred for waterworks is being paid off in a
large, solid way, and, some day, there will be no debt and
a rate of about 3d. The Australian idea of getting a fine
large supply, charging the lowest possible rate, never pay-
ing off the original debt, but adding to it by deficits, and
then asking the State for aid out of loans doesn’t find
favor in Great Britain.
Why municipal government is a comparative
and often a superlative failure in Australia—a debt-laden,
embarrassed, insolvent thing that is always waylaying the
passing Premier for aid, and holding out the low-spirited
hat for a dole, and why it isn’t in that state in England,
can be understood by consideration of the rates charged
in large English towns :

On a very long list there is only one town whose rates are
below 4s. in the £, and its bill is 3s. lOd. And in most
cases water rates are an extra, and come on top of these
charges. An Australian corporation which proposed to
keep solvent at half the figure which is quite common
in England, would be disembowelled by the ratepayers.
Helped by Commonwealth protection and open
interstate markets, South Australia has started to build
up what promises to be a very substantial salt industry.
One co., the Castle Salt, after a four years’ struggle, made
a gross profit last year (to May 9) of £6048 ; but £2OOO is
written off for depreciation—nothing hitherto had been
done in that respect, although the wear and tear on a
salt plant is very heavy. So far, the plant has cost the
co. over £12,000. Last year’s output was 33,600 tons, an
increase of 8500 tons on the previous years. The other
bigS.A. co. is the Federal Salt, which, during May and
the first fortnight of June, gathered 1000 tons, and ex-
pects to shortly treble this. These co’s work between
them a large number of shallow lakes on Yorke’s Penin-
sula and the West Coast.
* * * *
White Cliffs opal field is just now, The Bul-
letin hears, experiencing a rough time. The water-
supply has given out, and Govt, is employing all avail-
able teams to cart water some seven or eight miles. The
water, which is to be supplied to the public at 3s. Od. per
lOOgals., is said to be bad. The local opal market is
lower now than ever. First-quality gems always realise
a good price, but poor and medium quality stuff is almost
valueless. Opal, which a few months ago sold readily at
£1 and 30s. per oz. is scarcely saleable at ss. Of course
the miner is completely at the mercy of the buyers, and
is practically forced to sell at their prices. The whole
township presents an appearance of utter dejection,
weariness, and thirst. Also, White Cliffs suffers from the
“ Yellow Curse.” There are three bakers on the field,
two of w'hom are Chows. Out of eight stores two are
run by Celestials, two by Syrians, and one by an Afghan.
This, too, in a community that howls itself thirsty for
Unionism and a White Australia. Nathless, the
foreigners thrive on the fact that Labor-Union communi-
ties at large are so often ready to sacrifice their boasted
principles where cash is concerned, and the smug Chow
or Oriental can usually undercut the European store-
keeper by 7 a penny or two.
* * • *
By what, of course, was simply a coincidence,
Melb. papers, the other day, simultaneously published
details of a new solution of the B.H. sulphide problem.
Next day the Sulphide Corporation, which was reported
to have the process in hand, desired it said that nothing
conclusive had yet been reached as to either extraction or
costs. The process (the Sulman-Pickard) was chosen by
the co.’s metallurgist in Europe, where it was said to be
getting out 80 per cent, of the zinc, and leaving the
residue a smelting silver-lead ore. Now, this has for
years past been regarded as the ideal way of going
about the business. Schnabel, the Freiberg pro-
fessor, whom the Barrier co.’s imported 10 _ years
ago, therefore devoted his chief experiments
to the preliminary solving-out of the zinc. He could do
it in several ways ; but he could not make it pay anyhow.
In ’95, T. J. Greenwa.v, who was advising Block 14 and
the South, reported to the same effect. The sulphides
are a silver-lead ore, containing about 20 per cent, zinc
and much sulphur. Together or separately, the sulphur
and the zinc prevent a treatment by smelting. The
sulphur can be got rid of by roasting ; there are several
methods of doing it —the co’s are still experimenting, with
a view to finding out the cheapest. The sulphur gone,
the zinc still remains. The ordinary process ot zinc (or
spelter) manufacture is by distillation f that is, the ore,
mixed with coal or coke, is brought to a white heat in
small retorts and the zinc vapor is caught in con-
densers. If, however, there is much lead in the ore it
rapidly destroys the retorts, and this bars the process by
making it too expensive ; also, there is a considerable
loss of silver and lead, which go off in vapor with the
zinc, and don’t do the zinc any good. The alleged feature
of the new process is the use of a soft, caking bituminous
coal in the retorts ; this is stated to hold the silver and
lead in the pores while the zinc flies off. What is left in
the retorts, it is claimed, is a good smelting silver-lead
ore, surrounded by almost enough coke to smelt itself. It
is a very simple variation indeed from the well-known
methods ; and if it makes much difference will surprise
people who ought to know—that is, assuming that all the
leading features of the process have been stated.
It is quite possible that, with a little help, zinc-
production may be successfully begun at Broken Hill.
First of all, there is the stuff already on the surface along-
side the rails—hundreds of thousands of tons of it, and
already crushed fine. Every year about 500,000 tons are
produced, containing from 5 to 6oz. silver, 5 per cent,
lead, and 15 per cent, zinc; some richer, some poorer,
but varying less than the original ore did. This is not
only raised, then, but the first process, the crushing, is
done. The inevitable second process must be to get rid
of the sulphur. The Sulphide Corporation is trying the
Heberlein process; the Carmichael process, a Broken Hill
discovery, is claimed to be a step ahead of that. By this
the sulphur driven off can be converted into sulphurous
or sulphuric acid ; and either of these will solve the zinc,
which must be the third process. If it can be done to
pay has yet to be proved by any of the B.H. co’s., which,
so far, have been trying to get rid of the sulphur by
heap-roasting, partly, no doubt, because they can do that
without paying royalty or putting money into experi-
mental plant. They do not, however, save the sulphur
by this method. The great importance of a process for
getting out the zinc lies in the fact that, not only is
spelter very valuable (it is nearly always £3 or £4 better
than lead), but if it is out of the ore about 45 or 50 per cent,
more silver and from 20 to 25 per cent, more lead can be
recovered. If it were not for the latter fact the co.’s here
would hardly trouble much about the zinc, because, on
account of the high cost of recovery (mainly for labor and
fuel) there is no prospect of dazzling profits.
• * * *
At time of writing N.S.W. Mines Secretaryship
not decided. Office has been vacant for some weeks.
Fierce competition for the billet in and out of the depart-
ment, and much bitterness within. _ Mysterious outsider
said to be well in the running. Can it be an M.P. ? Mines
Department is the one in which political influence and
control are most largely exercised, and the Secretary is
nothing more than a routine recording official. It would
be casting an unmerited slur on the tribe of officials to
assume that not one of their number is equal to the
billet. It is a simple job for the trained red-tape man :
“ Obey orders, think in as many languages as you like,
but talk in none.” Let us, anyhow, have an honest man
—and if possible a man who really knows something about
the subject.
* * • •
Bee Mountain, a few miles south of Cobar, is
the locale of the latest find in that highly-metalliferous
district. A lode rich in copper, gold, and silver is said to
have been found. Reports necessarily very much exag-
gerated; if they are one-third true the show is good
enough. The mountain was a veritable hive of industry
for a few hours. The nondescripts of Cobar were there
in hundreds, working like Trojans—putting in pegs.
Several “ smart ” Sydney men, scenting another Cobar
boom, journeyed to the place, only to find the country-
side for miles dotted with posts and “ applied-for-lease ”
notices —mostly bogus. Catch a weasel asleep when you
get ahead of a Cobar man ! Concentrate next week and
the week after into half-an-hour in anticipation of some
prospectively-good lease-pegging on your own account,
and ten to one the Cobar fellow will strike the “ psycho-
logical moment ” before you 1 Your turn will come
when he talks 10,000’s of £s with as much nonchalance as
he displays when he calls for a pint of beer at your
expense.
• • • •
Eleanora mine, Hill grove (N.S.W.), sold in
Adelaide for £650. The plant on the ground is worth
more than that, if only as scrap-iron. Eleanora cost
Sydney and Adelaide speculators many thousands, and
worst of all, it killed a good man —Northern Territory
Griffith. Doctors certified death as from typhoid—
friends knew that it was Eleanora worry. Mine under
Adelaide control paid two 6d. divs., earned from the
treatment of old tailings in “Junction” Jolly’s time.
With the disappearance of this source of profit Jolly was
cute enough tol quit. ” Sharpe (his successor) tooted the
trumpet most vigorously in proclamation of the wonders
which he was going to perform. Now he is enjoying the
consolation which comes to the good manager, who
assumes that he can make a success of a bad mine.
Eleanora may be tried again, but not until fools with
whips of money to throw away are more plentiful than at
present. Antimony and gold-patchiness are the draw-
backs. If there were less, or more, antimony in the mins
it might be less of an anti-money proposition.
* • * *
Cobar Chesney : Alleged that another offer has
been made for this mine, and shares jumped from nil to
2s. No official intimation —for the simple reason that
there is nothing uncommon to report. Fact is that,
perhaps, for the hundredth time, directors have been
asked at what figure they will sell the mine. Every mme-
owner within cooee of Oobar is being pestered with a
similar question from all sorts and conditions of people.
The legion of commission touts in Sydney, assuming that
the Great Oobar deal is as good as completed, are working
their wits to get the right to dispose of other mines on
the field, without which, of course, Great Cobar will be
comparatively small potatoes. If the big mine be pur-
chased, and the new men decide that Chesney will be >
desirable property to acquire, they will not need to
trouble the commission hunters—they will get the mine
in their own way, on their own terms, and at their own
time.

Latest advices from Merton’s Reward state that
the lode, which seemed to be all over the shop at the
surface, is becoming more defined, but narrowing with
depth. Unfortunately there is a distinct falling-off in
in quality, and gold is no longer visible. Manager
Morgan, who was Callahan’s lieut. on L.V. Consols in the
boom days of ’99, is in charge, and preparing the property
for London market. Co. is already registered there, but
not yet floated. Battery will start almost immediately,
but will be hampered by scarcity of water. Of course, it
will treat nothing other than average stone —Kaufman
will see to that.

“ Mines Department Laboratory, Spencer-
street. —At the request of Mr. Ernest de Beer I conducted
a series of tests this day at 439 Bourke-street. The
results were in all respects highly satisfactory, the fur-
nace quickly coming to a working heat. Several assays
were performed, and various metals melted. Copper was
melted in less than three minutes, and also cast iron, and
no fluxes were used. This is sufficient evidence of the
capabilities of the furnace. —(Sgd.) P. G. W. Bayly,
As.S.A.S.M., Asst, to the Government Metallurgist, 9th
May, 1902.”*

Electricity in mining is being more extensively
adopted every day. The Austral Otis Engineering Com-
pany, of South Melbourne, the Australian representatives
of Ganz and Co., have just installed at the Great
Southern Consols mine, Rutherglen, a complete plant for
the underground haulage, doing away with the old costly
system of trucking. The work is now done by electric
locomotives at a much reduced cost; the whole of the
drives and surface works are also lighted from the same
installation. This company has also supplied electric
pumping plants to the Sons of Gwalia mine, and probably
the largest electric pumps in the world for the Charlotte
Plains and New Havilah mines at Maldon. Having
secured the services of a well-known European expert,
they are now prepared to advise, and contract for any
electrical service such as lighting, pumping, haulage,
traction, rock drilling, or the generation and transmission
of power by the latest and most approved methods, and
invite inquiries.*

develop well in the hands of tributors, to whom G. W.

Appendicitis, from which the well-plucked
King Edward is happily recovering, is, and has
for some tune been, a very fashionable disease,
r iiteen years ago it was practically unknown :
nowadays you 11 see an appendicitis joke in every
American comic paper, and there is a New York
club a condition of admittance to which is that
you ve been operated on for this complaint.
X here is more tuan a suspicion in the public mind
that there are more appendicitis operations than
appendicitis cases. Of course the public may not
be right. The King seems to be pulling round
quickly, and is now allowed to smoke—which is
strongly-reassuring news, both as regards the
condition of his digestion and of his heart.
* * * •
Apparently the crowd which hedges tiie
Throne tries to work the fiction that the King can
sutter no ill just as strenuously as the other fiction
that he can do no wrong. That H.M’s illness a
week before Coronation was serious was
shown by the sudden jump in insurance rates
from 4 to 21 per cent. ; but it was said to
be merely a slight attack of lumbago. News of
the seizure on Saturday, culminating in a fit, was
carefully suppressed. It was only when the
Coronation became impossible—when, in fact, but
for the operation, the King would soon have died—
that the truth was allowed to transpire. Even
now who knows whether we have got the whole
truth ?
A TEST QUESTION.
“Oh, yes, she's pretty, and knows a lot, but she
doesn't know everything
“ Does she know she's pretty? ”
Somewhat remarkable that both King
Edward and his elder sister, who died last year,
should have been stricken down prior to the
“grand climacteric” age of 63, after passing
through the fifties. It begins to look as though
none of Queen Victoria’s children inherited her
splendid constitution. They are not maintain-
ing her family tradition for longevity. George I.
was 67 when he died, George 11. was 77, grandson
George 111. was 82, and his sons, George IV. and
William IV.. were 68 and 72 respectively. They
drank port wine and took any amount of snuff,
but they didn’t smoke big, green cigars like King
Edward and his brother, the late Duke of Edin-
burgh.
* • • *
On Coronation Day the clergy in general
doctored their sermons round from the day of
feasting to the day of mourning, which was fitting
enough, and better, according to “the wisest man
that ever lived.” Now, whatever the Bible is, it is
not a Snob. It strips the King of all his trap-
pings. Its spirit is _ that of Carlyle when he
paints the King facing the void immensity.
Therefore, no preacher can give the Bible
away on this point. But how lamentably
the«e preachers do give themselves away ! The
bulk of them seemed to depict King Edward sis
ready to march, in his coronation robes, from one
drawing-room to another, from Westminster
Abbey into the empyrean! Pastor Fitchett
appears to be almost, the only clergyman reported
who dared, in explicit terms, to transfer Majesty
from Westminster to the surgeon’s table. Not
one preacher of the whole lot was faithful in pre-
senting the Almighty’s view of kingship. As is
shown in.the “ New Book of Kings,” the Almighty
believes in a Republic, but only concedes kings to
human weakness. When the Israelites demand a
king, they are told by Samuel that they are fools,
and all the tyranny the king will practise on them
(exactly as monarchs have done) is pointed out.
Where’s all this in the Australian preachers ?
* * * *
The true character of “ loyal ” official
“rejoicings” was shown in the fact that the men
who spend public money upon illuminations and
things in Australia (and elsewhere) were prepared
to perpetrate the Coronation shivoo even when
they knew the crowning was “ off,” and the Kin r
lay in the shadow of death. They had blewed
much cash and made a lot of arrangements for
pretending to be glad on their Sovereign Lord’s
account, so they were not prepared to substitute a
cup of sorrow for a quart of champagne at a
moment’s notice.
• • • *
The saddest holiday Sydney ever saw in
its life was that Coronation Day when there
was no Coronation. The few decorations—there
were very few at best—were dead. The shops
were mostly open, but doing no business. There
were prayers for the King’s recovery instead of
joy and drink and whoop. The people who had
arranged special functions had unsold food and
countermanded fowls left on their hands. The
children who had bought crackers either howled
because they had lost their firework display, or
else let them off in defiance, and were accused of
being glad that the King was i 1. .Flags were
dead stock. Country people who had “ come
down to see the Coronation ” —the country people
all talked as if the event were going to happen
locally—dragged “the missus” and “the kids”
aimlessly up and -down the streets. Barbers,
as they shaved people, talked drearily about
abscesses, or brought out and showed to
their customers that dreadful picture of bowels
which appeared in the evening paper. It was
“Hamlet” with everything left out except the
second grave-digger and the charge at the door.
It suggested a second-hand Sunday, or a birthday
feast without the guest or the victuals. Tie
weather was cold as the Private Secretary’s
feet, and grey and drizzly besides, and every-
thing was grey, like the weather, except the
public nose, which was blue, and the public
mackintosh, which was mostly a cheap, black
affair that had faded to green. The restaurant-
keepers, who had made preparations for an un-
usual multitude, reflected sorrowfully on the
unsold corned beef in the twilight. Almost
everything shut up early and went home to bed
in low spirits. But Premier John See’s new title
arrived through the gloom and the drizzle, so one
heart at least was glad, and there was joy in one
produce-store at all events.

There was a wild and woolly time
amongst the Melbourne unemployed and
the wharf-rats and Yarra-bank troglo-
dytes when beer and champagne were
distributed freely to the push and its
wives and aunts for the honor and
glory of King Ned. A brewing eo. pro-
vided six barrels of beer, and these
were tapped in a lane alongside
labor - leader Fleming’s boot shop.
The thirsty destitute were expected to
provide themselves with drinking uten-
sils, and they went, armed with cups, glasses,
' mugs, bottles, jam-tins, pannikins, billies, jugs,
and tea and coffee pots. One lady enthusiast
with a large thirst and great foresight even carried
a kerosene-tin. The police formed the applicants
into line, and marched them past the barrels, each
gettinfea pint as he or she toed scratch. Then
the refreshed ones made a mad break down the
lane and around the block, and chipped in at the
tail of the procession again. This march was
sustained till there was an endless chain of
boisterous drunks rotating round that block, and
then the beer-seekers began to despise order.
By then the legitimate unemployed were right
out of it, and the draggle-tailed Totties and the
wharf-rats and chronic sots were in full possession.
It simply rained beer in that lane. The drunks
waded in it and filled themselves beyond their
test register. The lady who had clung to her
kerosene-tin was now rewarded, and got away
with it three-parts full. When the confusion was
at its highest the police charged, re-captured the
barrels, took possession of the lane, and cut off
supplies, and the loyal demonstration was at an
end. There has not been so indecent a display of
loyalty in Melbourne since the Duke was there.
Fleming distributed 112 quart bottles of Lord
Hopetoun’s champagne at the same time, but this
was done with better judgment, and the wine got
into the hands of the legitimate unemployed.
Fleming argued that it did not necessarily require

Who was really responsible for the Hope-
toun shout of champagne to the Melb. unemployed ?
Fancy filling empty stomachs with a high-
voltage stimulant—the suddenness of the drunk,
and, alas, its brevity ! Better to have given them
good solid beef and beer, especially in this cold
weather. It was Hopetoun’s wine, but was it
Hopetoun’s folly to decree that the wine itself,
and not the value of it, should go to the hungry ?
» * * •
Suggested by one correspondent that Hope-
toun’s champagne for the Melbourne unemployed
was a satire, or a grim joke, or something, for the
politicians who drank his free wine so long by
“kidding” him that they were going to raise his
screw. Whatever it was, joke or gibe, Hopetoun
ought to explain. His humor, if it was meant as
humor, needs an interpreter.
* • • •
In Victoria the proposed “ Celebration ”
of the King's crowning mostly panned out in the
country districts to a matter of a bun and a medal
for the kiddies. The grocer’s idea of complete
joy—a full stomach and a tin gaud ! One Shire
Council (Fern Tree Gully) struck a sweet novelty
by bestowing medals on old age pensioners—just
as in Hingland they dress Charity-school children
in a special rig-out, so that they shall never be
mistaken for ordinary youngsters.
* * • *
Tom by conflicting emotions of pseudo-
loyalty and thrift, Victoria made a first-class ass
of itself over its Coronation celebrations. First
the Federal Government, the State ditto and the
Gov. -Gen. decided to drop the illuminations and
go ahead with such bun-scrambles as were pro-
posed. Next they reckoned that, as the flags and
flare ups were all ready, it would be a pity to
waste them, so Thursday’s papers announced that
the holiday would be kept, and fireworks and
review would go ahead as intended. Then some-
body experienced a rush of com monsense, and
realised that as the King was lying on what might
prove to be his death-bed, and that as there might
never be any coronation, it would be the acme of
bad taste to light up and rejoice. Even then the
feeble “authorities” hadn’t strength enough to
act “on their own,” but must needs cable to
Chamberlain, who promptly replied : “All fes-
tivities postponed ; you should adopt a similar
course.” This news arrived shortly before noon,
when most of the populace had taken up good
positions for the review, and of course there
wasn’t any review or illuminations, and most of
the crowd passed the wasted holiday in discussing
the King’s illness and looking vacantly at the
Yarra. But at least one individual, the Mayor of
Prahran, celebrated the occasion by sticking to
his programme and holding his annual ball on
Thursday evening ! It hasn’t transpired yet
whether Poet Fitzgibbon, Sympathiser in Chief
to the Royal Families of Europe, has slaughtered
this Mayor. Probably he will slay him with a
poem.

Governor Tennyson had a big day in
Adelaide on (what should have been) Coronation
Day. After putting in the morning at State
affairs, he drove round to all the suburban
recreation-grounds where children were being
fited. These lie literally miles apart in a kind of
circle, and the outlying ones had not expected
him, the feat being considered impossible. But
he did the round, and got back to the Central
Oval in time for the second half of the inter-State
football match (on which game the whole Tennyson
family is particularly keen), finish in- the day at
the intercession service at the Cathedral ! He
further announced that, barring unfavorable news
of the King, he would be pre-
sent, as arranged, at the speci-
ally - commanded “Tw e 1 fth
Night” performance on Fri-
day. and the S. AJ.C. races
on Saturday. A good all-round
man.

The butting of N.S.W.
Judges against juries, now quite
a fashionable entertainment
with the minor Bench, will, if
continued much longer, goad
Parliament into making a use-
ful example. A dismissed
Judge, it is to be borne in mind,
loses his title to a pension. An
old proverb says, “A fool, un-
less he knows the law, is never
a great fool.”
• • •
The noble family of Aber-
gavenny, represented in Aus-
tralia by Lord Richard Nevill,
seems to have a peculiar lean-
ing to the Law Courts. Lady
Cowley (a sister of Lord “Dick”
aforesaid), whose divorce from
her first husband was obtained
by herself, has chosen to retain
the title after her second mar-
riage, and last month she
scored some further notoriety
by bringing a libel action
against a little rag that stig-
matised her claim to the re-
pudiated Cowley’s title as “a
contemptible piece of snob-
bery.” The fair plaintiff told
the Court that, inasmuch as
her son by the first husband
would become Earl Cowley if
he lived long enough, she
wished him to know his mother
always as Countess Cowley
a reasonable answer to the
charge of “snobbery.” The
jury gave her a verdict but
showed their opinion as to the
necessity for dragging such a
case into court by awarding
her a paltry £lOO damages.
British juries are slowly losing
their reverence for the offspring
of noble marquises, and sich.
Here was a Lady, with a birth-
title in the first place, and a
dissolved - marriage title in
the second place, seeking cash
solace for a newspaper’s insult
to her family pride, and the jury assessed the
damage at £lOO. Twenty years ago she would
have got twenty times as much.
* * . *
The administration of the South Aus-
tralian Public Service has nearly always been
weak ; wherever possible a mechanical system has
been adopted. The fact is, little South Australia,
living so much by itself, is mainly its own cousin,
and mechanical promotions, &c., have had the
recommendation of raising the. least dust. As the
naturally pushful young man is thus discouraged
from pushing, lie doesn’t do it (certainly there is
not much ahead worth pushing for); and so this
service sleeps sounder than any other on the main-
land. Even in its scheme of retrenchment it is
mechanical—it just lops off all heads over 70. So,
too, in its appointment of Inspector-General of
fechools, deferred for five or six years, the easy
mechanical prooess has triumphed at last, and the
K? 1 ?* lns pector has been put up. At least two
Ministers have wanted to appoint a younger and
more vigorous man, but—it was South Australia !

A N.S. W. civil servant:—
Re the difficulty the Vic. authorities have in filling
goodpewtaorui from the Departments under present rules
JN.&.W. is rapidly getting as bad. The prizes in the
service are few, in comparison to the number of officers,
ana day by day promotion grows slower. Go from the
start; Examinations are held to test the eligibility of
candidates. The successful ones are appointed at, say,
±5O. Promotion to £l2O is fairly rapid. The brake is
then put on and it takes 20 years to reach £3OO. There
the civil servant must stop. Parliament declines to allow
an increase over this figure unless by its own vote and,
before he can reach £4OO, the public servant must have
luck, opportunity, ability (perhaps) and the eir of his
chief, or the man who runs his chief, besides having
to catch the Assembly (with a strong Minister) in a com-
plaisant mood. Then he might be told that there is no
money to grant increases, and he must await more pros-
perous times. Summed up, this means that one per cent,
will get past £3OO out of all those who enter the service
pj competitive examination. Dismissal from the service
18 a:r unknown, unless one is a hardened criminal,
and the only way to a step forward is over the corpse of
the man in front. And the man in front is always a first-
class life in the A.M.P. If new billets are created, the
chances are that a political outsider will get them,
therefore, it is not to be wondered at that the service is
being lowered to a dull level of mediocrity, and that it
will soon be hopeless to expect to find men of talent when
required.
S. Australia's retrenchment scheme dis-
closes the fact that at the Govt, printory an old
lady of 73 still draws her 6s. a day as a bookbinder.
Ihe record for the office, though, is held by a
compositor who at 77 still hangs on to his “ case ”
Ihe biggest sacrifices will be in the office of the
.Registrar-General, where the three tallest heads
are struck off; and in the police, which loses its
four senior inspectors, though not its chief.

The Commonwealth Postal Dept, finds
that the annual output of blank telegraph forms
gratis far exceeds, legitimate public needs. The
latest official idea is to charge a shilling for each
book of 100 forms supplied to business firms—
the people who are least likely to use them for
scribbling paper. Considering that the most
habitual wasters of telegraph-office stationery are
the men who shake ” forms by the handful from
the counters and tables where they lie loose, a
better rule would be to exact payment for all of
them—ls per 100 in the book, or Id. for six loose
ones. But what does the yearly leakage of
telegraph forms amount to, anyhow?

Published in Sydney Prince Alfred Hos-
pital annual report (what had been carefully kept
from the public) that there was a big shortage in
the late secretary’s accounts —reduced by sums
recovered to £1034 14s. 2d. The ex-sec. has since
died. As is usual, “he enjoyed the full con-
fidence,” &c. They always do. A man whose
accounts are drifting on to rocks is not likely to
show in his face what his figures would tell if
they were properly investigated. It’s books that
call for auditing, not conversations.

Missioner Geil advertised in the Adelaide
papers, re a ladies’ meeting, thusly : “ Spots !
bring Bibles and butter-dishes.” When the
women had assembled the hall took on the appear-
ance of an olla podrida of butter-dishes and Bibles.
The suspense was tremendous as the attendants
handed round black spots gummed on one side
and poured water into the butter-dishes. In the
midst of a vicious silence Geil told the ladies to
open at a certain chapter and affix the black spots
so that they could find the place again, advising
those who did not like to lick the spots to dip
them in the butter-dishes. That was all the joke.
It wasn’t much of a joke either. A spasm of
disappointment ran through the audience causing
the water to spill over its dress, and there was
much internal anguish, for there is amongst the
ladies more wailing over one spoilt dress than re-
joicing over 10,000 saved souls. Geil, as a
humorist, fell to zero over the silly butter-dish
funnyism. The woman who had carried a dish
two miles in great bewilderment merely to have
water poured into it, so that she could wet a
kind of stamp and stick it on Geil’s text, in case
she wanted to look at the text again, only
regarded herself as having been taken in by a
rather dull April-foolish ness.

Instead of a balance-sheet, the Salvation
Army in N.S.W. publishes the “fact” that
nearly 2,000,000 meals and 10,000 beds were pro-
vided last year ; fallen women to the number of
3000 rescued, and half of them converted ; also
800 discharged prisoners were taken into the
Home, and more than half of them brought to the
repentance-bench and put into billets. Butf.why
not let us know what money was made out of the
work done by all these people variously dealt
with ? It would be too much to ask what becomes
of the converts. If the woman of the street
is converted at the rate of 1500 a year, she ought
shortly to become extinct. See the city any night
of the year and judge whether that is so !
• * * *
“ Alpar ” : All male N.S.W. State school
teachers are now being drilled on Saturdays, with
the idea, I’m told, of attaining a uniform standard
of proficiency in all schools. Hitherto, drill has
been a “patchy” subject; but better things are
looked for after the teachers —some of them raw
recruits—have had a course of 16 drills. These
men give up their Saturdays for the benefit of the
children in their respective districts ; and some
of them think it hard that they should have to
pay train and other expenses incidental to attend-
ing at centres of instruction. One mart pays
3s. 3d. weekly for fare alone. That —to a battler
to live and look decent —is a consideration.
* * *
An English paper tells that in an English
public school, near London, a suggestion that the
children should sing the National Gorsave revealed
the startling fact that none of them knew it.
Further inquiry spread over 72 children only
unearthed ten who had heard of the Anthem. The
time is really coming when Seddon will have to
declare war against England for disloyalty to
itself.
T’other day a depressed stranger entered
the (office of the Vic. Under Treasurer, remark-
ing that he had £3 on his conscience, and paid it
in. Noteworthy that no one ever offers to give
the State more than £2 or £3 for an easy con-
science. According to Vic. figures the average
easy conscience is worth 275. 6d., and no man has
yet wanted an easy conscience so badly as to be
willing to give £lO for it.
* * * *
The 50th anniversary of the Gundagai
(N.S.W.) flood, that swept away 70 buildings and
drowned about 90 persons, fell on June 26, and,
in the absence of the Coronation, gave the local
residents an absorbing theme for talk.
* * • *
The anniversary of the burning of St.
Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney, on June 29, excited
plenty of reminiscences, but, like its predecessors,
failed to fix for certain whether or not an aged
man, once the Government hangman, was con-
sumed in the fire. Tradition has it that he dwelt
in a cellar or cloister in the basement, and perished
in the flames. The fire occurred in 1865.
An application was recently made to
N.S.W. .Reg.-General for the appointment of
a Guardian of Minors for the district of Casino
(to give consent to the marriage of an orphan
girl who was under age). The reply was
W. O. Bundock of Wiangarie, George K. Johnson
of Cannonbar and James Bray, Police-Magistrate,
are all Guardians of Minors in the district of Casino.
The humor lies in the fact that Mr. Bundock died
about four years ago, Mr. Bray about 12 years ago,
while Mr. Johnson is believed to be also dead. At
all events he’s not now known in Casino district.
This should take the bun for official ineptitude.
N.S.W. Governor Rawson, eager to seek
the bubble reputation at the common mouth,
didn’t funk it, last Saturday, when he found that
he had to share the opening ceremony of Pyrmont
Bridge with a breezy, horny-handed crowd. There
was no exclusive dais, no vice-regal frill arranged
for ; Admiral Rawson had to push and shove with
the rest of them. But he didn’t seem to mind. It
was the reporters of the daily press who suffered
most from shocked dignity, and the representa-
tive of one solemn paper was only comforted when
he found that the broad and generous back which
he had been using as a “rest ” for his shorthand-
book was (solemn fact) the Governor’s. When
his Excellency was unconsciously subjected
to sucli indignity, he thought he might
put up with a little jostling. Even Lady
Rawson didn’t escape from an occasional shove.
When jolly-dog Rawson rose to harangue (that’s
the most suitable word) the people he called for
three cheers for the contractor, and one bloke
promptly corrected him: “ No—cheers for the
King first”—and the Governor apologised and
stood corrected. It was, on the whole, the most
off-hand vice-regal “function” that N.S.W. has
seen for a long while, and if Governor Rawson
would like to go into Parliament, he’s a dead bird
for one of the waterside labor constituencies.
