A pathetically humorous tribute to the
great god Respectability turned up in Melbourne
t’other day, when a young mother was shown to
have abandoned the infant which had been born
in wedlock, but had arrived rather too soon after
the ceremony. She and the father had been
engaged for years, and she could not face the
disgrace of her own parents learning the truth.
The court fined her £5.

A much-married Teutonic
gentleman who has an inordinate
desire for the society of single ladies
of tender age, made an assignation
with a girl whom he had long be-
sieged. The girl’s big brother (a tram
conductor) also turned up shortly
after, and gave Emperor Bill’s sub-
ject the father of a hiding in the inky
darkness. Next day and the next the
Eursuer of virtuous maids stayed at
ome. The following morning saw
him in the usual place tramming it
to town, with heavy dark goggles over
his eyes. “ Morning, sir ! ” remarked
the conductor; “bad eyes? ” “ Yes, I
gannot stand ze glare; in fact, the
Australian sun iz too sdrong for my
eyesight.” “Yes,” replied the conductor ; “it’s
pretty warm, even taken the skin off my knuckles,
too ! ”

The Sydney jewellery firm of Fairfax and
Roberts recently manufactured a bishop’s staff in
solid silver, and the object was alluded to with
great gravity in the local press. What a bishop
wants with this mighty prong of metal is not very
clear, but it is certainly cjuite unsuited to Aus-
tralia. An Australian bishop’s staff should be
short and manageable. There should be a thick
end, which he can use, if he is a city bishop, to
keep off the push if it besets him when he is re-
turning late from seeing a sick supporter, and a
whistle let into it so that he can call a shilling—
not more than a shilling—cab. The other end
should be a spike, so that he can use it, if he is a
country bishop and gets bushed in a dry spot, to
dig for water (the country bishop can also use the
thick end to keep off wild dogs). And there
should be a kind of hook with which to hang the
property up on a nail, for, if it is leaned up against
a corner, it is apt to slide down heavily on the
floor in the night, and disturb the episcopal
slumbers. This is a practical country, and old-
world frills and ornaments are out of place.

N.S.W. Comptroller General Neitenstein
submits his annual prisons report, which is not
quite such dolorous reading as it used to be. The
“inside” population is decreasing (though that
is probably due to the increase in the number
of criminals that escape arrest), and the inside
horrors-—dark cells, bridles, gags, cats, &c. —have
almost ceased to be. Two points stand out
demanding attention. First, the one-sided
arrangement whereby the Victorian discharged
criminal gets a chance in N.S.W., while the
N.S. W. man found the other side of the Murray
within three years of his discharge is liable to 12
months in irons. Common sense and common
humanity should end that. Second is the number
of prisoners held in default of fines. A small
instalment of a time-payment system has enabled
1204 to buy themselves out, and save the State
keep and custody aggregating 42 years—value not
less than £2OOO. If all that is practicable were
done in that way those figures might be much
increased. In Darlinghurst 80 per cent, of the
prisoners are held in default of fines !

“Domainiac” sends along a solid bit of
philosophy for use by the social rank and file :
YVas recently sitting: on a bench in
Hyde Park, Sydney, when a dilapi-
dated female of the “ Jiggetty Jane”
type planted herself alongside of me.
Said she, after staring'at me for a few
moments with her bleary, blood-
shot eyes, “You’re a respectable
married man, I s’pose?” “Very
much so, madam,” I answered,
for safety—and hurriedly rose to
depart. “Stop!” said the
woman hurriedly, as she jerked
me back on the seat by the tail
of my coat. “ I only want to
give you a bit of advice. I
don’t want anything of
you. I couldn’t swallow a
drink just now for a for-
tune. But I like j'our face,
and if you’re really married
take my advice, sonny, and
never take in lodgers!
Look at me—the pretty
picture that I am ! —and
know that lodgers have
done it all! I was married
and lived happy till my
husband took in a lodger.
This was a quiet, hard-
working chap, and he and I
became first friends, then
lovers, and—well, familiarity
breeds consent. No sooner
had I taken the wrong step
when I became sorry and was
pleased when the lodger sud-
denly departed. My husband
had suspected nothing, and I
was determined to go straight
for the future. But, finding the
7s. 6d. handy on rent day, my hus-
band, despite my remonstrances,
took in another lodger. No. 2 w f as
a hard, cynical, sneering man, and
at first I disliked him, but the
lodger-habit had a strong grip on
me, and in less than a month’s
time he had re-placed No. 1.
He treated me entirely different, and
abused me awfully as soon as he got me into his power.
Unexpectedly, one night, lodger No. 1 returned, and,
despite my hitter protests, was duly accommodated by
my husband with the only other spare room. Dodging
my husband had been hard work at times, but now I had
to dodge lodger No. 1 when I was with No. 2, No. 2 when
with No. 1, and the husband when with either. This
nearly drove me mad, and I took to drink. The two
lodgers were the first to get an inkling of the state of
affairs. They became fearfully jealous of each other, and
finally each put my husband on his guard against the
other. My husband watched, found me out and kicked
me into the street. I appealed singly to the lodgers, but
both turned dog, and refused to assist me either with
money or advice. Neither of the wretches would buy me
a drink to-day if I was walking on my tongue. So take
my advice, young fellow, and keep clear of lodgers.” She
rose and staggered away with the air of one who has dis-
charg'd a sacred duty.
WRONG CONCLUSIONS.
BOWLER : “If yer can afford to dress yer girl in
costly furs yer can afford to ’elp a yore bloke
what’s in trouble.”
TOPPER: “If you can afford to smoke cigars you
can afford other things.”
BOWLER : “It 'was guv me, governor.”
TOPPER : “ Well, it isn’t my girl.”
So far as N.S.W. census papers show,
something like every eighth man and woman is
away from home on any particular night. (It is
assumed that census-night was an average night).
The ordinary fool who doesn’t know much about
those things would not expect that the figures for
the sect of the Calithumpians would differ much
from those of the United Mumbo-Jumbos : but
they do. Of the big sects the R.C. apparently
lets his wife go holidaying oftenest ; the C. of E.
and the Presbyterians stick to the average. Only
one in every nine Baptist wives was away, and one
in every 13 Congregationalists. Of “ Free-
thinkers, Agnostics,” &c., one in five ; of other
non-Christians no less than 579 in 857 —which
perhaps means that it’s easier to describe yourself
as a non-Christian when your wife is away from
home than when she isn’t. Without Coghlan’s
latest N.S.W. census figures most people
would probably have thought that, of all
the religious sects, the Jews would show the
smallest percentage of mixed martiages. As a
matter of fact, they do take this place some
of the way. Of 889 Rachels and Rebekahs who
were at home on census night, Isaac and Jacob
watched over 781 of them —that is a bigger pro-
portion than any other “church” shows. But, on
the other hand, Isaac and Jacob have been sad
wanderers; possibly the supply of Rachels and
Rebekahs has, at some time or other, run short.
Anyhow, there were 1034 Jewish husbands whose
wives were at home on census night, and. as said
just now, only 781 Jewish spouses among
them ; so that every fourth Jew has a Christian
wife, while only one in every eight Jewish wivis
has a Christian husband. Using plain figures,
one Church of England wife in every seven has
some other brand of husband ; after them, come
the Lutherans (where a common taste in religion
has, perhaps, less to answer for than a common
taste in cookery), the Methodists, Congrega-
tionalists, Presbyterians, and Unitarians, the last
two about one in five each. In spite of all the
whipping-in, the R.C.’s are low down the list; a
limb or two more than one in every four wives in
the faith is yoked with an unbeliever. On the
other hand, less than one in every six R.C. hus-
bands has some other sort of wife. Right down at
the bottom of the pit are the Salvarmies —only
1078 red guernseys among the husbands of 1439
women who wear the blue bonnet of a shrieking
life.

To great men about to die.—There are
literary executors and literary executioners.
THE HEART’S DUNGEONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In many a heart is a chamber
That with cobweb-curtained door
Dumb, forgotten, keeps its secret
Since the key was lost of yore.
But one day when skies seem clearest.
Falls a flash that lights and burns
On the key that lies long-hidden . . .
How the hinge groans as it turns !
Sad reproachful eyes of beauty
Still unrighted in its wrong—
Threatening, leering, mocking faces—
Through the narrow doorway throng.
For the heart has Bastille-dungeons
That love not the honest day ;
Well it is that Keeper Memory
Doth so oft his keys mislay.

Sydney is very full of sheep-men here for the
Show and the sales. When the State Governor
goes hence he will bear away a large pastoral
education acquired on the spot. The effect of the
times was put so straightl.v to him by Mr. Suttor,
the President, at the opening of the Show, that his
bushy eyebrows fairly waggled with sympathy.
In 91 our flocks numbered 62,000,000 sheep ; at
the end of ’Ol there were under 41,000,000, and
within two years the value of wool sold in
Australia dropped from £13,000,000 to about
£6,000,000.” Afterwards the Gov. put blue
ribbons round the necks of the champion ewe and
ram, made some more speeches, and looked awhile
at the sheep-dog trials. The dogs had to drive
three sheep round two flag-posts, through a hurdle,
round a third post, twice though a maltese cross,
and then into a pen in lOmin. All of which has a
tendency to spoil good dogs by encouraging them
to waste time in fancy evolutions instead of taking
the sheep the shortest cut to the tank. Anyhow,
neither the President or the Gov. could have done
it.
A fascinating American book, “ Wild Animals
I Have Known,” says: “ The dog’s nose tells him
what the clubman’s eye learns when he looks over
the. club register.” So when the sheep-dog
Biddy ’ strolled out into the paddock, sniffing
the tracks of collies which were at previous trials,
she was thinking “So that ‘ Darkie ’ is down here,
is he. Br-r-r-r, wait till I settle with him ! ” She
nosed some more, then looked up saying, “and
here’s that cur ‘ Boon’s ’ tracks. Smelling of
bullock’s liver, too. I didn’t get anv liver; I owe
her an ear-chewing. Perhaps we’ll go home in
the same dog-box, then I’ll pay. M’Gracious !
why Bros’. ‘ Needle ’ is in town ; I knew
her mother, and one of her fools of sons.” Biddy
and Darkie took prizes.
To console His Ex. for the many disappoint-
ments in ball-going the Minister for Works
thoughtfully provided him, on Saturday, with a
bridge to open. New Pyrmont Bridge (Sydney)
gives one the loveliest swing—much better than a
gate. A man presses a button and it swings
round and back in 44sec. I’d like to hire it, and
swing all day—especially if the persons I don’t
like were waiting to cross. It will never bear a
heavier load than it carried last Saturday, when
nearly all adult Pyrmont, with considerable of
the infancy-in-arms, was crowded on to it. There
was a lot of human nature in the men of the
crowd, for the lo ’dest and heartiest cheers were
given for Miss Rawson.
The little vice-regal dais was quickly swamped
by youths with handkerchiefs knotted on their
necks, and damsels (the serrated edges of whose
wide-brimmed sailor hats rasped the hair-nets off
the invited women guests, and abraded the necks
and cheeks of the vice-regal women). They were
microscopically observant, these invaders, and
very frank. One named Florrie secured a capital
position by shifting the Minister’s daughter
with her elbows and saw-brim, and unselfishly
shared the knowledge thus acquired with one Bill,
who was in the back ground. “ The Governor’s
coat ’as a little ladder of black cotton ’ook ’oles
down ther left front side, Bill.” “ Has it ! ” said
Bill, interestedly; “ that must be ter ’ang ’is
medals an’orders on.” “ You’d think,” continued
Florrie, “ that he’d ’ave a second coat without
them loops fer dyes like this. Bill, tell Nellie
that Lydy Rawsing has a bangle with a gold acorn
and a watch, and a lucky pig on it.” “I’ll pass
my knife along ter you,” said Bill, “an’ yer get a
bit o’ the blue ribbon when she cuts it.” So Florrie
shoved a few more guests aside, and, almost before
Lady Rawson had severed the broad ribbon, had
cut half-a-foot of it with Bill’s jack-knife, and
pinned it over her chest.
On behalf of the contractors for the bridge Mrs.
O’Sullivan was presented with a splendid diamond
and sapphire pendant, and Pyrmont demanded
that it be “ parssed round.” Bill and Nellie were
displeased that Florrie’s aunts on the outskirts of
the crowd couldn’t have a look. The Minister for
Works himself got a pair of gold mounted spec-
tacles “ to enable him to see his way to build more
bridges.” George Dibbs spoke and presented
Lady Rawson with a cup which he had carved
from one of the piles of the old bridge. A similar
one was handed to Wor's. The cups’were like
chalices, highly brilliantiued, and carried inscrip-
tions on silver shields.

The wail of a woman student: Many of the
women graduates are dissatisfied with the con-
dition attached to the newly-inaugurated Queen
Victoria Memorial Scholarship for girls. The
condition is that the winner, if possessed of suf-
ficient private means to defray her University ex-
penses, must hand over the money-portion of the
prize to a poorer competitor, thus reducing the
Q-V.S. to the anomalous position of being a
scholarship, in theory and a bursary in practice.
The two things are not by any means synonymous.
Each, it is true, is equally a sum of money given
to a student to be spent on a Uni. career, but
there the resemblance ends. A scholarship is an
honor publicly awarded to only conspicuous merit,
while a bursary is merely financial assistance
awarded to those applicants who show “ sufficient
promise of benefiting by a University course,” and
who may, and in some cases do, hold the lowest
places on the entrance lists. Prof. MacCallum,
the only speaker at the inaugural meeting who went
into the subject in any detail, remarked that no uni-
versity had yet tried to combine these systems.
But he might have gone a little further and asked
the Q.V.S. Committee to try the experiment on
something smaller first. Other speakers alluded
in touching. terms to the poor girl of striking
genius who is at present excluded from ’varsity
advantages by reason of her poverty. They forgot
or didn’t know the fact that each’ year four Govt,
bursaries (fees, books, and £2O per year to town
and £5O to country students) are awarded to girls
who have attended public schools for a year pre-
vious to exam., while girls from outside schools
have always enjoyed a very fair share of the 11
University bursaries annually awarded. The
matric. records for the last ten years show that
the girls who have taken only third class
honors in.this exam, would have been provided for
by the existing bursaries even if each one of them
needed such help.
You will see Max Schluter’s photo, this week ;
he is a young Danish violinist, a pupil of Joachim
and Emanuel Wirth. He will play before the
Gov.-General, Governor Rawson, family and
suites at Cranbrook next week, and later in
public. At Paling’s, last week, he gave a recital,
and with the passionate grief of his violin in the
“ Chanson Triste ” wrung the heart from everyone
who heard. Then he flashed through a brilliant
composition of his own. His soprano supporter
will be handsome and tuneful Mrs. Gilbert
Wilson, daughter of the evergreen Mrs. Palmer
(Carandini), who has been living in Brisbane
almost since her marriage.

All the illustrated papers are reproducing por-
traits of Queen Alexandra as she was when she
married, as she was when carrying Prince George
pick-a-back; as she was 25 or 30 years ago, and as
she is (said to be) to-day. The contrast shows the
immense progress made by photography. The
Queen of 30 years, ago is an ordinarily-pretty,
rather inane-faced girl. To-day, a woman of over
50, she is (pictorially) absolutely beautiful; and no
older than she looks in those old-fashioned but
probably correct prints.
Miss Margaret Flockton, the Sydney artist, has
managed to get at least four Australian flower-
spirits to reveal themselves to her. The American
Tobacco Co. is issuing reproductions of her panel-
paintings of the waratah, the broad-leafed wattle,
the native fuschia, and the honey-flower. The
work is beautifulj each separate flower seems to
say, “ Verily, it is I.” The reproductions are the
most charming things of their kind yet issued in
Australia, and every household of artistic tastes
should acquire a set, to frame and hang up so that
they may be a joy for ever.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The hardest-worked official of that luckless
non-Coronation Day was Better-Not-Wally. He
had only half recovered from a chill to the bones
himself, and was kept croaking explanations,
orders, information, particulars, to all and sundry
through the telephone from early dawn to foggy
dark that Thursday. The cancelling of the
Governor-General’s evening party was the cruellest
blow of all. It was only at mid-day that the
scratching was communicated to official folk.
Then society people kept a-ting-a-linging to the
weary Wally all day long for news, and yet in the
end there were many with cabs ordered and hair
in crimp at 7 o’clock, setting about togging up for
the party.
The dress-builders have made their pile. They
had been cajoled and harassed during the last
week by guests invited to the two V.R.
junkets. No expense was to be spared. The
Melbourne lady - upholsterers took the hint,
and piled on stitchings, flutings, pleatings,
tuckings. And now the chiffon triumphs—the
treasured toilettes precious with laborious effect—
lie in lavender till the King gets quite well and
the day of rejoicing wheels round again. Great is
the disappointment of the girls who were booked
to debut at the cancelled evening party; they
aim. ; st certainly will not get the chance now of
making an entry into society under the eyes of the
Governor-General.
“At a recent tea-scramble, a fortune-teller’s
tent was the attraction of the day. A strange
feature of the rush was the number of married
women (fat and old, too) who sought to approach
the oracle. Girls, bubbling over with excitement
to know when their dark or fair ‘ Fate ’ was timed
to arrive, and other future happenings, were left
out in the cold, while the old women scrambled
in and out of the tent, with toques askew
and tongues busy recounting the bliss (?) the
gods had in store for them. ‘There’s Mrs. Toney,’
said one irritable miss ‘ with seven children,
having her hand read ! Hasn’t she had enough
fortune by this?’ But the aged mother of seven
evidently wanted the mysterious veil still further
lifted on her already crowded life. The seer plays
on the vanities of womankind in an almost in-
describable way. The uglier the client, the more
ready she is for the five shillings’ worth of palpi-
tating, impatient Romeos provided for her. The
fat and forty matron (her removed ring leaves a
tell-tale dint on the index-finger) glows with
pleasure as she is told of the young man with dark,
classical ringlets who will win her love—with
pleasure at the thought that her charms still
warrant such a prophecy, and also with some of
the vague, expectant trembling of the girl who
watches the East, half hoping, half fearing to see
uprise there a rose-oloud bodeful of the dawn of
Love.
‘‘The daughters of Senators and Reps, from other
States are almost unanimous in declaring that
they will be sorry when the session ends, as they
are having a real good time in Melb. The local
grand dames aren’t too warmly enthusiastic in their
treatment of the inter-State girls, especially dames
with girls of their own to marry off. But fickle
man is attracted by fresh faces, and male atten-
tion makes up for much female snubbing. At
afternoon parties, receptions, and such I have
seen some very pronounced cold-shouldering. But
the Federal misses always came up smiling.

days which intervalled her return from Adelaide
and her second set-out for England. Mrs. George
Chirnside, Lady Hutton, and many other spark-
ling matrons feted the horsey dame right royally.
Mrs. Corbet is making the trip ’Ome this time in

“Descendants of Westralia’s ‘Six Families’
mourn the untimely taking off of Premier Leake,
whose grandfather and granduncle were amongst
the most respected of W.A. pioneers. Subse-
quent Leakes have maintained the social prestige
of the clan, and have intermarried with tne Lee-
Steeres, the Brouns, and the Stones. The late
Premier’s grandmother, Mrs. Luke Leake (mother
of Sir Luke of that ilk), was a prominent identity
in the highest circles of old-time Sandgroperdom.
A beauty in her youth, the Junoesque dame
retained her social knack through thick and thin,
even when facing the music as a large-sized widow
with a big family of frisky boys to educate on
small means. The other five 4 Families ’ appre-
ciated cheery Mrs. Leake so highly that her
memory is green to this day—3o years after the
echoes of the laughter aroused by her ‘unex-
pectedness’ have died away. Soft-hearted
‘dads,’ given to ‘ sparing the rod and spoiling the
child ’ in their own households, used to get politely-
worded notes, presenting Mrs. Leake’s compli-
ments, and ‘ would Mr. So-and-So kindly visit her
at his earliest convenience for the purpose of
chastising her fatherless boys.’ Westralians of
that period never dreamt of declining the hest of
a lady, and a widow at that, but their ohivalry
galled them badly at such times, especially as the
‘ fatherless boys ’ began to grow big and re-
bellious.
“A huge crowd farewelled the Runic (White
Star line) on Saturday. A jaunt ’Ome for £2O or
so has attractions even for a section of the
‘ Naicest,’ and, as passenger lists are not pub-
lished, sensitive travellers are not given away as
‘cheap-trippers.’ Mrs. Andrew Rowan, two of
her daughters, Lady Madden and ‘the Ruby,’
farewelled a young relative on Saturday. Tne
Chief Justicess looks 10 years younger since her
retirement from the vice-regal pinnacle. She
laughs quite merrily now that ‘Gor-Save’ has
had time to wear off her nerves. Her daughter,
too, appeared literally shining with health that
day on the pier.
“ Some day there’ll be an awful accident on an
out-going ship. Only one gangway for serried
masses of people, converging from all parts of the
vessel to reaon the pier while gongs sound din-
ningly * all ashore, ’ and in the thick of the fuss a
procession of men, carrying mail-bags, force their
way on board in the teeth of the would-be dis-
embarkers. First time a woman faints in the
crush, or a pickpocket starts a scare, a mass of
‘weaker vessels ’ will be hustled over the side.
“Lady Hutton is one of the few women in
Australia who always greet the Gov.-General
with a profound courtesy. Whether on Fleming-
ton terrace or in a ball-room, the fair-haired dame
bobs in approved maid-of-honor style to His Ex.
This amiable lady is constantly observed at social
shivoos prompting her hubby to go and speak to
someone or other. The swearing Chief forgets
names, faces, and social obligations, but his
thoughtful and sweet-tempered partner prods him
ever so gently in the ribs, and bids him to go and
say a word to Miss Blank. ‘ Who ? ’ queries the
warrior with the fret-work face. ‘ Miss Blank,’
whispers his dame. ‘Do I know her ? ’ ‘ Yes, of
course you do—the Blanks of Orrong Road.’ Then
the Chief squares his shoulders, swaggers along,
and does the correct thing, while his blonde half
nods her pleasure. Big-Chief Hutton relates the
incident, and Miss Blank is convulsed.
“At the postponed tea-party of President
Wrixon and his wife, at ‘ Raheen, George Jenkins
and others of his ilk were not able to sport their
knee-breeches and laces, so that, to them, the
show was shorn of its finest features. The
Governor Clarkes were the special guests, and for
them a chorus of the programmed singers
warbled several verses of a National hymn.
The benevolent Miss Wrixon, who wears a
halo of the good Samaritan about her bare
and lofty brow, was diamondless and sheen-
less _ in black, and looked like a convent
novice amid much worldly vanity and pomp. The
Surple and Ethiopian garments of Archbishop
arr caught the breeze at ‘ Raheen ’ like angel
wings, and carfied their wearer in full sail around
the rooms. Dean Vance, gaitered and long-coated,
was another staid divine, while the brother
of Lady Janet Clarde (Rev. Evelyn Snodgrass)
played the part generally set down in fiction for
the genial and popular curate. Some of the finest
women in the crowd were Mrs. Arthur Moule,
Mrs. Norman Armytage, and Mrs. Donald
Mackinnon. The most remarkable frock of the
occasion was sported by the grey-haired Miss
‘ Millie’ Carter. It was a grey and black towel-
like material with lacey tattooings laid thereon.
Mrs. W. Croker was a leaf from a fashion-journal.
“ There was an amusing incident on the road to
‘ Raheen ’ last week. The distance of this man-
sion from trams and trains is considerable, and all
did not understand that fact. Two smart girls
essayed to walk from the Kew tram, but they
were out of training, and soon pulled up on the
road for a rest and an adjustment of the points of
the compass. No cabs could be spied, but one or
two spry milk-carts went rattling by. In despair,
they determined to hail the very next cart and
beg for a lift. It was a furniture van. The ap-
pearance of two smart, well-plumed charmers on
the box-seat with the driver of the van set every
passer-by tittering; and, alas! the van crawled
very slowly up the steep hill. Carriages, dog-
carts, hansoms passed the girls, and their final
descent near the gates caused a big grin along the
lines of the‘coachies.’ The guests grinned even
more broadly, but the girls stood the chaff de-
fiantly.
“The Syme and Mackinnon clans spread out
more and more in chiffon circles every day.
Their number at the Wrixon party was appalling.
The recently-arrived wife of Geoffrey Syme is the
fairest member of the big clan. She is ‘quite
English, you know,’ young, petite, pleasant-look-
ing, with taste in frocking, and a manner. At
this affair she wore an uncommon black silky
dress, with blue embossed flowers sprinkled
thereon. A well-considered toque went with it.
Geoffrey is back on the Age staff.
“ Senator Drake, who at social junkets walks
softly, looks sweetly, and says nothing, in the
manner prescribed by the blithesome.' Beatrice’
to her Benedick, held high festival with, his wife
last Sunday, over his Federal baby’s christening.
The Bananaland Minister unloaded official Atlas
burdens from his much-taxed shoulders, stowed
his playful pince-nez into a vest pocket, brushed
up his brown, gingery locks and mo., and worked
up a party smile. He is very proud of that son’s
arrival under the Federal Flag. He is to be a
wonderful little Australian native. Mrs. Drake
is a stout, fussy lady, with definite ideas of
Senatorial prestige. In the Federal House they
hold the Postmaster General to be a sincere and
earnest soul, but his soft-walking, unctuous man-
ner and velvety voice give him a Pecksniffian air
at which outsiders guffaw prodigiously.
“ The cards for tne State reception (postponed
sine die) were without gilt lettering or expensive
flourishes, but plain black-and-white pasteboards.
Lady Silverlocks puts on no side, and she tells
society cronies that they are not millionaires at
‘ Stonington.’ Captain Vallange does not look too
happy in his role of Private Secretary. His duties
keep him busy, and his pretty wife has not the
chance to be in his company these days.
“ The last raid of the ‘ Thirty Thieves ’ suffered
from chill in more senses than one. Jack Frost
got in his tentacles, and, few as the spectators
were to begin with, some of them went shivering
away before the end of the show. Dorothy Yane
(Mrs. Norman Menzies), in frilly white, laughed
at Lauri’s freaks, but apparently took little joy in
Josephine Stanton’s nasalisms. No theatre busi
ness is brisk at time of writing. Only confirmed
* nobodies * venture to junket in public during the
King’s illness.
“ ‘ Thirty Thieves * were not removed from the
Princess’ a day too soon. May Beatty’s frocks
were showing the strain of active service and the
spry Lauri’s careless treatment, in a way that was
alarming. Half the tinsel was danced off May’s
brief frock. Some of the chiffon punctuations
were missing from her circular skirt towards the
last nights, and May’s plump shoulders were pro-
truding further and further above the circum-
spection-limit—so much so, indeed, that a hand-
kerchief was used as a safeguard against accidents
to the low-cut bodice when May got going in a
dance with Lauri.
“ The Mayoral hop, at Prahran on Friday night,
attracted 1200 guests, after shaking hands with
whom the perspiring Mayor ambled off to the un-
dressing room and shed his robes of office. There-
after he had to depend on a municipal halo to
mark his hostly head. The two most diamonded
dames among the guests were Treasurer Turner’s
cheerful partner and Mrs. A. H. Payne—the
shapely skating champion of smart circles. Lady
Turners black, outstanding, heavy silk gown had
honeycombs of ivory applique at intervals around
the trained skirt. The Federal Treasurer, sprucer
than he has been seen for a long time, almost
waxed jovial as the evening advanced.

“ Brother ” King O’Malley’s imitation
Yankee spread-eagleism is getting a bit tiresome
in the House of Reps., and the Man with the
Hat, who, earlier in the session, oould depend
upon having plenty of listeners, now often has to
talk to empty benches.
The Mac Patorson, of Queensland, who
howled with rage when the Loan Bill was so
coldly received in the House of Representatives,
assured that body that all the talk of Australia
having over-borrowed was folly, because “but a
few years ago there were people in Europe pre-
pared to take over the whole debt of Australia in
exchange for our railways,” and Bill Lyne said
“ Hear, hear! ” Four common delusions in Aus-
tralia are (1) that there is somebody somewhere
who will give a fabulous sum for a million used
penny stamps; (2) That jewellers want to buy
cigar ash at a guinea an ounce ; (3) That if you
find a man hanging you mustn’t cut him down
till the police (who may be 10 miles away) arrive ;
(4) That there is, or was, somewhere in Europe, a
great syndicate ready to pay £207,000,000 for a
railway system which returns barely £4,000,000 a
year—in other words, which wants, or wanted, to
investa stupendous sum of money at 2 per cent,
when it could get nearly 3£ per cent, by buying
up our Government bonds, and have the security
not only of the railways but of the whole Aus-
tralian nation as well. Mac Paterson, of Queens-
land, seems to really believe in that syndicate.
Probably he also collects cigar ash with a view to
selling it to a jeweller some day at 21s. per ounce,
and wouldn’t cut Philp down till the police came,
if he found Philp hanging up.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The Man with the Owe —O’Sullivan.
Uneasy lies the Ed. that wears a crown.
N.S.W. Treasury receives £30,000 or so
probate-duty in the estate of the late Thomas
Dalton, M.L.O.
Governor-General Hopetoun is the second
Marquis salaried by the public of Australia. Nor-
manby governed Queensland, Victoria, andMaori-
land about 30 years ago.
Soon after the King’s accession a German
cancer-specialist visited him, and stayed several
days. That is one reason why so much alarm is
created in England whenever H.M. is announced
to be ill.
The late Premier Leake, and Kingsmill,
his successor for a day or two in Westralia, were
both educated at St. Peter’s College in Adelaide,
which still has a big entry of boys from Perth and
thereabouts.
The Dutch Queen’s dangerous illness was
not typhoid, as the cables stated, but peritonitis,
caused by another mishap. Chances of the pre-
sent royal line of Holland being continued seem
rather remote.
Rev. Horatio Gilbert Parker’s bride goes
40oz. to the ton. Sir Horatio Gilbert lives in
London, in the swell Carlton House-terrace,
opposite St. James’ Park, where even the dustman
is a reduced duke.
Obit. : At Moonee Ponds (Vic.), Mrs. Jas.
Mirams, aged 61. She was a granddaughter of
Lieut. Paterson, who served under Col. Collins
during his attempt, in 1801, to found a convict
settlement in Port Phillip.
At Ballarat (Vic.), t’other day, Michael
Alex. Carmichael, said to be one of the heroes of
the “ Charge of the Light Brigade,’’ was granted a
small old age pension by the Justices. “That’s
what he did for England ; that’s what she did for
him.”
David Syme, of Melb. Age, is 75 years
old ; Graham Berry will be 80 on 28th Aug. next,
whereas J. S. Butters—the evergreen Butters who
has a Melbourne reputation for great antiquity—
is but a little over 70, or two years younger than
the mercurial L. L. Smith.

John See got several congratulations from
England on his knighthood. One (from an Aus-
tralian politician now in London) was brought in
with 10s. to pay. “ I know who that’s from,” said
Sirjon with a chuckle, opening the envelope. And
sure enough he had guessed it.
N.S.W. Premier John See says that when
a title is offered by the King it is the duty of a
subject to loyally and thankfully accept it. Will
somebody tell See about William Pitt, William
Ewart Gladstone, and but, no doubt, these
suffered from a defective sense of duty.
The sister of Senate President, R. C.
Baker, has been decorated by the Pope, “in re-
cognition of her great services to the R.C.
Church.” Of late R. C. Baker himself has been
fast losing his social dash and cultivating an air
of ponderous meditation as if he too were in train-
ing for an ecclesiastical title.
Ballarat’s old Bishop, Dr. Thornton, now
vicar of Blackburn, Eng., on a better salary than
he was able to gather in Australia, is keeping up
his old name as a fighter. The C.E. Temperance
Society and he are in “holts” over the question
of a drink or two, the bishop, as most Australians
know, inclining to a glass if the liquor be good.
LorcbHopetoun has scored a marquisate
because (presumably) of his entertainment of the
Prince of Wales in Australia. And he wanted
Australia to pay for getting him the step-up in
the peerage ! Had he remained in England the
marquisate would have only come through extreme
good luck and the expenditure of tons of money.
E. W. Owe Sullivan is being badgered in
N.S.W. Parliament as to the reply from Lord
Rosebery to his letter asking for the money to
build the proposed “ Australia Facing the Dawn ”
Statue out of Rhodes’ bequests. It is safe to say
that the O’Sullivan will not rush into print with
the answer, as he did with his silly letter embody-
ing the request.
Premier Seddon, it appears, has several
big business undertakings on hand which will
engage what share of his attention he can spare
from running the Empire in London. The chief
is a big West Coast coal-mining venture. Politics
and the uplifting of the masses don’t absorb all of
Mr. Seddon’s energies by any means. Hence he
is by no means lean financially.
Recorded that the King’s first words upon
recovering consciousness after the operation were :
“ Will ray people ever forgive me?” The King’s
Australian subjects will forgive him everything if
he gets well. They don’t hanker after a king like
George of York, whose visit to these parts was
one of the direst calamities which ever happened
to Monarchism south of the equator.
Like so many others, the able, affectionate
and domesticated Primate Cowie (M.L.) has
passed away just as he had entered his name for a
rest and trip to his native England. He was born
there in 1831, was army chaplain for a while (he
was decorated for really gallant services in the
Indian Mutiny), and consecrated Bishop of Auck-
land in 1869. Heart disease suddenly closed his
career.
One of Australia’s greatest artists is the
picture-restorer at N.S.W. National Gallery. In
doctoring many of the best and most expensive
paintings, which seemed cracked and disfigured
beyond repair, lie has done the work of a wizard.
What a splendid trttasformation, for instance, has
he made in the aspect assumed in late years by
Jean Robie’s beautiful still-life canvas, alike the
envy and the despair of all who paint flowers,
metal and shells!
Barton, who has accepted a G.C.M.G., as
head of the Commonwealth, would have been dis-
tinguished as “Mr.,” as compared with “Sir”
See and other Premiers ; later, if lie became Chief
Justice of the Commonwealth, the “Mr.” would
be again a favorable comparison with the Sir
Maddens, Ways, and others. But the Common-
wealth is, so far as Barton is concerned, just
going to be a seventh Australian State.

I make no apology, and expect neither favor nor cen-

Curious that John Morley, the noblest
“pro-Boer” of them all, is the only politician
raised to the new Order of Merit—an order which
is evidently to be given, not for toadyism, but for
merit. There can now remain (after the precise
definition of this order) no shadow of an idea that
there is any “merit” about a K.C M.G.—which
is, in a sense, compensation for the thought that
it is, at root, impudence for any mere hereditary
monarch to patronise John Morley with a dis-
tinction.

M‘KLinlay’s connection with the country seems to

Rhodes’ bequests seem to have had a
curious evolution: —
He made six wills. The first, made in 1877, bequeathed
all his estates to the Sec. of State for the Colonies for
the time being, and the Attorney-General for the pro-
vince of Griqualand West. This was revoked in ’B2, when
he bequeathed his wealth to a young man named
Pickering, employed in the De Beers mine. After Picker-
ing’s death, in ’BB, he made a third will. Making pro-
vision for his brothers and sisters, he left the whole of
the residue to a financial friend. In ’9l a fourth will was
made, leaving the personal estate to the same friend and
Mr. Stead. The fifth will, in ’92, appointed the same
financial friend, Mr. Hawkesley, and Mr. Stead sole
executors and joint heirs of Mr. Rhodes’ fortune. It was
in January, ’95, that Mr. Rhodes first announced his
intention to found scholarships. It was in July, ’99, that
Mr. Rhodes revoked his will of ’9l, and substituted what
is now known as his last will and testament.
What would have happened had the Colossus
lived another 10 years ? He might have left all
his belongings to The Bulletin.
Almost the only Laborite in the House of
Reps, who owns up both to smoking and to
“ taking stimulants ” is Rev. J. B. Ronald.
Members of the party who have sworn off
both include Brown (who would probably
have learned better if he had gone right
through his course for the kirk as Brother
Ronald did), Hughes, McDonald, the
O’Malley, Page (Page but recently kept a
pub in the bush) and Thomas. Of all the
Ministers in the Reps., now that Barton
and Forrest are away, only long Lyne
smokes.
Much wonder is expressed by poli-
ticians this side of the water at the
speed with which Maoriland Agent-
General Reeves gets the tip as to
important official matters in London.
Reeves was again ahead with the news
of peace, and enabled Treasurer Ward
to first inform even the Gov.-General.
It is asserted that Reeves must have
secret-service money at his disposal, but
the plain fact of the matter seems to be
that he is a young man, doesn’t whisky
as much as some of his colleagues, and
owes a lot to his early press training.
to The Bulletin, from
London:—
Saw Rosebery, at a sale at Christie’s, t’other day. How
scandalously and audaciously the illustrated papers do
“ kid ” the public ! To look at the London News pictures
of kings or peers, you would think they were all 7ft. high.
Rosebery is a small, rather spare man, grey-haired, with
a soft pink face, appealing blue eyes- almost as light as
blind-blue—and splay-red lips. The youthful rose-bloom
of complexion with his grey hair is so soft as to be un-
manly. What an asylum for the blind mediocrity this
England is, when a soft prattler like Rosebery—a one-
eyed man—is regarded as the only hope of Liberalism !
The newly-knighted lawyer Rutledge, the
ex-parson and various politician, kept a title-foot
first in most of his doings of late years. Speaking
at the farewell dinner tendered to Lord Laming-
ton, Mr. Rutledge expressed a fervent hope that
his Lordship’s successor would be nothing less
distinguished than a “British nobleman.”
Rutledge has now begun to be a British nobleman
in his own eyes, and no doubt thinks the fact will
help him to the coveted first seat on Q. Supreme
Court Bench.
“ Van D.” : Re stories of old-school Tas-
manian clergymen. In my native district we had
a pastor who, I was assured by an old schoolmate
of his and of Thackeray’s, was the original of Chas.
Honeyman in “Pendennis.” He was a most
eloquent man, but having been in his early days
a free liver, he had been relegated to the country
parish where I first knew him. He sang a good
song, told a good story, and smoked a good smoke.
On one occasion I was present at the consecration
of a church, and at a “ spread ” after the cere-
mony, our minister —much to the horror of his
Bishop and reverend brothers—volunteered a
song, and, despite coldly repressive glances, sang
“The Coronation” from the Ingoldsby Legends.
Only much influence saved the old man’s gown.
George M‘Culloch, who was manager of
Mt. Gipps sheep-run when Broken Hill was
found on it, and who was for many years,
if he isn’t still, the largest shareholder,
has, during the last 10 years, put over
a quarter-of-a-million into pictures.
His galleries, which now hold one of
the finest private collections in Eng-
land, are at Kensington. And yet
in the Barrier’s early days Mr.
M‘Culloch was summed-up as the
roughest and ruggedest of all the
west country sheep and cattle men;
and, curiously enough, so up-ended
do things come out of the bag,
none of them put up a harder fight
against the interloping prospectors
than he. But he forgave them ;
and he didn’t leave the Barrier till
he had made a handsome gift to
the local miners’ hospital—which
is more than any other of the Aus-
tralian millionaires has done.
Maoriland King Seddon
while in Britain saw to it that
Captain Haw-Haw Russell, of
Hawkes Bay, one-time leader of
the Opposition, wool-king, director
of the Northern Investment Co.
and other things, was knighted. The per
sonage always described in the local prints as “ the
Gallant Captain ” has waited long for a bauble,
and although he imports everything, down to his
shirts and socks, direct from England, and is
about times more English than Joe Chamber-
lain himself, that bauble was long in coming.
Premier George Leake, of Westralia, died
in harness a few days ago. He was by descent a
’Groper of the ’Gropers. His father, George
Walpole Leake, was a Westralian M.L.C. in his
latter days, but held ten different State billets
between 1857 and 1890. As a curious instance of
the upside-downness of things Westralian, he was
promoted from being Acting Chief-Justice in 1880
to the billet of police-magistrate in ’Bl ! The
deceased Premier’s uncle, Luke Leake, was a
Westralian knight, M.L.C., Speaker, and other
things. George Leake’s first and only portfolio was
that of Premier. He didn’t graduate through any of
the subordinate offices. His first Ministry lasted
six months (May to Nov., 1901), His second, after
he had blown the trivial Morgans out of his road,
started at the end of Dec., 1901, and also lasted
six months, till his death. His colleagues were
men like himself who had never held office before.
He assumed power with a slender majority, when
the State was burdened with the huge debt heaped
up by Bigjohn Forrest, and the dead weight of
the Groper traditions were all against him.
Nevertheless, he was the best democrat that
Westralia has ever grown on its own soil; and he
did much good work. With more time, more
experience, and with more experienced col-
leagues he might have done much more. He was

The United Typewriter and Supplies Co.,
one of the largest concerns manufacturing type-
writers, seems to be getting more of its share of
the world’s business. One of its machines, the
“Yost,” was favoured recently by the Indian
Government with an order for one hundred and
twenty machines. In addition to this their
Austrian representative now reports the sale of
five hundred “ New Century ” Typewriters for use
on the Government Railways. An important
fact is that these two are record orders for the
world, and, what is more important in the eyes of
the Company, were obtained in competition with
all the leading makes. Australian buyers can
obtain both of these machines from the Company’s
office in Sydney, Melbourne and Perth.*

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
THE “ COLD SNAP ” IN MELBOURNE.
YARRABANKER : “Say, mister, would yer let a
cove in ter warm hisself?”
Such is greatness. A N.S.W. member of
the Federal House of Reps., who has been making
stirring speeches -on the fodder duties, recently
visited an outlying part of his pastoral electorate,
where the local publican and policeman quickly
gathered round the great man. Wishing to test
the impression his speeches had made, “Do any
of you fellows about here ever get Hansard ? ” he
asked. Boniface thought a moment; then his face
illuminated :—“Jack Flanagan’s damn bad, and
we don’t know what’s the matter with him ;
perhaps he's got it! ”

Q. Govt, papers tell how many of the
Bowen Kanakas are married, raise crops, and send
their children to school, where they show them-
selves “clever in imitative and memory work-
such as writing, spelling, &c., but very weak in
arithmetic.” That was ever Tanna’s weakness.
He always imagined £6 per annum was a fair
wage, and then gave it away for 30s. worth of
shoddy.
* * * *
“ Mungana ” : Travelling has lately been
reduced to a science in N.Q. T’other day a
married man left Redcap, carrying a small water-
bag. Next day his family (which comprised a
wife and five children) also left. The only dif-
ference was that the wife and family all had bluey
up; the eldest lad, about 10, staggered under a
drum fit for any old whaler, while another game
little pebble, apparently not more than seven
summers, bent beneath the weight of the family
bedding. The woman—Heaven pity her!—had a
child at the breast, and seemed to be suffering
from sandy blight.
“Vinter”: Met a female “swaggie,” when
making through the bush, near Seymour (Vic.).
She was quite 70, and carried a fair-sized bluey
and a billy. She had been on the tramp for three
days, looking for work —washing, scrubbing, sew-
ing, cooking, milking, or anything else that might
offer. “But,” she said, “it’s hard fora body to
get work these times. The d d men are doin’
women’s work at tuppence a day hereabout.”

“J.C.H.” : J. D. Moore (8., 24/5/’O2) was
far at sea in writing that the Kenniffs could
make “further down the Diainantina, from there
to the Roper, and camp with the buffalo-shooters,
mostly nomads.” The shortest cut, through the
bush, from Diamantina to Roper, can’t be less
than 800 miles, and there are no buffalo-shooters
on the Roper. Further, I defy J. D. Moore to
name one buffalo-shooter in the N.T. that is
wanted by the police. I had 19 years N.T. ex-
perience, and know what I’m talking about.

A blackfellow on the drought:—“First
time horses and cattle come up, plenty grass,
plenty water. Waterholes go dry ; make ’im
dam ; fetch ’im more cattle, fetch ’im sheep.
Dam go dry; make ’im hole alonga grounda, make
’im water come up ; fetch ’im more sheep. Then
God get angry, an’ he way : ‘You dam white-
feller !—you make ’im water come up alonga
ground. Now you make ’im grass grow /’ ”

“F.M.”: Australian squatters are dull
men, except at cutting down shearers’ wages and
travellers’ rations. Butler, a farmer of Walgett,
ploughs 200 acres of land, uses bore-water, and
harvests 400 tons of hay. (He could put down
2000 tons of ensilage at less cost). Hay, squatter,
Collindina (Murray River), ploughs sand-ridges
and light-red soil, tows lucerne, and trebles the
carrying-capacity of his run. Yet the water from
500 bores runs mostly to waste, while a thousand
sure sites are untapped ; and of 10,000 sq. miles of
country in E. Riverina not 10 are ploughed and
sown.
• • • •
Johnson hadn’t long been transferred to
the Bank of Reconstruction’s Possum ton branch
when his fellow clerks noticed something peculiar
about him. “ Have you observed that diabolical
Btare he transfixes you with out of his left
eye ? ” said the teller to the accountant.
“Rather,” said the latter. “Why, I was
making up Jumbuek’s overdraft yesterday,
and felt something paralyse me so that I
forgot to charge interest. It was John-
son’s eye going through me like a gimlet.”
Johnson turned up a bit late one morning,
and the teller noticed something wrong.
“ Where’s your eye ?” he shrieked. John-
son put his hand up, felt an empty socket,
and rushed excitedly out. He’d forgotten
to replace the glass eye, which he’d taken
out on going to roost the previous night.
So he rushed to the hotel and climbed
up to his attic. The girl was cleaning it
out. “Di-id you see a —a round piece of
glass,” he stammered. “ D’yer mean yer
eye?” said she. “Well, I seen little Jimmy
playin’ wid somethin’ that might ’er been a
glass eye.” Johnson hurled himself down-
stairs, bounded into the yard, pounced on
Jimmy, who was spinning a top, and shook
him till his heels rattled against his teeth.
“ What d’yer do with that round piece o’
glass you took out of my room ?|” he de-
manded. The youngster whimpered. “I
I swopped it f-f-or this ’ere top,” he said.
“Who to? Tell me quick or I’ll murder
you.” “To-ommy Smith.” “Take me to
him.” So they went and found Tommy.
He had swopped it for a hoop to Billy
Jones. Billy had sold it for a penny to
Sandy MTherson. Sandy had bargained
it for a white rat to Paddy O’Brien, and
Paddy had dropped it in the river on his
way to school. Johnson turned up at the
bank a bit later with a black patch over his
left eye, and the spell was lifted. After the
overdraft closed for the night he went
trawling in the river for his optic, but
though he got an eel and a kerosene-tin,
the great deep refused to give up John-
son’s eye.
“Traveller”: Sheep are valuable these
days; 5000 wethers were travelled down from
Lake Victoria station to Morgan to be trucked to
the interior, and, owing to food-scarcity on the
road, owner had to truck hay from Adelaide to
Morgan, and have it carted and spread out for
last 30 miles of the journey. Then the mob were
spelled for two days at Morgan, and fed on hay
to put them in buckle for the rail trip.

* * * *
“M.M.”': He statement (B. 7/6/’O2) that
the “ Noogoora burr” is a curse. In Boggabilla
(N.S. W.) district horses and cattle have been kept
alive on this burr for the past three months.
They eat round it in a circle, chew the top off and
drop that, eating only the stalk. They do not
fatten on it, but manage to hold their own;
otherwise they would have been food for crows
long ago.

The drought and the wind-storms and the
sand-drifts of the last few years have exposed very
many aboriginal and other relics and curiosities in
Centralia ; the writer can talk with most cer-
tainty of far-western N.S.W. Museum authorities
might do a lot worse than encourage way-out
surveyors, patrolling constables, and station-
managers to lay hold of what they can.

“ Merool ” : At a sale near Wyalong,
recently saw a man buy a mob of 60 very poor
bullocks at £3 per head. Said he expected a good
many of them would die before he got them home,
but what remained alive would pay him well.
Which augurs badly for the price of beef-meat for
the next few years.
» * • *
Lately passed out at Taradale (Vic.) one
Smith, who could give “Uncle Tom” Brassey
points at falling-off. His first big fall was from a
bridge crossing the Coliban at a height of 140 ft.
Whilst working on the structure he slipped and
fell to the bed of the creek beneath. So con-
vinced were his mates of his certain destruction
that they did not go to his assistance, but, in-
stead, went in search of a stretcher, and a bag to
throw over the mangled remains. On reaching
the bottom, however, they found the fallen one
with left arm and leg broken, and cursing them
with much vigor for being so long. Patched up
in the course of a few months, he started work in
one of the local mines, only to tumble down a 20ft.
winze. Arm again broken. Then he took to
doing odd jobs about the townships, and while
engaged painting the stable of one of the local
pubs, slipped and fell to the ground a distance of
sto 6 ft. Picked up unconscious and died a week
later from spinal-paralysis.*

Gem of botanical information from London
Scraps
The “ cannibal” tree grows in Australia, and people
are shy of it. It is the shape of a huge pineapple, and
attains a height of lift. It has a series of broad, board-
like leaves, growing in a fringe at the apex, and strong
enough to bear the weight of a man. The instant that a
person touches one of these leaves it flies together like a
trap, crushing the life out of the intruder. Formerly the
natives used it lor executing criminals.

“Via” : Re dogs and bites (“Abo.”
14/9/’l). Ever heard that if attacked by a dog
and you grab his front paws hard that he (or she)
won’t bite ? Tried it myself once some years
back, and it was all right—for the dog : he appre-
ciated the squeezing by taking about 4oz. off my
leg. Know hundreds who believe in the paw-
squeeze theory. I prefer a tree. Also, a St. Bernard,
if kept *on the chain for a month or two, makes
the most savage watchdog ever wanted and a bit
over, as they will tackle friend and foe, and 9st.
of vicious dogs-ineat is a bough proposition in the
small hours.
[All the same, it is a fact that if you grab one of a dog’s
forepaws and squeeze it hard and unflinchingly, he can’t
bite you while you retain the strength and nerve to keep
the squeeze hard on to his digits. Present writer, years
ago, when challenged to uphold the theory, tried it on
with a lfiib. bull-terrier, which did not succeed in biting
him within the specified 2min. Anyone who would see
for himself without risk how the thing works has only to
squeeze the paw of his pet dog. Firstly it will whine,
and gently lick his hand, and beg to be let off ; then it
will squeal ; finally it will try to bite. It may succeed after
a struggle in scratching the back of your hand with its
teeth, but it can’t bite so long as you keep the grip on.]

THE WORK ON FATHER’S MIND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We’ve got three hundred acres, and
There’s forty fairly clear,
But father’s got the lot in hand
And whispers in our ear
Of crops that will be grand to view
In paddocks all outlined—
I wonder when we’ll battle through
The work on father’s mind.
From jackass-laugh to dingo-cry
We’re grubbing giant trees,
And stacking wood that by-and-bye
Will bring us wealth and ease ;
Or else we’re clearing scrub away
That grows again to blind
The bit of crop, and so delay
The work on father’s mind.
He has his burden of the toil,
But reckons when he’s gone,
’Twill be a splendid bit of soil
For us to live upon.
He’s up before the sparrow kin,
And we’re not far behind—
There are eighteen-hour stages in
The work on father’s mind.
So day to day, and year to year,
We slave and slog and grub ;
There’s only forty fairly clear,
The rest is full of scrub.
And if we ever battle through,
I know we’re sure to find
’Tis but an introduction to
The work on father’s mind.
vio.
M. O’M.
THE PENITENT,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The great Evangelist, who nightly strove
For Temperance and Heaven’s sake,
Had roped the thirstiest, most sinful cove
That ever saw a crimson snake,
And down upon his knees, with woful face,
Went Pete, the penitent, and cried for grace.
Then Brother Yamm was filled with bliss intense,
And o’er the alcoholic Goth
He whooped anew ; with goodly eloquence
He whipped the meeting to a froth.
The women wept, the wondering fellows thought
That in their time a miracle was wrought.
“ My friends,* the glad reformer cried, “ behold
A brand plucked from the burning here,
A wayward lamb returneth to the fold,
No more to drown his soul in beer 1 ”
And on he went for hours, his mellow tones
The lowly “ drunk ” diversified with groans.
But penitence with zeal provokes a heat,
And heat induces thirst in some,
And now, up from the penitential seat,
Pathetic protestations come:
“ Yah 1 sling it, mister. Take a shorter cut,
We shan’t get out afore the pubs are shut I"

natural-history fact is the
complete disappearance of
the dasyurus from Vic.
western district. Trapping
rabbits 15 years ago I used
to catch lmudreds of these
“native cats,” but about
five years since the animal
suddenly disappeared from
that locality, and now there
are large areas in which it
is not to be found at all.
There is still plenty of
cover, and bandicoots and
’possums are still numer-
ous ; but the “cat” has
gone completely, although
it was never systematically
hunted. In fact, trappers
always hated to see a cat
caught ; so the disappear-
ance must have been due
either to an epidemic or to
wholesale migration.

Writes ‘ Shaker from the Far North :
On his inspection tours Dr. Roth (Protector of Q.
Aborigines) labors under the disabilities of royalty,
Were it possible—it isn’t—for him to visit the Peninsula
incog he would have his eyes opened to many things.
N.itbless, the nigs, are not so badly off under the Protec-
tion Act as some people make out. Doubtless, the police
bunt them about a lot, but on the other hand indis-
criminate “dispersions” (official and unofficial) are prac-
cally things of the past. Also, if the police do comman-
deer all the young gins, the latter are thereby saved
from the Ohows and the beastly immoral diggers. The
Govt, ration is certainly scanty, and possibly does not
always reach its destination ; but, as a set off, there are
the diggers’ camps, which can now be looted with im-
punity. Every resident knows that the blacks, as far
north as Ooen, are rotten with disease ; but that is the
resultof the “advance of civilisation.” After all, if things
are just let slide, the trouble will settle itself quietly
within the next 10 or 15 years.

“ Salgy ” : Re catching crows (B. 28/6/02).
I have found the following method very successful
in both N.S.W. and Queensland. Wing a crow
with a gun, and peg the bird, back downwards,
by its wings to the ground. The captive’s out-
cries will soon bring more crows along, and pre-
sently one of these is sure to jump upon the con-
finee, only to be gripped by the latter’s claws and
held as in a vyce, making capture quite easy. The
second prisoner can then in turn be used as a
decoy.
“Mid” - : Re obtaining young crows.
Apart from waiting till the nestlings are feathered
and then felling the tree, I have taken numbers
thus : I have watched the nest until the young
birds appeared on the side, and have then watched
for and shot the parent birds. Then, within a
couple of days, the young birds have been found
at the foot of the tree, having been driven by
hunger to attempt flight when they could only
just do enough in that way to break their fall.

“ Ben Sun ” : Re fire-fighting (“ Bob R”
B. 21/6/02):- S V ’
The watercart would be useless in rough, broken
country ; and, against a bush as distinguished from a
grass-fire, would be utterly ineffective. Further, bags,
bushes, or greenhide beaters are usually available, while
water is seldom so in the bushfire season. Also, if fire-
fighting is “ reduced to a science ” in Riverina, it isn’t
elsewhere. Have never seen a fire fought scientifically,
or even intelligently. Mostly men belt around anyhow,
get “ winded” and half roasted, and only drive the fire
faster. Moreover, when a fire is driven by a strong wind
through heavy grass, leaves and other rubbish, little can
be done, and the only check possible is to burn back from
some existing break. But, given a crowd under control,
an advancing grass-fire can be beaten quickly if the
fighters divide into small gangs, and every two of these
start to burn back at opposing angles towards the coming
fire. If the intervals between the starting points are well
estimated, each gang meets another in time ; the force of
the wind isn’t fully felt; every man is working effectively,
and the checking fire on the forward side can be beaten
out close to the point of ignition all along. But if a
checking fire is carried across the wind it’s pretty sure to
get away. Finally, clubs should be formed and men
taught how to deal with bush-fires, without wasting
energy and endangering life.

simple form (r.e., cash in bank, land or scrip, without
encumbrances, or a life-policy), and you want to leave it
simply (say to one person, or to several persons, tot
division), with reasonable intelligence you can make youf
own will. “ Forms,” as a rule, are pitfalls. Just say in
simple words what you intend, and see that it is wit-
nessed by two persons who do not benefit by it. If you
want to go into complications of life interests, dependent
heritages, and so on, better hire a lawyer.

The sun-picture on this page is of Mr. Louis
Grist, an athletic young Sydney musio-com-
poser and voice-trainer. His music has a
cathedral echo in it, and there are vague threats
that he will do something “ pretty good ” in the
near future.
“ Blackbirding ” in the New Hebrides for
Q. sugar plantations is becoming every week more
difficult. The recruiter Roderic Dhu has only
been able to obtain five men in two-months-and-a-
half at Aoba, and then the ship was fired upon by
Kanakas. Last trip of the ltoderio Dhu was
fraught with bloodshed, but the facts were care-
fully suppressed by most of the papers.
Douglas Rannie, Assistant-Inspector of
Polynesians at Bundaberg and Mackay, has been
writing pathetically to the Q. press on the woes
of returned kanakas:
Then what of the children, born in the State, educated
in the State schools, brought up in Sunday schools ;
must these children be banished into the blackness and
darkness and corruption of heathenism? .... I view
with horror, contempt, and disgust those who clamor to
drive these young Christians down to barbarism, &c.
Unfortunately for Mr. Rannie’s pathos, kanaka
children born in the State of Queensland are
British subjects and cannot be forcibly returned
to the Islands But if the heathenism and cor-
ruption of the Islands is worse than the heathenism
and corruption of the Chow dens and brothels
where Tommy Tanna picks up the rudiments of
civilisation, then things in Polynesia are very,
very rotten indeed.
CAPTAIN DUMARESQ, of Longford (Tas.),
who reached his 100th birthday on 10th
Jume, 1902.
Captain Dumaresq, whose portrait is pub-
lished in this issue, is perhaps the only absolutely
authentic centenarian in the Commonwealth. He
was 100 on. June 10, has a son aged 63 in the Tas-
manian Parliament, and a brother (now dead) was
made a colonel by Wellington on the field of
Waterloo.
Possibly it is only that unconscious
humorist, the cable-man, who is responsible for
this:
The Kaiser has issued ordfers regulating the marriages
of army officers. The officers, it is decreed, must have a
private income of at least £l5O per annum. They must
give a full description of their intended wives.
The German officer, according to this, must give a
full description of the lady before marriage. It
seems a very large order, and it doesn’t seem quite
respectable.
The popular definition of a “ lady ”
becomes very hard to arrive at. The other day a
tall, handsome girl of 18 indignantly repudiated
the charge of vagrancy thrown at her in a Mel-
bourne police court on the ground that “ she was
a lady and was being kept by a Chinaman.”
Governor Tennyson perpetrated a fresh
stanza for “Gor Save” in Adelaide the other
day:—
Lord God show forth Thy power,
And guard his every hour,
God save the King.
Clothe him with righteousness,
Crown him with happiness,
God, with thy blessings bless and save the King.

MOSTLY MELBOURNIAN.


And is deriving immense
benefit from a system of “ San- 1
dowing” that he has of late
been indulging in.
Mr. G. H. Reid, leader of the Opposition
in the Federal Parliament, speaking to a
Daily Telegraph Representative yester-
day, said that he had never enjoyed such
robust health. He has decreased in weight.
At one period in his history Mr.. Reid tried cycling as a form
of exercise, but soon arrived at the conclusion that for one of
hip ample proportions that form of outdoor recreation was a
trifle precarious.

The Office-Boy’s Nightmare.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The office-boy was weary,
So laid him down to rest;
Then came his troubles dreary,
And sat upon his chest—
“ A ” shelves, “ B ” shelves, pigeon-holes and files,
Throwing out their pockets, shedding manu-
scripts,
Cartoons, book-plates, ’graving blocks in piles,
;Careering round and round him with fantas- .
tic
skips.
Around him danced cotillions —
A regiment of books,
». And out of them, in millions,
Trooped with laughing looks—
Long scraps, short scraps, scraps of every hue,
Dropping tracts around him, sowing seeds of sin,
Love-scraps, war-scraps, lying scraps and true,
Yelling out their secrets with Hell’s
own
din.
The office-basket grumbled
In Editorial style,
And out of him there tumbled
Effusions by the mile—
Long pomes, short pomes, pomes of every sort,
Shedding tears around him, venting music sweet,
Love-yarns, snake-yarns, tragedy and sport,
Telling him their troubles with a mourn-
ful
bleat.
Then brooms began to muster,
* nd scissorses to grind,
The paper knife and duster,
And God knows behind—
Paste-pots, ink-pots, pots of every Size,
Putting on the war-paint, putting on the jam,
Square brush, round brush —dab him in the eyes,
And the “ office-boy ” arises with a great
big
Dam.
n.s.w.
E. S. SORENSON.
