POLITICAL
POINTS
Protection means independence, Freetrade
dependence.
Tf the A.N.A. wants to justify its name
it should make a rule that members must give
preference in all things to goods of Australian
production.
Trenwith intended on a recent Sunday in
Melbourne to discourse upon the suggestive
theme of “A Modern Ananias, the War burton
Railway job, and other things,” but the Age and
the Argus declined to advertise the text. Now
why ?
There will soon be no rank-and-file of the
Freetrade-and -Geebung crowd out of a billet.
Both the N.S.W. State and the Federal Govts,
are filling every appointment from their ranks—
presumably to ensure the Opposition plenty of
funds for next election.
N.S.W. Government proposes borrowing
£1,000,000 for water-conservation, drainage, and
sundries. And if this expenditure adds £lOO,OOO
a year to the land revenue, that will be spent
cheerily as the Owe Sullivan goes along, and the
£1,000,000 debt will remain for ever, as a matter
of course.
The man who takes over the Premiership
as George Leake’s successor (probably Walter
James) will be the fifth Premier Westraliahas had
inside 17 months. John Forrest, the mightiest
borrower of them all, held on for over ten years,
but the men who have to face the bill after the
great borrower is gone have a precarious billet.
Premier See says he is going to establish
a comprehensive Local Government system in
N.S.W. Meanwhile Sydney has had local
Government for over half a century, and after all
that period we still find Daley, M.L.A., with a
notice on the Assembly’s business paper demand-
ing that the Government shall repair some street
or other at Dawes Point!
The new Transcontinental Land Grab
Railway movement in S. Australia wants watch-
ing. A very strong local crowd has been got into
the swim—and Kingston, who once kicked a shark
downstairs for making a cheeky proposal of this
sort, is out of the way. Of course the Register
approves; and Advertiser Bonython has been
elected first vice-president, so the wobbly patriot
has presumably been fastened down too. Oppo-
sition-leader John Darling is on the executive,
and the Fat of the land is all something or other
in the big game.
The stopgap, divided Government will pro-
bably remain in power in Victoria until the end
of the session. The Factories Act exercises a
mighty influence. If not re-enacted before the
end of the session, it is a dead letter—falls abso-'
lutely from the statute book —and the Labor-
party is prepared to keep conservative Irvine in
power on the understanding that he re-enacts the
measure without delay. The Factories Act, also,
is Peacock’s one ewe-lamb, and sooner than sacri-
fice it he would vote with the Govt, against his
own want-of-confidence motion.
S.A. Govt, lately wanted to buy land at
Port Adelaide to extend its big produce-depot.
The owners, the wealthy absentee S.A. Co. of
London, who had originally paid 12s. 6d. for it,
coolly asked £750 per acre ! The land was still in
its natural state when Kingston was S.A. Premier.
The S.A. Co. was founded by George Fife Angus
as a philanthropic institution in 1837.
The same crowd that worked the S'African
press just before the war is trying its hand
once more, this time to get the Cape Con-
stitution suspended. Nobody expounds the
majority’s view; the blatant minority is having
all the say—and it must be a minority ; a majority
could do as it thought fit, without any suspension,
or it could suspend the Constitution itself.
The best part of a year has elapsed since
Queensland, Victoria, Tasmania, and S.A. were
relieved of a considerable burden of taxation by
the abolition of the inter-State duties, and only
now have two of them made their first attempt at
raising a little extra revenue to make good the
loss. The other two have done nothing. And all
four have moaned bitterly because, while paying
less taxes, they have not had so much revenue.
Kingston never showed himself so dis-
tinctly the fighting man of the Ministry as in the
debate on the Bonus Bill. It was by sheer per-
severance and deadly earnestness and force of
character that he drove the Bill past the critical
second reading division, and though it was practi-
cally wrecked in committee after all, he fought it
to the last with a courage that deserved better
fortune. All the other members of the_
put together have about half C.C.K.’s grit. /
Statistics read by Mr. S. G. Martin before
Brisbane Royal Society show that the mortality
of the Polynesians in Queensland during five
quinquennial periods was (per thousand) 74, 102,
75, 51 and 36. The lowest rate was that for the
period 1895-99. The rate for the European popu-
lation of the ages of the Kanakas (15 to 45), is 8 per
1000. One-half the deaths amongst the islanders
are due to tubercular diseases; and pneumonia and
dysentery are other great causes of mortality.
Hall-Jones, the funereal blow-pipe of the
Seddon Government, appeared in the pulpit of
Scots Church, Wellington, on a recent Sunday
when a thanksgiving service was held in honor of
the church-yard peace in S’Africa. Among other
things, Hall-Jones eulogised the Boers as brave,
honorable and sturdy foemen, “ well worthy of
our steel, &e.”; whereat it has been pointed out
that the Government is no longer a one-man
affair, since one of Seddon’s colleagues dares to
differ from “the boss.” “Treacherous,”’“un-
generous,” “ unscrupulous ” are some of the
mildest epithets that Gory Dick has applied to-the
men whom the Jingoes now try to palaver with
soapy epithets. Perhaps when the Mighty
Bounder comes back he will consider the oase of
Hall-Jones.
AT THE PRESS CLUB.
MR. PRATTLE : "Hullo, Baldy, you look da[?]ful.
What’s up?”
MR. BALDWIN : “ Got the sack from the Daily
Austral. I was doing the cricket-cables and
only made two columns out of a 10-word
message.”
Edmund Barton (who up-to-date is making
a braver show in London than his best friends
hoped for):
With regard to the suspension of the Cape Constitu-
tion, he could only say, without attempting to trench
upon the domestic concerns of Cape Colony, that once
free institutions had been given to a people, their sus-
pension for even one day would be a dangerous experi-
ment, only to be undertaken when there was a certainty
that no other course would succeed.
The matter is one on which an Australian Premier
may well speak ; if one colonial constitution may
be suspended so may another.
When Hughes, of N.S.W. , drew the atten-
tion of the Representatives to the fact that Aus
tralasia had in 29 years borrowed, °n account oj
the State and local bodies, close on £200,000,000
and had onlyreceived £28,000,000, the rest having
gone back in interest and charges, M Mdian put
m the bright remark, “ Haven t we got the assets
created By the money?” So we have-bamng
those that are worn-out or obsolete, and saying
nothing of the fact that a great deal of tne
money was never represented by assets.
But the sorrowful fact is that if the
money paid in interest had instead been
paid to a Public Works Fund we
might have practically all these
assets and have no debt at all.
And as we can’t undo the past
the right thing is to start on a
non - borrowing basis now and
keep on it for the future.
It appears by cable that
Premier Seddon has been doing
a very extra whoop indeed in
London about his own desire
and the desire of Maoriland to
give a trade preference to the
“ Motherland.” And yet, sad
to say, things get no “ forrader.”
Britain admits most Maoriland
produce in free, and the Govern-
ment the Gasbag
charging duties on
Britan produce which are the
heaviest in the world, bar, per-
haps, the rates of Canada and
the United States. Britain buys
about £10,000,000 worth of goods
in a year from Maoriland, and Dick’s
State only buys about £6,000,000 worth in
return. Compared with the wide liberality
of Germany, Britain’s best customer, whose
anti-British tariff is much lighter than that of
Maoriland, and which buys from Britain about
£7,000,000 more per annum than Britain buys
from her, the bitter hostility which Seddon’s
Government shows against British industries in
practice looks very bad. Certainly it doesn’t at
all square with Richard of Kumara’s theories.
Premier See’s latest is that, “if time
permits, he intends this season to grapple seri-
ously with the question of Local Government ” in
N.S.W. But time never permits to grapple seri-
ously with the question, which is left mouldering
at the tail-end of the notice-paper. And time
always permits to grapple with the question
that is taken first. If John See is going to
tackle the Local Government problem as soon as
everything else is finished it has a beautiful show.
S.M. Herald would compass sea and land
to get one gird at the Labor Party. On the
A.W.U. rule which fines a member £3 if he votes
or works against the union candidate, it says:
“ The position of M’s.P. who take the solidarity
pledge is much the same in principle. They are
not fined £3, but they are menaced with the loss
of their seats.” And since Party Government
began wasn’t any member of any party subject to
the same menace ? A crooked vote meant death
at next election, and nobody thought of mention-
ing coercion in that connection.
It seems to have been possible for any
Tom, Dick, or Harry in Vie. public service to get
hold of a frank stamp, and send his letters and
bundles through the post on the nod. Minister
Drake’s recent investigations have brought to light
a set of false teeth sent through O.H.M.S. by an
M. Another even sent his love-letters “On
His Majesty’s Service.” He was “ paying his
addresses ” without paying. The Vic. Govt,
simply printed O.H.M.S. envelopes and wrappers,
and sent them round according to the dictates of
whim and fancy. Australia needs to learn that
eternal vigilance is the price of economy.
Someone should be brought to book over
the muddling of N.S.W. Statutes consolidation.
G. C. Heydon, when a practising barrister, pro-
posed to consolidate them without fee, but with
“a- small amount of paid service.” He was
allowed to start. After some time hitches arose.
G. H. Reid, then Premier, quashed the enterprise.
But Mr. Want revived it, and as Attorney-
General, appointed a staff of junior barristers at
something like £3OO a year each. Cutting and
pasting have been going on since. In salaries
alone over £lO,OOO has been consumed ; other ex-
penses run the sum up, it is estimated, to £20,000.
A Cocky to The Bulletin :
Re your remarks (28/6/’o'2) on the presidentship of the
N. Farmers and Settlers’ Association as a probable
step to an M.L.0.-ship : Is everybody in this crawlsome
country merely a servile solicitor for a bauble of some
kind? The illiterate bush-person “enthuses” over the
bush “ mimber ” because he scents a J.P.-ship, and the
Premier of a State slobbers “ Joe ” for a knighthood—it’s
simply awful! Shouldn’t the honor of being the head of
a large organisation working for the common welfare of
its members be sufficient for anyone? Evidently not.
The Farmer’s Ass. is in a tight place now. It is cordially
hated by the Govt, and despised by its own class, and
nothing can pull it through but a strong masterful farmer
(not a “ strange combination ”), a man prepared to deal
stoush out to the political curse when it obtrudes itse f—
in fact a farmer-enthusiast. The Ass. can gain nothing
by electing a place hunter or a hybrid creature.
Though the evidence given by Tas. em-
ployers before the recently-touring Vic. Shops
and Factories Commission was violently anti-
labor, it must not be supposed that said employers
are making a big profit out %>f sweated labor.
Barring a few clothing mills and the jam factories,
wherein child-labor is largely availed of, there is
little actual sweating in the Isle of Dreams.
Wages are wretchedly low, of course, but the
workers are not yet numerous enough to be suc-
cessfully run against each other in the cockpits
of machine-made competition, as in larger centre
like Sydney and Melbourne. Successful sweating
THE BOSS OF THE LOT.
“ Now, if you had to be some kind of animal
what sort would you be?”
“Oh, I don’t know! I suppose a man.”
requires a waiting army of unemployed round the
factory door. In Tas. the unemployed doesn’t
wait; he mostly drifts to the mainland.
A N.S.W. Govt, officer of experience on
day labor :
Day labor is right enough. In theory it is the right
thing ; hut it’s terribly handicapped. In this respect
Labor’s friends are its worst enemies. You’d think the
Labor party would have enough horse-sense to give the
thing a fair show ; but they haven’t. They expect all
their constituents to be employed by O’Sullivan. All
their friends’ friends who are incompetent to do anything
else are thought good enough for us. Well, as for me,
when a man comes with a note from a Minister to be
put on at once, he’s not put on at once ; if he is put on,
he is not kept on if he’s a duffer. I’m up to my
neck now because I sacked two of ’s nominees, and
won’t put them back. Much of this, and your billet is
not worth keeping. We get no thanks for kicking up the
dust, so most of us let it go, and our estimates go, too.
If day labor ever goes overboard, it’s the M’s.L.A.. chiefly
the Labor men, too, who will be to blame.
Labouchere in London Troth od the
Hopetoun question:
I entirely approve of the action of the Australian
Parliament. The president of the U.S. receives £lO,OOO
per annum. Our ambassadors abroad receive, on an
average, about £BOOO per annum. I presume that the
idea of the Australians is that they wish their Governor-
General to live in the style of the chief executive officer
of a Commonwealth, and that they think that what is
enough for the American President is enough for him.
We are told that they will not get good men for the
money. I could find, them plenty of excellent men who
would gladly accept the position at the price. ... If I
were an Australian, I should insist on my Governor-
General being an Australian ; in fact, I should favor his
being elected to the position by Australians. The unity
of the Empire under one sovereign might be secured by
the King personally ratifying the selection, although this
would be a mere form, as is the Sovereign’s approval of
the choice of a Speaker by the Commons.
In view of the looming Victorian dencit,
the long, loose, rambling Shiels says his only
remedy is to sit tight on the Treasury chest. But
that won’t meet the difficulty. Since Victoria
has lost its border customs, and since the Federal
tariff has been so violently cut down by the push
to which its own Sargood belongs, more revenue
is imperative. The sitting-tight process, when
carried beyond a certain point, consists in letting
schools go out of repair, in letting railways go to
seed till they are dangerous to travel on, in letting
everything run down —and then floating a big
loan for repairs. And that is dry rot —not
economy. Victoria is already more cheaply
governed by far than England is in peace time,
though it pays old age pensions and England
doesn’t. The tightness on the chest has already
gone about as far as is possible without bad
results.
The Jingo fury ran riot in Westport
(M.L.) on Peace Day. A well-known and
much-esteemed French citizen innocently enough
hung out the French flag by way cf cele-
bration. This was too much for the patriotio
pro-Gores, who hauled down the flag and
trailed it in the mud. Another crowd paraded
the'town with an effigy of ex-President Kruger,
and demanded free drinks at the hotels. One
publican refused to accede to the steal, whereupon
the drunken Jingoes proceeded to hang the effigy
to the lamp in front of the door. One of his sons
attempted to prevent this, and a violent scuffle'
ensued, ending in the young fellow’s leg being
broken. These proceedings have excited disgust
even on the Jingo-ridden West Coast, and a,
public apology has, through the influence of cer-.
tain citizens, been made to the Frenchman. The.
leg-breaking episode will probably reach, the law;-,
courts.
The great dailies would have us believe,
that Canada is enthusiastically Jingo, but the real
position is carefully suppressed. As a matter of
fact, there is a strong Separatist party in Canada.
Its real stronghold is in the French provinces; but
its adherents are to be found all thrpugh the
Dominion. Premier Laurier is a French Canadian,
and, though his staunchest supporters are opposed
to cohentingenting, he has played to the Imperial
gallery in order to conciliate the “loyal” pro-
vinces. Had the Premier been anything but a
French Canadian there might have been violent
anti-jingo manifestations in the French provinces
ere now. Also, there is a strong feeling in
Columbia on account of Chamberlain’s disallowing
the Aliens Restriction Act. Yellow labor is
cutting out white in the mines there, and the race
question is becoming so acute that J. Brum, will
have to forego his fondness for the Jap. if he
wishes to avoid consequences so hideous to con-
template that it would be treasonable to mention
them just now.
TIME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Time called for a man,
There came John See ;
See and O’Sullivan,
And Mister Lee.
Time, sick with debt and drought,
Brooked no delay ;
They played at in or out,
And drew their pay.
The Time said : “ Hearts and men,
Peak, pine, and die.”
They: “ We’ll discuss that when
We’ve got Supply.”
The Time said : “ Rain will come—
Make ready now.”
They : ‘ ‘ But we cannot slum
The King’s pow-wow.
“ We needs must make that bright,
Though things be dull.”
The Time’s reply was quite
Unquotable.
But the deep, indrawn breath
Seemed to partake
Of prescience of death
And ruinquake.
And when the next call’s heard
And understood, i
The people’s answering word |
May show a mood
Disastrous to the clan
Of Premier See,
See and O’Sullivan,
And Mister Lee.

Newcastle
Debility
COMPLETELY CUBED BY
VITADATIO
A resident of Magill, S.A., has added his testimony to
the many others in praise of this wonderful remedy.
He says:—“ A member of my family was suffering from
nervous debility, and became so weak and ill that the
carrying out of daily duties become a burden, and at
length impossible. Medicine did not appear to do any
good, and a change of air was suggested, but as this was
impossible under existing circumstances, I looked for
something else to afford relief. In looking over a paper
one evening the words “ TEY VITADATIO,” caught my
eye. I did so, and after the sufferer had taken the first
bottle an improvement was noticeable, and three other
bottles effected a complete cure. This was two years
ago, and there has been no return of the illness since.
I have recommended VITADATIO to several similary
afflicted, and the same good results have followed.”

The American Declaration of Independence,
printed in English and Spanish in parallel
columns, was being distributed among the Fili-
pinos when the U.S. military authorities inter-
fered and suppressed it. One officer declared it to
be “ a d incendiary document ” as, from a
Jingo standpoint, it undoubtedly is.
Hear the bitter cry of Melb. Argus :
The defect of the Commonwealth honor list is that, apart
from military officers who fought for the flag in S’Africa, it
is made up mainly of politicians. . . . We look in vain for
adequate recognition of men distinguished in scholarship,
etc. . . . Some day, and soon, let us hope, the Imperial
recognition of merit will be broadened here as it has been
broadened in the United Kingdom.
And, let us hope, dear friends, that it will be
broadened in time to include at least one member
of the present Akgus proprietary.

DEAFNESS.
In addition to the inconvenience inherent in the
infirmity itself, DEAFNESS has a marked influence on
the Mental Development of the individual, when the
latter has been attacked bv it in youth.
For a long time DEAFNESS and CHRONIC AFFEC-
TIONS OF THE EAR have been considered almost
incurable, but recently a very simple means has been
found in the ELECTRICON to successfully apply
METALLIC GALVANIC ELECTRICITY direct to the
Ears, PERMANENTLY CURING DEAFNESS, and com-
pletely banishing all distressing head noises.
Those who are interested in the subject would do
well to read the complete description of this method of
treatment in the “ Journal for the Deaf, published by the
R. VERNON COY.,

HIS RUPTURE
CURED.
Engineer on the Midland Railway
Restored by the Wonderful Rice
Method,
One of the well-known engineers on the Midland
Railway, haying been in their employ a long time,
relates some very interesting facts about how he
,hi lns . e lf of a bad rupture by the Rice
Method. His name is George Jordan, and is in
good standing, so what he says for the benefit of
other railway men who are ruptured can be relied
upon as truthful and of value.
27, Bishopstone Road, Gloucester,
July 21,1901.
Dr. W. S. Rice.
Dear Sir,—l am very pleased to say that I am
cured of Rupture after using jour treatment for
three months, having been ruptured for seventeen
years.
I have already spent a lot of money on treatment,
having paid one doctor sixteen guineas wi bout
getting much good, and L had lost an hope of being
cured until I read your advertisement in Lloyd's
Newspaper.
I acted strictly on your advice, and I have not been
troubled with my Rupture since the first week 1 tried
your treatment, and although I get a lot of shaking
about (being a locomotive engine driver on the
Midland Railway), I have not worn a T ru ;s for a be u
two months, and I do not feel the slightest weaki: as-
now, and am enjoying better health than I have
had for seventeen years.
You can make what use you like of this letter, a j
I am anxious for all sufferers to know of vour treat
meat; and I shall advise all my friends afflicted with
this complaint to use it, as I know it will cure per-
manently.—Your faithfully, George Jordan.
'i he RICE Method of curing rupture is as near
perfect as anything could be, and a great many
remarkable cures have been effected. Every
runtm °d railway man should write Dr. YV. S. Rice

showing conclusively that his method cannot fail o!
a cure. It is a home treatment at small cost, with
out pain, danger, operation, or an hour’s detention

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Isn’t it about time that Australian newspapers
decided to preserve a solemn silence re the brutality of
Spanish bull-fights, which are mainly a slaughter of
broken-down horses ? In one hurdle race at Flemington
last Thursday four horses came to fatal smash —died at
once, or were mercifully shot soon afterwards. In the
steeplechase a horse fell at the first fence and broke its
neck Five other animals fell without happening to hurt
themselves, and the jockeys had singularly good luck.
One of them got slight concussion of the brain, but that
is a mere detail. No funerals resulted.
Hd all five of these horse fatalities occurred
through the sinful “ stiffness ” of the jumps in the steeple-
oourse, the demand for a removal of at least one danger-
ous obstacle might have moved the V.R.C. stewards to do
as they would be done by , if they themselves were com-
pelled, by the club’s rules, to ride over the big sticks
every’now and then.. Flemington.steeple-course is an
awfully cruel ordeal for even first-class jumpers
accustomed to easier conditions. As time goes on it be-
comes comparatively more and more dangerous, because
’chasers are more frequently raced elsewhere, over lower
fences, than in days of old. They travel faster and fall,
oftener—at Flemington. To every appeal for a reduction
In the death risk at headquarters the conservative
stewards answer “No.” They glory in Flemington’s repu-
tation for being a caution to jumpers. They dangle
the scalps of slaughtered gee-gees from their official
girdle, so to speak, and contemplate the stone walls and
Formidable timbers with the proud smile of heroes who
don't have to take part in a Grand National. A while ago
It was hoped that the stone wall near the abattoirs
(generally called “the Cathedral,” because no jockey
nears it without thinking of his soul and his hereafter)
would be removed from the track. “The cathedral”
doesn’t even give the occupants of stand and hill any
horrors for their money. They can’t see it, and don’tknow,
most of them, that it is the most fatal obstacle on the
track. In the unholy name of Sport The Bulletin herewith
lays its curse upon the Cathedral and other excessive
dangers of the V.R.O. jumping country. There has been
far too much bloodshed at Flemington these last few years.
Phantom riders of phantom steeds haunt the course at
night,’tis said, and shake skeleton fists in the direction
of the stewards’ stand. But the comfortably-plump
gentlemen who run the gory show have never held a mid-
night meeting with the ghosts. They take no risks of
any sort. Theirs not to do and die ; theirs but to drop
awgh, blow their noses and wonder why somebody blun-
dered.
* # * •
Of the five nags killed in “lopping” at Flem-
ington, Thursday last week, Watchdog was best known ;
he won a Newmarket for his then owner, the late Mr. W.
Forrester, and at his best could get over 6 fur. in won-
derfully good time. Subsequent to being purchased at
the dispersal of Warwick Farm stud, Watchdog was
schooled over the sticks, but never took kindly to that
game. He first saw light at Warwick Farm, and was by
Gibraltar from Rose Flaneur, by Segenhoe from Tuberose,
by Grand Flaneur.
* • * •
V.R.C. Gr.N. Hurdles twenty-second meeting
eventuates this Saturday. That real dyed-in-the-wool
champions are scarce among entrants is proven by small
margin (401b5.) difference between heaviest and lightest
loads. M.L. Battleaxe has top position with 11.12 —141bs.
less than Record Reign, hailing from same province,
humped prominently last season . Only question about
Battleaxe is, can he jump well ? Though there’s no public
showing to go on he must have done something to please
his party. In the matter of speed and bottom
Battleaxe is well up to w.f.a. form, as proved in last
year’s Auckland Century Stakes (11m.), which fell to him
with 8.12, while admitted top-notcher Seahorse finished
out of a place. Battleaxe is rising 8-years-old, and has
started 27 times this season, winning on five occasions.
His haul for the past year totals £1390. The nag’s trainer,
Percy Johnston, through wide experience at cross-
country work, should know what he’s about.
• • * •
Punters on the first two days of V.R.C.
National meet can spare themselves a lot of groping for
“ dead-birds ’’ by at once making up their minds to
follow the Miller and Manifold colors in every event and
every instance. They may have very bad luck. Probably
it will be not too bad. Possibly it will be very good.
Anyway, the fool who confines his hopes to the pair of
M.s, and indulges in a sneaking fancy for Shoddy, is
pretty certain to do as well for himself as the average
clever person with no settled policy in his mind, and all
sorts of information up his sleeve.
• * * •
At time of writing, it would seem that the
Holkar-Sindhia G.N. double-backers are badly scorched.
As short as 3 to 1 was accepted about the former’s pro-
spects straight out, and, wherever available, 100 to 2 on
the combination resulted in immediate business. Holkar
is now reported lame, and Sindhia shapes anything but
promisingly. Holkar comes from a family of jumpers,
and is full brother to those cracks, Mysore and Doondiah ;
but, all the same, appearances and portents are ominous.
Victor (list.), in same stable, may come now. He, like
Holkar, is a gelding by defunct Wellington, and is in
clinking condition. good performer, little
talked of, but worth watching, is Isis, winner of about ten
races this season (inclusive of doubles). At Border Town
(S.A.), after securing the Hurdles in each instance, she
won a flat race also. Saturday last, with 11.5, she
annexed Adelaide Hurdles from 10 others, and will only
carry 9.6 in the G.N. event.
• » * *
M.L. champion ’chaser, The Guard, among ac-
ceptors for the G.N. Steeple, to-day (Sat.), and appor-
tioned 12.7, is a fore-fronter of the first line. M.L. scribes
vote him possessor of every right to be considered among
the best leppers that ever raced in that State. Though
not a weight-carrier in looks, he humps crushing burdens
in startling fashion. During his career, M.L. Grand
National, Napier Park, Wanganui, and Great Northern
'chases have been won by him. The Guard is a gelding
by Vanguard Yattaway, by Nordenfeldt-Yattacy, by Yat-
tendon-Ecstasy. Money scooped by this prad last year
was £B5O, and this year £lOBO. The Guard is in the stable
of J. H. Prosser, who last year brought Advance and
Boreas to Australia. M.L. nags have not once succeeded
in either of the big jumping struggles at Flemington.
Donald (twice), Ixion, and Keppo filled places in the
hurdles, while Peter Osbeck, Dart, and Titokowaru have
steeplechase seconds credited in their records.
• * • *
Postmaster-General Drake is alleged to have
terrible designs on Ikey Mo. and co., and, with this end
in view, has instructed the Postal authorities to get par-
ticulars of such Mo.’s as use the Postal Dept, for business
purposes. So far, after long and exhausting inquiry, the
authorities have discovered the staggering number of
two in S.A. and six in N.S.W. ! Now, suppose P.M.
Drake were to put an office-boy on to copy the names of
the hundreds of gentle Mo.’s who invite business through
the post in the press of the Commonwealth ?
* * * *
A simple-minded litigant, suing for the return
of his deposit money on a readied-up horse, told a Melb.
Bench lately that “ he thought he could trust defendant,
as he was a member of the Salvation Army.” The reli-
gion that can induce a man to go straight in a deal in
horse-flesh has not yet been invented.

Deingomar won the Williamstown Steeplechase
last Saturday in taking style, and may cut a prominent
figure at Flemington anon.

All-round success of recent Auckland R.C. meet
is without a parallel in that body’s history. Tote showed
£5509 better than previous year, and year-to-year swell-
ings of prize-money since the ’95-’96 gathering have risen
from £10,105 to £21,670.

Announced that famous brood mare Duenna (21
years old) recently died at her owner’s stud, Bylong,
leaving a colt foal by Grafton. Duenna was by Lecturer
from Signora (imp.), dam of Cardigan and Mantilla by
Newcastle from Actress, by Stockwell, and the dam of
Amberite, Flintlock, Pelissier, Acetine, Watchfire, and
others.

Entries for M.L. Cup total 66—25 better than
last year, and 10 less than the record of ’9l. Three-year-
olds number 27, and embrace the best M.L. boasts.

Gozo-Pastime 3yr. old gelding My Mate, well-
backed, won Apprentices Handicap prize at Rosehill
Saturday. Medium sized and shapely but lop-eared, like
others of the same get. The nag galloped in a most
promising manner.
Rosehill Nursery, Saturday, fell to Randwick-
trainer-owrier “Sam" Fielder’s leased colt Famous, by
Grafton from the marvellous mare Vanity. He finished
with plenty of steam left. Was bred at Coolah by Mr.
Gleeson. Sentrona, who ran away with a similar event at
Canterbury two or three weeks previously, went out odds
on and figured well into the straight but her heavy load
crushed her.
• • •
Return match Australia v. Yorkshire opened in
fine weather, but on a soft wicket, Monday last week.
Twenty-six bats were out first day. Initial innings of
Australians produced 106 ; Darling (40) and Hill (34) doing
best. Yorkshire’s first essay brought 77. For England
Rhodes got 5 for 49, Haigh 4 for 18, and Hirst 1 for 34.
For us Trumble (6 for 17) and Saunders (4 for 58) did all
the bowling work. Second innings results were—Aus-
tralia 87, towards which Gregory compiled 42, and Hop-
kins (next best) 10. Haigh bagged 5 for 49, Rhodes 4 for
22, and Hirst 1 for 12. Yorkshire accumulated 72.
Trumble scattered stumps six times for 27, Saunders
twice for 26, and Howell once for 15. Australia thus
finished 44 best. This Australian success tops everything
previously done this tour. Wicket conditions were
apparently about the same for both sides, though Aus-
tralia had, perhaps, something to the good in getting to
work first and forcing things before the pitch became
worse, to which is attributable the comparatively big
totals and lengthy partnership of Darling and Hill, that
practically pulled the visitors through. None have
shaped with anything like the same effect as Trumble,
and his 6 for 17 is the best accomplished by him so far.
Noble seems to have dropped form with the bat com-
pletely ; his record to the close of the Yorkshire match
was only 142 in 15 finished innings—three times n.o.—
thus giving an average of 11 ‘B3. Rhodes got Noble
twice—once for a blob and next for a single. In the sub-
sequent match against an English eleven at Bradford M.
A. managed to notch one each time.
• • • •
“An English Eleven" met the Australians at
Bradford recently, and turned out to be the weakest
combination that has yet come forward to be butchered.
All the big counties needed their full strength elsewhere,
and the Australians piled up 402 first attempt ; Duff
making 182, Trumper 113, and Armstrong 38. England
hit up 240 first innings and 203 second. Australia’s
second innings saw 3 wickets down for 42, of which Hop-
kins made 11, and the not-out men, Armstrong and Duff,
14 and 11 respectively. In English first innings Noble
took 3 for 34, and Saunders 3 for 13. Second innings
Saunders 4 for 66, and Noble 3 for 67. Australia won by
seven wickets. The match was a solemn sermon on the
uncertainty of cricket. Four Austral wickets were down
for 35, and then the score jumped to 226. Just here
Trumper scored his thousand runs in 16 matches, and
beat Darling’s record for a whole tour. Trumper’s
aggregate is 1024, and with 21 matches yet to slog through
he has a fair chance of shifting the record above the 2000
mark. Darling reached the 24th match before making up
his little lot in ’99 ; Trumper has made his in an excep-
tionally short time in the wettest English cricket season
for 40 years. This was the first match played right
through without rain.
Things sporting seem coming to a head in
connection with Sydney Cricket Ground. The N.S.W.
Cricket Association, the Football Union and the Bicycle
organizations all complain against the Ground Trustees
for the grasping all-round charge of 20 per cent, on the
gross receipts for all events on their ground. This comes
hard on the sporting associations, for in the event of
failure through wet weather or other cause the Associa-
tion Ground collars its 20 per cent, just the same. The
cyclists and cricketers are quite at the mercy of the
Trustees, but the footballers have effectually kicked. The
Agricultural Society combined with the Cricket Ground
to force the Union to pay 20 per cent, as usual for the
coming interstate football matches with Queensland, but
the University people, whopossess afine oval, unexpectedly
cut under the combine. The Football Union has closed
with this offer, and all the matches other than some club
engagements will be played at the University. Which is
rough on the Sydney Cricket Ground subscribers, who for
their yearly £2 2s. are promised, among other attractions,
the pick of the football matches. Supposing other sports
follow suit, the members of the Cricket Ground, whose
one privilege is sitting in the pavilion, will have to fall
back on ping-pong to get value for their money.
* * * *
Inter-State football, Vic. v. S. A.., “ Australian ”
game, had a 10,000 gate in Adelaide. Jack Worrall went
over as manager of the Melb. 18, who were very strong
and were expected to win easily. They shone at hand-
ball, their punching being marvellous, and were, perhaps,
altogether more scientific; but for brilliant individual
work the home team scored nearly all the time. Lament-
ably bad kicking by S.A. kept their goal tally well behind
till nearly half-time, when they equalised. Again at
three-quarter time they were 11 points behind, but stay-
ing power brought them home by 11 goals 15 behinds to
9.8. It is only S.A.’s fourth win, as against 14 to Vic.
. • m * *
“ H.P.” : Re A.M.B. (28/6/’O2) on the kicking
of Sydney footballers. Why doesn’t he have a look at all
the clubs? What about Western Suburbs, with Stan.
Wickham and Johnson —undoubtedly the two finest
place-kickers in N.S.W. ? On June 21st at Uni. Oval,
Wickham kicked five goals from marks —two at a
distance between 70 and 80 yards, the others at between
40 and 50 yards. Johnson, a week before, at North
Sydney, kicked two marvellous goals from near half-way
—one almost on the touch-line. Western Suburbs have
won nearly all their matches by kicking, and are now
level with Glebe at the top of the competition. Further,
nowadays, there is much “ kicking for the touch-line,”
which is becoming an art in football.
* • • *
Out of 23 points to nil scored by Western
Suburbs against South Sydney, Saturday week, Wickham
(captain) kicked 5 goals from marks, totting up 20 points
on his own. The kicks were from all distances. There
are possibilities about being Wickham's dog.
“Old Timer”: Recently dead English sport,
Capt. Machell, once jumped from the floor to a mantel-
piece. In Sydney, years ago, a prominent bowler, in
evening dress, and without divesting himself of a single
garment, jumped backwards from the floor to the bar-
counter of the old Lighthouse Hotel, Druitt-st., to satisfy
a wager made on the spot a few minutes before. Height
was every inch of 3ft. 6in. This rather overshadows Capt.
Machell’s feat.
* • • *
Sheep-dog trials at Sydney Agricultural So-
ciety’s Grounds, last week, were not up to last year’s.
Time originally allowed to drive three sheep round two
flag-posts, through a hurdle, round a third post, twice
through a maltese cross in a fixed direction, and then into
a pen, was smins. less than last year’s limit (15mins.), but
the old order was reverted to on the final day, and still
not a dog did it completely—penning blocked them all.
Last year one tyke finished the whole business in TJmins,
Messrs. King and M’Leod’s Biddy came out best with 185
points. She operated in remarkably cunning fashion,
and got through everything but the pen-puzzle, though
one of the trio walked in but came out again. Messrs.
Maiden Bros.’ Darkie (182 points) was runner-up, and
worked more demonstratively than the rest, exhibiting
heaps of skill and patience. The next best score was
Messrs. Maiden Bros.’ Needle’s 175. Subsequently Biddy
and Needle contested a private match for £25 aside, and
Biddy gathered up the money with 277£ points to t’other’s
257 J. Biddy brought her charges to the outside of the
pen in the time which found Needle still battling at the
cross.
• • • «
Table-tennia craze still rages in Sydney, where
competitions are progressing and talked of to the complete
shutting out of ordinary tennis, croquet, puff-billiards,
and kindred nerve-racking pastimes. Ladies’ amateur
supremacy of the State finished at the Y.M.O.A. Hall,
Monday night, before a full house, mostly women. Two
couples of sisters—the Misses Dransfield and Maitland —
were the surviving four of a large entry. Big crowds
watched the play afternoon and evening, and but for the
interspersing of heats of the “Gentlemen’s First-class
Handicap,” would have been crushed with the monotony
of the thing, as most of the ladies sacrificed all to
“ safety ” ; the few who did show brilliant work went out
early—they took risks. Misses Nellie Dransfield and
M. Maitland met in the final, and, after a dragging,
wearying match that once looked like lasting all night, the
first-named won, 12-5. Champion male player, Horace
Elliott, easily annexed first pot in the competition
restricted to men, as not one of the others—though many
were capital exponents—could pace it with him. Said
that Elliott practises night and day, and this scribe is
quite ready to believe it. As The Bulletin goes to press,
a big tourney, promoted as a spec., wherein something
like 150 table-tennis cranks will compete for £l5O worth
of prizes, is starting at the Queen’s Hall, Sydney.
* • *■ *
Apropos pigeon-slaughterers Eales v. “Locks-
ley.” Strangely enough winner (Eales) has never suc-
ceeded in annexing N.S.W. supremacy, but on three
different occasions has defeated owners of that title in the
persons of Massey, “ Bobby ” Grant and “ Locksley.”
Conditions for these matches provided for 100 birds each,
and the consistency with which Eales performed is evi-
denced by the fact that he brought down 288 of the 300
shot at by him. Anyhow, a man who shoots pigeons
with a gun at catapult range and calls himself “ Locks-
ley ” deserves to be beaten. What the others deserve this
writer cannot declare in polite language.
* * * *
Inter-State bruising match, ’tween Melbourne
“ Billy ” Palmer and Sydney “ Charlie ” Frost at Golden
Gate A.O. (Sydney) Saturday night, was a warm, willing
scrap. Palmer rose inches higher and stretched inches
further than Frost, and was apparently a stone heavier.
Both fought all they knew right through. Frost thumped
hard lefts home, followed instanter by skull-shaking
rights on the jaw and point frequently. Palmer also
drove the left in stiffly and landed effectively with right
swings and crosses that brought the Sydney man down.
They slogged away, at times toe to toe, without flinchng
and as fast as one could punch the other, and came in
again to take and give some more. The air was thick
with bull-like roars and fierce offers of 6 to 4, 5 to 4, or
evens Frost—as the changing situations prompted. It
was the real stingo till, in the fifth round, one on
the point floored Frost, who went to his knees and then
sat down on his haunches, bent upon taking as much of
the lOsec. allowance as possible, but he left it too long,
and didn't begin to rise till after “ nine,” and was outed.
’Twas finely-cut counting indeed, but none the less fair,
and should always be so, though it rarely is. Referee,
are loth to end a fight too early, anxious that spectators
may get the worth of their coin, thus ignoring the faot
that the principals are fighting a match (and not an
exhibition bout) for money and reputation.
* * * *
Quite a swagger committee has been got together
to supervise the coming championship glove contest
between Felix and Doherty, in Melbourne. Guy Madden,
following in the footsteps of his waxed papa, takes a hand
in it, and several other mighty gentlemen sit with him.
This committee can declare the match “ snide,” and
scatter the prize amongst the hospitals if not satisfied
that the lanky black and his more lumpy opponent have
done their darnedest. Basto referees, and his judgments
are respected by such clean people as like boxing.
• * * *
Jeffries and Fitzsimmons are actually matched
to fight, at San Francisco, for the heavyweight champion-
ship of the world, probably on 4th July.
* * * *
Swimmer George Read easily won his first
race “ down-under,” a half-mile event at Surbiton,
in 9.12 J Some Sydney papers got sadly tangled over
the time. One evening sheet wisely opined that the
distance must have been 500 yards, as the time, com-
pared with previous performances, was impossible for
880 ; another felt perfectly satisfied that the message had
been mutilated in transmission, or something to that
effect. Truth is, however, that Surbiton is on the
Thames, and at a spot where the current runs strong
mostly, and extra strong at times, hence the swimmers
would be carried along at a great bat.
* * * *
Gabled from London, Monday, that at the
English Life Saving Society’s big carnival previous Satur-
day, witnessed by 30,000 people, Australians Read and
Cavill started in the mile championship for the Prince of
Wales’ Cup. Britisher Jarvis finished first, with his
fellow-countryman, Billington, second, Read third, and
Oavill fifth. Time (25min. 35sec.) though fast, is much
slower than that accomplished here by both Oavill and
Read last year, which fact indicates that they are not
themselves yet; Oavill could only have reached London
a few days before the race, and then weightily handi-
capped by lack of funds and opportunities to prepare.
Probably subsequent contests will see both shaping
nearer their Australian form. By cable also comes
information that at the same gathering Australian
Fred Lane won the big 150yds. handicap from scratch,
and possibly beat England’s and the world’s amateur
sprint premier, Derbyshire.
• » » *
At the Glebe Bicycle Club’s sports, held on the
21st ult., N. Oraig, on his Massey, won the half-mile han-
dicap in brilliant fashion.
* * * *
Never has such interest been taken in a road-
race in Australasia as in the coming Dunlop race from
YVarrnambool to Melbourne. Entries are already coming
in fast from country districts ; the fact that the first arid
second prizes, together with four others, were won by
country riders last year, evidently gives encouragement
to country cyclists to compete against the metropolitan
cracks.
• * * *
Popular notion that last year’s record (£153,399)
for tote biz. at Auckland R.C.’s Ellerslie meetings would
stand long has been knocked endways this season, as the
13 days’ racing saw £174,734 put through. An increase of
£21,335 on previous best, is staggering. Inference, of
course, is that A.R.O. prize-money will swell considerably
henceforth.
THE BOXERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A hundred quid is in the hall, and both are fit and strong,
And take the centre smartly when M‘Ewan thumps the
gong.
Like jealous cats we creep and spar, like cat’s his small
eyes burn.
We’ve a bad old score to settle, and a hundred jim to earn.
The crowd drops still and silent, but for me there’s not on
earth
Any living thing but Gander, and I fight for all I’m worth.
In thick, short neck, and arm, and flank each muscle is
awake,
And his wicked left is darting with the venom of a snake.
So, warily, until he feints, and, leading, lifts his guard,
Then, biffl my counter clips him on the cheek-bone,
straight and hard.
We mix it for a moment, and the lads behind him cry,
“ First blood 1 ” It is to Gander for a jab above the eye.
In corners while they fan and spray my flesh is gll elate
With fierce, hot lust of fighting that is passion but not
hate.
It’s vile and low and brutal to the ewe-necked man, I
know,
But before the crawler’s cunning I prefer a honest blow.
The gong ! And once again, with thew and nerve and eye
alert,
We slip about the ring, and hit, and clinch, and break
unhurt.
We fight now not for money or the glory of the hour,
But for the pride of race, and for the clean man’s; love of
power.
The go is willing, first and last. The backers yell delight
When I smartly lead or counter, or he swings his vicious
right;
And yet I feel him waiting, like a panther lying low,
And watch his tricks to out me with his famous body
blow.
Ten rounds are fought—ten clinking rounds. I’ve seen
him neatly spread
While eight was called. His people looked like mourners
o’er the dead.
But Gander rallied gamely, and was succoured by the
gong ;
The stab with which we parted left me rather in the
wrong.
It’s good to be in corners now, with towels briskly going,
And, like a mother’s blessing, feel the tender waters
flowing.
The gong! We spar. It seems to me the room is getting
dark.
He leads. The crowd gives one sharp yell. My left has
found the mark 1
But Gander grows before my eyes, he tops me like a wall.
As on a stack of bricks I feel my feeble counters fall.
His white set teeth are all I see. I want to turn and run,
And deep within my heart I know that Gander’s got me
done.
A stunning left, a heavy thud, then silence all around,
Till faintly, from a distance, comes a slow, familiar sound.
A voice is counting : “ Seven l . . . Nine ! ” Then breaks
a smothered shout.
As in a dream I hear it—Gander’s cobbers yelling :
“ Out 1”
I'm licked! I’m lickedl And stupidly I wonder what
is there —
A man upon his back. The chaps are pumping him for
air.
My legs are planted under me. I peer again, in doubt.
Good Lord 1 I’m up and waiting. It is Gander who is
oubl

Dyspepsia and Liver Disease.
Cubed by Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills.
Of the hundreds of letters received from people
who have derived a benefit from taking Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills, there are few that we
take more pleasure in publishing than the one
from Mr. J. H. King, 11 Jersey Terrace, Sydney.
His words have the ring of truth, and will carry
weight. They are as follow:—“It is with the
utmost pleasure I testify to the efficacy of Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills in a case of chronic
dyspepsia. Por many years I suffered from this
dire complaint, with its attendant evils ; have con-
sulted doctors, and tried inumerable remedies
with no beneficial result. At last, on the recom-
mendation of a friend, I purchased a bottle of
your pills (putting little faith in them), but was
surprised after a few days to find my digestion
decidedly improved, and now I am pleased to
inform you, after a course of your pills, I am
perfectly free from all symptoms, have a good
appetite, and can digest any kind of food.” Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a perfect blood
purifier, and a positive cure for biliousness,
indigestion, constipation, headaches, sallow com-
plexion, liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples,
boils and blotches, and for female ailments. Put
up in amber bottles, and the full name blown
thereon.*
Some time ago Good Words offered a
prize for a Coronation ode. Responses have come
from 1050 alleged poets, including 182 Australians.
Of the latter M.L. contributes 71, Victoria 59,
N.S.W. 20, S.A. 14, Tas. 11, Q. 25, and W.A. 2.
In the U.S. only one was sufficiently inspired by
the big prize to ode the event; while Canada was
stirred to the tune of 91.
“ Koorawatha” to The Bulletin
You err in stating (8., 21/6/’O2) that Generals de la
Bey and Olivier escaped from Cronie’s laager a few days
before the surrender. As a fact, De la Rey was at Coles-
berg, and Olivier at Stormberg, when Cronje’s laager was
being surrounded, and it was Christian de Wet’s plucky
attacks which sought to relieve the pressure. After
Magersfontein, De la Rey was ordered to Golesberg,
where Gen. French had gradually pushed the Boer forces,
under Piet de Wet (Christian’s brother), back to the Free
State border. Soon after De la Rey’s arrival several
sharp engagements took place, all more or less in favor of
the Boers, the result being that the British forces were
gradually forced back to Ochertang. Gen. French had
meanwhile been ordered to help in the relief of Kimber-
ley, and De la Rey was still in command when the Wilt-
shires were so badly cut up in their game effort to put a
good face on a general retreat. It was not until Oronje
had been surrounded for several days that De la Rey was
ordered north to attempt—in conjunction with Christian
de Wet—the relief of Cronje.

.... THERE IS NOTHING BETTER
James Brunton Stephens.
THE LAST OF THREE.
T ~ ~ , , e
James Brunton Stephens, the last of the three
whose names will be naturally bracketed m the
literary history of Australia, died on Sunday
morning, June 29th, 1902. It is twenty years since
Kendall was taken away from St. Vincent’s Hos-
. , ~ , .
pital to die m his friend s house. Gordon put
the muzzle of a rifle to his head down by
Brighton beach. Stephens had a longer, perhaps
a happier, life than the others, but—oh, the pity
of it! that his life, like the others, was mainly
wasted. He was created to write, to interpret, to
, . , v it r .1 • _ . ii’ nanar*
explain the parable of things to the present gener-
atom, to add to the artistic: inheritance of man-
kind. It was m him to have done as mucn tor
Australia as Bret Harte or Mark Twain did for
jhe „ M. of America—neither of these
SS ro<S«ic^“b„. a AuXafil'§id
not want, or was not permitted, to know him in
that way It could only hud him bread as a tutor,
, 7 : , J v-oa i r.f a
a .schoolmaster, secretary Minister, head of a
civil service department-Because^h« wa s ordained
to be a poet ie . wa P.‘ • y • thpVid- that
these and there is a curious irony ■mlthe.fact that
the placing him thus was regarded of
special grace and favor. There is nothing new m
it. Burns was made a guager bee-use he: had the
SSS
white angel side, the only poet of his time
Stephens was a Scotchman, born at Linlithgow.
He worked his way through school and college in
the old heroic Scotch style, and in 1866 made the
long sailing voyage to Australia. Nothing m the
old world seems to have impressed him. 1 here is
not a touch of “Caledonia stern and wild
throughout his verse. It was a maiden muse he
took to his tutorship in the bush, and the first
rhymes strung were Australian as the kooka-
hurra’s chatter.
The humor and the tragedy of hum m life im-
pressed him more than inanimate matters; indeed,
it may be said without detracts h that he never
caught the actual characteristics of the Australian
beauty or the Australian gloom. He was differ-
entiated from Kendall in that way. Humanity
magnetised him everywhere, and he saw the
greatest of Australian matters in the light of pos-
sible human effort. “The Dominion” is the
best of all his verse in this way, and the
comparison therein of the tide of Federal feeling
and the “unseen river” of the continent stands
out colossal amongst all efforts of Australian
imagination. Black gins and Chinese cooks are
graphic and true as B. E. Minns’ sketches ; and
those old “Boanah” and “Marsupial Bill”
ballads, which made several Xmas Queens-
LAN dees remarkable, are of things which will
remain.
But the one poem on which his literary fame
must rest is “Convict Once”; given the oppor-
tunity, encouragement, sympathy such natures
require, the author of that poem could have raised
a temple of art comparable to anything accom-
plished in his generation. The subject was un-
happily dragged out of the strictest taboo
of Australia, and its unrelieved sadness re-
cC;taPto W X m sSe„t^he do hi«h e " otth“i-
-of art, the bold imagery, the exquisite workman-
ship, even as the harrowing pathos and the unre-
lenting agony of that great poem, appeals irre-
sistibly. It is no more to be read for pleasure than
is the “ Antigone or the Cenci. Fate works
to an end in the old classic way, and the eutha-
nasia of the Queensland governess is inevitable
and terrible as the self-slaughter of the daughter
of (Edipus. Brunton Stephen put all his soul and
all his strength into that work ; each stanza he
carved in ivory or in ebony, and the whole stands,
and will stand, a model of what maybe done in
the handling of human subjects ainongst Aus-
tralian circumstances.
It is said frequently as foolishly that he ex-
hausted himself in that work. The truth is, he
gave us other kingly evidence of what was in him,
and we gave him in return—those things which
have been already mentioned. They, or at least
the more considerable of them, would never have
come but for the sympathetic recognition and aid
given by Gresley Lukin, as sole head of the
Courier and Queenslander papers. Lukin gave
Stephens what help these papers could give,
found him a field where he might at least assert
himself locally, and forced recognition in a prac-
tioal way from the Ministry of the time. Through
press influence Stephens was taken from the
school-desk and made corresponding clerk to
Tbomas M’llwraith (not exactly Milton and
Cromwell over again !). Later he became Under-
Secretary, and found as much inspiration and
opportunity to write verse, as Mr. Critchett
Walker feels inclination.
Ihus the man was lost, and the full measure he
brought into the world spilled. How he suffered
in th B at i osing and spilling is amongst things that
w in never be told. Those who knew him most
intimately know that his life was one of stern and
continuous self-repression. He had a Devil, as
ve m ° st of that company, but he kept him down
through years which came very close to three
score and ten . He fulfilled all fche uaua]ly
recognised obligations of life, and died in his bed
an esteemed and worthy citizen. But what of the
might-have-been ? What if those great and
wealt} W newspaper proprietaries, whose best-
P a -™S <? la Vf , have been the brains of literary
workers had in this case as m others done what m
of Heaven and earth it came to them tQ
do ? what the o ld QUEENSLANDER began to
, . ~ , , c nlu -t*. .i
do ln fche before lt ,’ as all fcbe others > lefc the
, Stranply.eUgh, theS.lt
. for , that might-have-been in a kindly-enough
leader on the poet The constant anxieties
depending upon the man who has to earn his
§ bread as well as immortality must have
interfefed to a large extent with Mr. Stephens*
fertility as a poet. The main business of a man
abu ' dant if gifted as Mr . Stephens was to con-
tibto go fa / a | he could to the world’s literature
the literature which the world cannofc spare .
Unfortunatel for us> Mr . Stephens had to con-
"imscl, and Ms P«i t io„ before indulging hi,
But why did nofc « us ,» that particular “ us,” so
arran g e out of its abundance in another sort that
daily bread should have been available with-
ouj. renunciation of the golden apples which
m {ght have been plucked for the cheering and
healing of mankind? He was worth more than
daily bread for even a little prose work on any
g re at daily paper. But there was no place found
f or him, and so he dragged out his dreary life,
g t rvi ck his one full chord and his subsequent
broken music, and died.
Personally, Brunton Stephens was a man whom
few knew, who allowed very few to get inside
the husk of commonplace he chose to wear. He
was utterly unselfish, knew nothing of jealousy,
appreciated everything everybody achieved in his
own art, and understood what they intended.. He
was a good comrade on rare Johnsonian nights,
and a keen fighter when, with much artful sym-
pathy, he was induced to draw in earnest. The
bit of good old Scotch granite in his constitution
kept him from crumbling or yielding, and held
him up to the unremitting, strenuous effort re-
quisite to the earning of the daily bread, and the
renouncing for ever the golden dream. And so he
died, the esteemed head of a great State dep-irt-
ment; but will live in other ways. “Godolphin
Arabian,” “AHundred Pounds,” and “Fayette”
are with things which do not matter much,
“ Mute Discourses ” are not strong or deep with
the fullness of life; but the august Dominion
verse, the classic and tragic “ Convict Once,” and
the “ bits of blackfellow ” will endure while the
wattles bloom, and the myalls shine, and the she-
oaks wail, and old Kosciusko shows his winter
shroud and summer flower crown to the sky.

London Daily Chronicle says “ Australia
does not permit consumptive persons to be

The Jenkins Ministry is retrenching S. A.
civil servants wholesale although a classification
Board, after going right through the service, has
reported it to be undermanned and underpaid.

F. T. Derham, with his Tory crust on
him like a 90-year-old turtle, wobbled out from
amongst his biscuits, t’other day, to be made
president of Vic. Employers’ Federation, and de-
livered once more the oration he has been re-
hashing over and over again for the last 12 years.
Derham’s everlasting speech consists of four main
spasms. (1) That the Employers “abhor sweat-
ing ; ” (2) that conciliation and arbitration, and
“all other socialistic legislation, must be strenu-
ously combated.” No. 3 is all about the imaginary
capitalist who came from Labrador or Greece to
start a large business, but wouldn’t when he saw
the awful way this country went in for new ideas ;
and the fourth and most terrible spasm sets forth
that the “socialistic legislation” aforesaid has
attracted so much of the country population to
cities, that the poor farmers are unable to get men.
It is no longer necessary for the Melb. papers to
send a reporter when Derham speaks—they
just print his four spasms again.

Australian book published for a long time than

writings of Harry Morant (“The Breaker”).

verses which it contains. The book gives a photo-
graphic reproduction of the “ copy ” of Momnt’s

The Canada Cycle and Motor Co., of 357
ileorge-street, Sydney, have instituted, in connection
with their cycle trade, a most up-to-date and splendidly
stocked sports department. Nothing has ever existed in
Australia on a par with it. The department is controlled
by an expert who has served years in learning every
point of physical culture. For a few pounds—for much
iess money in fact than the average man spends on wine
and cigars in six months—anyone can be fitted up with a
svstem of home exercises which will give exceptional
vigor to the body and extraordinary spring to the mind
and will. In this world, the battle goes to the strong
without exercise. One great boom is a practically noise-
less punching bag. Prior to the arrival of this noiseless
bag, a man could not punch the bag without kicking up
a devil of a row, causing the neighbour and his wife to
rush upon the scene screaming that the twins had taken
a fit through fright. Another boom is a rowing machine
which affords better exercise than rowing itself. Not
only that, there is no fear of falling out of it into cold
water and getting eaten by sharks. A third is the Sandow
spring-grip dumb-bell which makes you strong enough
in the hand to split a bullock’s forehead with one punch.
A fourth is the Sandow exerciser, which not only braces
the system everywhere but also adds greatly to a man’s
personal appearance. A fifth is Sandow’s embrocation
which cures all muscle-pain or stiffness. In short, the
company represents the brain of the world as applied to
physical culture. Write the co. for books and informa-
tion.*

The up-to-date business man appreciates healthy
competition—the keener the better. It acts as a
stimulant, and increased success means new business
enterprise. Such a man is Sydney’s well-known jeweller,
Mr. A. Saunders, whose name has become synonomous
with excellence and cheapness all over Australia, for all
that the jeweller’s art can produce. Sis extensive
business premises, in George-street, Sydney, opposite
Redfern railway-station, contains show-rooms which
absolutely defy faithful description. For wedding-
presents, birthday-gifts, presentations &c., &c., Mr.
Saunders offers a choice as wide and varied in price and
quality as could be desired. And, in addition to his
business as manufacturing jeweller and silversmith, he
has opened a new optical department, and has made
arrangements for testing eye-sight free of charge, and the
manufacturing of lenses to suit all defects of the eye.
Opera and field glasses in great variety, and at reasonable
prices. Country clients are invited to send for his
beautifully illustrated catalogue, which will be forwarded
post free.*

Business, Bobbery, etc.
The output of that big Melbourne beer factory,
Oarlton brewery, is said to be on a par. with that of
Tooth’s brewery, Sydney—viz., about 2000 hogsheads per
week. Think of it! — 100,000 gallons, or 800,000 pints
every seven days ! Oarlton does two-thirds of the beer
trade of Melbourne, and pays £lOOO weekly in excise
duty. Lieut.-Col. Ballenger, the man who makes the
mixture, draws the salary of a Cabinet Minister.
* * * »
The cable announces an alleged discovery of
means to utilise atmospheric electricity. The idea has
been already exploited in Sydney, about the same time
as “ atmospheric gas.” The “ inventor” had a room in a
boarding-house, and was acquainted with the atmos-
pheric-gas man, who possibly suggested the idea to him.
Writer sold him the battery, which was the source Of
power, and, in all innocence, fixed it up, and three small
incandescent lamps, in his bedroom. The .battery was
hidden behind a screen in a corner, and the wires con-
cealed neatly, and controlled by a switch. Then the in-
ventor went round and announced that he had discovered
how to obtain electricity from the air, and as there are
plenty of flats in Sydney, to whom the word elec-
tricity is a magic term, he ,Jhad no difficulty in
persuading flies to walk into his parlor. He
first .of all pushed a small gas-pipe through
a hole in the wall of his room, and went out on to the
balcony and poured some liquid ammonia inside. In a
few minutes the pungent fumes of the chemical filled the
room. This, the inventor said, was the electricity
materialising. When it had got strong enough he
switched on the batl&rv current, and the room was
immediately brilliantly illuminated, and the mugs
swallowed all the patter that he poured into them, and
he got an order from one tradesman to light up his shop,
receiving £4O on account. A plumber was called in and
given orders to run pipes all over the building, atmo-
spheric electricity being different from the commercial
article and disdaining wires. The inventor then slipped
his cable and made tracks Of course, the present
discovery may not be of that sort, probably isn’t, for the
inventor seems to take it seriously.

•‘Outlaw”: Saw two women looking into a
second-hand shop in RusseJl-st., Melb., and cackling, with
handkerchiefs to mouths. The fat old dame who kept
the place came out when they had passed on, and after
carefully looking over the articles in the window
ap arently decided that there was nothing humorous
about the goods. Strolled up and saw an ancient
mattress on a still older bedstead, and attached to the
latter a card with the announcement, “ Buggies for
hire.”
* * * *
Told by a Sydney suburban grocer. Small boy
got credit to 35., and the money hail been owing for two
months, when one day the boy turned up with 3s. 6d. to
make purchase. The grocer impounded 3s. in payment
of debt, and sent the boy hack with the 6d. Soon, a
stylishly-dressed young lady appeared, and indignantly
inquired what ttie grocer meant. “ She had sent for
certain articles by a boy who lived near her house, and
the grocer had stolen 3s. of her money.” The grocer ten-
dered humble apologies, told the story of the back debt,
and did a general crawl. The lady allowed herself gradu-
ally to be placated, received the 3s. in cash and Bs. worth
of goods on credit, and went. Then the grocer’s boy,
whom he had just cuffed into a corner, smilingly ex-
plained that the young lady and the small boy were sister
and brother.

The dispersal of the Ward-Cole estate in Mel-
bourne brought under the hammer the ferry-steamer
Gem, and under notice the unique record of that neat
little vessel. She was built in’6B, in a small yard where
Queen’s Bridge now stand, by Messrs. Sinclair (an engi-
I neer) and Colquhoun (a ship’s-carpenter), and her bottom
was of Tasmanian blue-gum, with kauri topsides. Good
judges aver that, with her lately-inserted triple-expansion
engines, she is as good now as on the day she was
launched. Her first, and, up to two years ago, her only
skipper was Capt. Robert Watson. ' For 34 years this
little product of loeai industry cleared her owner from
£BOO to £lOOO a ye ir, while from Jan. Ist to May 31st this
year her net earnings averaged £244 a month ' She was
knocked down cheap at £3OOO.

“Merool ”: Even amongst insurance - agents
there is sometimes a genius. About 20 years back the
Northern parts of Queensland were toured by an agent
who, at each township, gave a banquet, to which every-
one with money was invited. Then, when the company
were comfortably drunk, the genial entertainer would
start in and generally succeeded in making a good
“ bag.”
• • • *
Related that a well-known Sydney firm lately
advertised for a grocery-hand—apparently just to see how
many of their own men answered it. Anyhow, three of
them who did answer it were immediately “ sacked.”
• » * *
“ Ell.” : A N.S.W. farmer lately told me that
he averages nearly 30 letters, etc., a week sent him by
pill-men, machine-agents, wool, grain and produce firms,
etc. He reckoned the total postage on the lot he received
last year ran in‘o over £6. What an enormous sum must
be spent in this manner !
* * * *
“ Serulia ”: A Port Douglas man whips the cat,
per local press, over a small consignment of fruit sent
over on spec, to Sydney, on which consignee duly lost
time, labor and money. He is one of the many who prefer
the congested market far away to exploiting the back
places nearer home. Small consignees mostly lose
through freightage, bad stowage, and agents’ exes.—too
lazy, ignorant, or conceited to cater for local needs—
besides it sounds big to talk of “my agent in Sydney.”
Meanwhile the intelligent Chow hawks stringy, half-
rotten bananas, &c., to local factories and townlets,
realises famine prices, and can’t meet the demand of
the beef-and-flour-sickened inhabitants. Same applies
re wine and brandy exported, while North pays fid. and
Is. for rottenest drinks on earth. Tea is also rotten.
Room for a distributive agency with brains.
An Adelaide money-lender lent a needy Gentile
£5 10s. on a note-of-hand, and, when it became due,
charged 10s. a month for renewing it. In this way he
received £4 10s., and then, in default of payment, sued
for £6 (the original sum, plus one month’s interest).
Commissioner Russell described the transaction as
“ simply extortion,” and gave a verdict for £l, the
balance of the loan after deducting the £4 10s. paid in
interest. Also, he entered a nonsuit so far as the sure-
ties were concerned.
“ Will J. *: There’s a living to be made out of
the old tins that litter our country commons. A man
who lately relieved Warracknabeal (Vic.) of its rusty dis-
figurements, sometimes attains to a tenner a week. He
travels from place to place, makes a gigantic pile of all
the tins he can collect, liberally intersperses brushwood,
burns the pile out, rakes out the plates of solder, and
realises.

“Joss ” : There has been much in The Bulletin
lately about the tricks of the wily Hindu hawker, but
nobody hitherto has suggested a method of preventing
him from forcing his goods on unprotected females in
the country districts. These hawkers obtain their annual
licenses, when possible, at the nearest licensing Court ;
otherwise they come to the city and apply there. Before
a license can issue, the applicant must produce a certifi-
cate of good character. And this guarantee is always
signed by the wholesale people who supply the hawker’s
wares. The police, also, are supposed to make inquiries
about the man’s character ; but, as they never go farther
than the people who have already testified thereto, the
whole thing is a howling farce. Country people who have
been intimidated by the black trash should lay a com-
plaint before the magistrate so that the report could lie
forwarded to the Licensing Court and used agains! the
offender upon his next annual application for lie use
renewal.

The Perfection of the Bargain.
A bargain, in order to be perfect, should be a
purchase which is profitable to the purchaser, and
at the same time, not a loss to the seller; We
may therefore call this present offer of the “ EX-
CYCLOP JEDIA BRITANNICA ” an absolutely
perfect bargain. The Daily Mail can afford to
sell the “ Encyclopaedia Britannica ” at half price
for this almost obvious reason : that the demand for
it at such a bargain pr ce is sure (in fact, has
already proved itself) to be extremely large,
enabling, therefore, the printers, binders, im-
porters of leather, manufacturers of paper, and so
forth, to base their cost on very extensive orders,
which, as every business-man will at once re-
cognise, makes it pos ihle to produce each set of
the volumes at a much smaller price than if only a
few sets in all were to be prepared. From the
buyer’s point of view it must be evident, without
argument, that the bargain is almost, if not quite,
unique. You obtain your books at half-price.
Not only that, yeu obtain then, upon a system of
payment which is suited to anyone posse-sed of
even the most moderate means, and which enables
the buyer to have the use of the books while he is
still sending the monthly instalments of 125., by
which he completes their purchase. .
-♦ —-
The Purchase Price.
But, you will naturally ask, although it is very
easy for me to seiid in ss. as a first payment, and
to pay the remainder of the price on such easy
terms as those named, yet still the price of the
book remains as a whole as high as before, and
can I really afford to pay such a sum for one book,
however desirable, however valuable, and how-
ever useful it may be? The answer to your
question is this—that the Daily Mail is offering
you this great work, not only on a system of pay-
ment most convenient and most simple, but it is
doing far more than that, it is offering you the
work at less than half the regular price asked for
it by the publishers.
+
How to Understand the
Bargain.
We cannot, of course, give here either a full
description of the book or complete details of the
bargain. These, however, can be obtained by
everyone who will accept the invitation we cor-
dially offer. You may put entire confidence in
the illustrated Prospectus and other descriptive
matter which will be sent on application.
The Daily Mail has only one regret in offering
this unique book-bargain in Australia, and it is
this—that they are only able to sell at this price,
and on this system of payment, a limited number
of sets. The demarnd has already been very large,
and is steadily increasing day by day, and
the result of this in the near future must be
the withdrawal of the offer, and the consequent
disappointment of those who hesitate too long,
and who send in their orders too late. Free
delivery as far as Sydney.

THE BOOKCASE.
The case is substantially made and neatly
finished. It is about 21in. square and 3ft. high,
and is mounted on castors, so that the ENCY-
CLOPAEDIA may be brought to the side of a
writing-table when in use, or left in a corner when
space is needed for other purposes. The volumes
are arranged on two opposite faces of the Book-
case in so compact a form that any desired article
may be consulted without trouble or loss of time.
The side of the Bookcase is fitted with an adjust-
able slope, on which a volume in use may be placed
at any desired angle. It is entirely optional
whether the Bookcase be ordered or not. It is,
however, a great protection to the books as well
as a convenience in their daily use, and a hand-
some piece of furniture.

The Sixpenny Habit.
Systematic saving is an excellent habit to
acquire, not only because it is the high road to
fortune, but for other reasons. It is so easy to
spend odd sixpences, and, in spending them, un
consciously to fritter away not only shillings, but
pounds. If we could only tot up at the end of a
year the number of odd sixpences we have spent
at odd moments on odd things, we should be
astonished at the sum total. Not only this, but
we should feel that, if we had not so fre-
quently put our hands into our pockets and
taken out 6d. to spend, if, instead, we had laid
that sixpence away—we should not only have
saved a considerable sum, but we should have been
able to purchase, out of these savings, many luxu-
ries and comforts which we have denied ourselves,
believing we were unable to afford them.
Sixpenny Books.
During the last year or so many publishers
have taken to issuing sixpenny paper-covered
volumes, many of them, as regards their contents
at any rate, of very high quality. We can hardly
call it waste of a sixpence to invest it in a six-
pennyworth of good literature. But the time has
now come when it is possible for a man—by the
systematic saving of sixpence a day—to acquire
for himself not only good literature in the form of
sixpenny books, but the very best of good litera-
ture in the form of one of the finest works, both
in quantity and quality, and handsomely BOUND,
that has ever been published. Supposing—and
the supposition is not very extravagant —that you
have ss. by you, which you want to put to some
use, and naturally to the very best use you can ;
and supposing that you see your way, in addition
to this, to saving sixpence, or rather less than six-
pence, a day, you can now acquire the complete
and unabridged volumes of the “ENCYCLO-
PAEDIA BRITANNICA” by so doing.
4
A New Feature.
For the purpose of encouraging the more general
use of the ENCYCLOPAEDIA BRITANNICA
as a means of self-culture, a Twenty-sixth Volume,
entitled a “ Handbook of Systematic Readings, ”
has been prepared. The Handbook Volume is
uniform in style with the other volumes of the
work, and contains over 230 pages of practical
suggestions, greatly enhancing the educational
value of the work. The “ Handbook ” will be
sent FREE to every subscriber.

Hope’s Hill, Southern Cross (W.A.), after long
silence, announces a return of 4040 z. from 1410 tons
including cyaniding—which is quite unpayable. Pro-
-30-head battery, was taken over in
96 by Scottish Westralia from “ Captain ” Oates and Nat
Harper, ex-Mayor of Kanowna, and floated in London for
£160,000. Four Griffin mills were installed, under
Manager Honeyman, hut were never used, as test crush-
lngs in the old battery proved unremunerative. Co. was
then reconstructed, and Arch. Wilson, manager of Fraser’s
mine, superseded Honeyman. He built himself a mansion,
ordered a 40-head mill from Martin, of Gawler, and a
magnificent 250-h.p. Robey engine, and discarded the
unused Griffin mills. Wilson’s reign was short and sweet
after the new battery got to work, and the mine was again
closed down as unpayable. The return now announced
should finally convince John Bull, or rather, Sandy Scot,
that Hope smiles but to deceive.
* * * *
Long Reef, Lennonville (W.A.), whose 20-head
mill was closed down in March for a .six months’ spell
owing to all developed ore being depleted, is to make an
immediate start on 2000. tons from the Galtee More lease.
Developments lately have been good, and as the co. now
owns several mines, shares should be a good spec, for a
long shot.
* * * •
Management of Golden Link (W. A.) publishes
in local press the report of a sensational find of a 12ft. lode
on the Eclipse lease, assaying 2oz. 4dwt. on surface.
Writer made exhaustive inquiries, and is quite satisfied
that alleged richness is mythical. Two parties of
tnbutors have tested this lode thoroughly by battery,
and find it unpayable, averaging only 7dwt. per load of
30cwt. They have given notice to abandon their tribute.
The erection of May’s plant will kill the pretensions of
the Eclipse and its neighbor, the Croesus Proprietary.
• * • *
It is definitely announced that Bewick, Moreing
and Co. have been appointed consulting engineers to the
Ivanhoe. They now control more than a score of W.A.
mines. Moreing, the moving spirit of the firm, is an old
Cornstalk.” This monopoly of mine-control is viewed
with much disfavor by the old school of mine-managers
who think it highly improper that “ school-boys” should
be put in charge of mines. There are many people who
it better to employ intelligent, educated “ school-
boys ” than the old-fashioned practical pragmatical men
who never had no lamin’," and take pride in the fact.

Chaff er’s (W. A. ), owing to the favorable develop-
ments in the adjoining Golden Horseshoe on their nor-
thern boundary, are to start serious exploration bv driving
at the 400, 600 and 1000 ft. levels. Mine is now in charge
F. W. Cape, well and favorably known in Sydney.
This co. a year ago floated the Great Fingall South Blocks
adjoining Fingall Consols, but diamond drilling so far has
located nothing of value. Bore at 991 ft. passed through
reef 45in. wide worth 2dwts., and was continued forJJOOft
more without making any other find.

The old White Feather Reward, Kanowna
(W.A.), which was one of the earliest of the State’s mines
to start a battery, is now being taken vigorously in hand
under the direction of Nat Harper, who has been so suc-
cessful with the adjoining mine, and previously with
Frasers. A new main shaft is being sunk ; present
djpth, 500 ft. Mine’s returns in 1896 averaged over 2oz.
>er ton, but were sweetened by pickings from the rich
leaders on the adjoining Red Hill, where Tom Doyle is
now getting such sensational returns Co. never paid
a div., and none in sight.
