At the Associated (W. A.), the rich chute at the
500 ft. level, Tetley’s shaft, is still giving some good dabs.
The latest manager credited to this mine is Gleisberg,
who formerly had the dual management of the Lady
Looh, Coolgardie, and Half-mile Reef, Bardoc. Mine
and machinery in a terrible muddle, and the man who
gets things ship-shape will deserve heaps of credit.
* * * *
Good progress is being made on the Atlas, Kal-
pini (W.A.), under the control of A. W. Irvine, a capable
man from Queensland. Mine has been opened up by a main
underlay shaft, now down 300 ft., carrying a large payable
quartz reef all the way. . Some 200 ft. of driving has been
done at 200 ft., with highly satisfactory results. Two
a ijoining blocks have recently been acquired, and a 10-
nead battery has been ordered. Orushings expected to
average an ounce.
Latest published return from Peak Hill (W.A.)
is 30200 z. from 3317 tons, 55 per cent, of the gold coming
from accumulated tailings. Darlington Simpson, sponsor
of mine, who recently visited it, assures shareholders that
better results may be looked for ; but his previous pre-
dictions went far “ agley.”

Shareholders of Hannan's Proprietary Develop-
ment Co. are again buoyed up with hopes of what may be
expected in their numerous leases. Oroya East is to get
the B. 11. Extended and also the Iron Duke lodes. The
former is very patchy, and the latter is striking south-
west in the centre of the Oroya property and underlying
west, right away from the Oroya East. The Queen of the
VVest is to get the “ rich” lode being worked in the Iron-
sides North, but “ richness ” is a matter, of degree, and in
this instance is nearly freezing-point. The Croesus ProD.
chute is also being dangled before long-beguiled share-
holders, but it is a stage-property bunch or carfots. Co.
has been pursuing a will-o’ : the-wisp since 1895, and is
still on the same track.

“ Shaker ” : Some of the joys of mining in Cape
York Peninsula: Prices at Ebagoola, 35 miles from port
over a good dra.y-road Flour, 3d. per lb. ; suvar 5d •
butter (tinned), 2s. 6d.; beef, sd. and 6d. ; and a 21b.’
loaf, 9d. 1 Other things in proportion, except grog
which is, relatively, cheap and powerful. Gold (allu-
,vial), 555. ; wages, when obtainable, no higher than
at Charters Towers or Croydon. Sixty per cent of
the population sick, with the nearest doctor at
Cooktown—a week’s trip by special buggy, with the
alternative of a possible three weeks in a wind-jammer.
The Inspector of Mines resides at Charters Towers (600
miles off), and when th 1 serious accident—there have been
two close calls already—comes, the lucky man will be
the man who is killed outright. Not enough rain to run
some of the main creeks this year, and every prospect
of a water-famine.

~ ‘‘W.F.” writes:—“Outside of Walhalla and
Matlock (Vic.), the Oassilis mine near Omeo is the biggest
thing in Gippsland mining at present. The co. has an
up-to-date crushing, concentrating, roasting, and chlori-
nating plant, and, better-still, a reef worthy' of it.
Worked from a tunnel, the bottom level shows'the reef, a
dense, black,, refractory mineral, to be as rich as, and the
chute longer and wider than, at the levels above, being
over 200 ft. long and tapering, comet-fashion, from 12ft”
in width at the north end to a thin wedge and, finally, a
blank in the south. The school of mines management is
visible in the main shaft now being sunk, so as to cut the
lode at about 100 ft. The mine appears to be officered to
an unreasonable extent, there being an. official even to
measure the holes bored. Writer reckons that with shaft
sunk further from the lode that scrip (42,000) would be a
reasonable gamble at a third of present value of 235. The
Maud and Homeward Bound, Glen Wills, is the only other
working show of note. Competently managed, the co.
has paid a few Od. divs. from the upper levels, besides
making a small fortune for some of the tributors,
but the bottom level—the fatal bottom level—shows the
reef to be mullocky and poor. The co. is putting
in a still lower level, which will either break the mine or
make it—most likely break it. Scrip, which touched £l,
now barely saleable at 4s. At Sunnyside the water is
running out of the mouth of the United Brothers’shaft.
A really good mine, with three distinct gold-chutes
Plant too weak for the work, and directors not stron
enough to resist temptation to pay divs. with mone^
made from the tributors. A good mine and a poor crowd,
who have also had 110 acres of the best prospectable land
on the field locked up for years."

* * * a
\ non sense j s written and talked in
London about Australian mining tenures, and the cry is
being taken up by a few busybodies in Melbourne. It is
asserted that the leasing system gives no security. The
answer to this is that no instance can be cited where a
leaseholder, who could show that he has held the land for
legitimate mining purposes, has been dispossessed. No
bona-fide application for suspension of labor conditions is
ever refused ; if anything, too much leniency is displayed
in this direction, it is argued that the expenditure of
so much money in plant ought to give the holder of the
land a right to it in perpetuity. Under such a system
the owner of half-a-dozen mining plants could secure a
monopoly of half the continent, just as hundreds of
miles of the richest lands in N.S.W. were stolen, per
medium of dummy frame-houses. Let the Londoner com-
pare the conditions under which he is allowed to carry on
mining operations in England with those of Australia,
and he will see how unreasonable are his complaints
against the latter. It is amusing to find American adven-
turers voicing the same complaints. In Australia an
American is at liberty to take out a-miner’s right and
exercise all the privileges which it confers. In America
an Australian is treated as an alien ; he is not allowed to
“ locate” a claim or to be registered as the holder of an
interest in one until he has resided for a certain length of
time in the country and then forswears his former alle-
giance. Australian “ insecurity of tenure ” is a hideous
wild-cat shriek.

“F.0.H.” : Par. (8., 24/5/’O2) about an Auck-
land directorate recalls the following: At a directors’
meeting at the inception of one of the old Etheridge co’s
(when that boom was on some years back in London) one
of the “ guinea-pigs ” could not understand how there
could be so much water below as to necessitate sending
out pumps, and at the same time none on the surface to
crush with. No amount of explanation could get it into
his thick head, but at last the brilliant idea struck him to
obviate the necessity for sending out the pumps by
“ putting the mill down the mine and utilising the water
there for crushing purposes. At this luminous sugges-
tion the old N.Q, prospector who had sold them the mine
nearly died of laughing, much to the disgust of said g.p.,
who could not see the joke.
“Assez”: A Tas. tin co. spent £35,000 on its
property. When the mine looked like a failure, the co.
had a church built, and advised the employees to pray.
Had it prospected the surface within a radius of five
chains of its ore-dressing plant, it would have discovered
one of the richest tin lodes ever found in Tas. Not by
prayer alone is a mine to be saved.
- *■ ' - * * *
Hackett, Perth newspaper “boss,” tells an
English interviewer that he has had exceptional facilities
to get first-hand information from the mines but “ finding
it quite impossible to fathom the depth of the market
manipulat ions he has ceased t oinvest any money in Westra-
lian gold shares.” A denser, less-informed man than
Hackett should know that W. A. shares are not yet within
reasonable distance of the investment stage. Take the
best of the mines and apply to it the principles which are
supposed to govern a so-called mining investment and the
result will be that present market value is 50 per cent,
above the mark. The man who could really fathom the
depth of market manipulations would become a multi-
millionaire in record time—a few clicks of the cable
would do it. It is the man who thinks that he has
fathomed the mystery who goes down like a sounding
lead.
* » * *
Sydney Barrack-street Savings Bank has, at
last, made a lot of people happier by discarding its
obsolete system of receiving deposits. You now simply
-fill-up the-usual bank deposit-slip; hand it across the
counter to one of the tellers —there are now two receiving
tellers—who enters the amount in your accompanying
deposit-book, and you go. No more weary wait; no more
curse-talk ; no more injured corns.
• * * •
Charles M'Kinley, Zeehan, writes :—“ As a
tributor, who for the last few years has been tributing on
Zeehan silver-lead field, I should like to tell the public of
Bome of the difficulties that have to be contended with
by such of us as have low-grade ore to sell. We have on
the field a Smelting 00. and a Metal 00. (both German)
who will take your ore on the most liberal terms (for
themselves), and the tributor will be unusually fortunate
if he obtains for his ore anything approaching its value.
It has often been said that during a former period of in-
tense depression tributing saved this field. That is quite
true, but at that time the Smelting Co. had not started
business here. There were several ore-buyers and some-
thing like fair competition existed. The Smelting 00.
has got contracts with various mining co.’s to take all
their ore ; and the co.’s can only let tributes with a
similar condition attached. The result is that we are at
the mercy of the Smelting 00. There are generally de-
ductions sufficient to absorb over 4-sths of the assay-
value of £5 per ton ore. If the Tas. Govt, would adopt
a policy calculated to foster mining industry, it would
erect a smelting plant and treat the ore at a fair price to
. leave a profit just covering interest on the outlay; it
would also reduce railway freights. As things are the
Germans make a very good thing out of the business,
by practically depriving the miner of what ought to be his
legitimate profits.”
[There’s nothing novel in Mr. M’Kinley’s growl. It is
heard all the world over. Every ore-seller thinks that every
ore-buyer takes more than his fair share of the apple.
There can be no doubt that Australian smelting cos’,
charges and deductions are most unreasonable. Owners
answer the.assertion by saying. that the works do not
pay. If that be so the reason is that trade is not
encouraged. . Expensive staffs have to be maintained
While works are run at half capacity for the want of ore.
When big works were established in N.S.W. it was sup-
posed that a fillip would be given to mining for smelting
ores, but the reverse has been the case. The smelting
co.’s do not give a small man a ghost of a show. Their
purchase statements are longer than a lawyer’s bill of
costs, and every line of them means so much grab. The
copper miner of to-day, for example, is much worse off
than he was 20 years ago. He could then get better
terms from the late Lewis Lloyd—who was not likely to
make contracts which meant a loss to himself—than he
can get from the big co.’s of to-day. With copper at £53
per ton a miner dare not consign less than 16 per
cent. ore. The smelter’s initial deduction of two
units for assumed loss is an outrage. There is no
such loss. If slags were to show more than % per
cent, copper furnacemen would soon get the shoot.
Then the charge of 2s. 6d. per unit—or £l2 10s. per ton of
copper—for realisation is simply barefaced robbery.
Contracts can be made in Sydney any day in the week for
the disposal of copper at a returning charge of from £4 to
£5. It is doubtful whether State smelting works would
get over the difficulty. There would always be the risk
of failure through the appointment of incompetent
managers. Mining is excluded from Commonwealth
authority—why, no one seems to know. Were it other-
wise, it would he a desirable thing for the Commonwealth
to establish an up-to-date mining school. Connected with
it there should be a complete smelting-plant, which,
worked on fair commercial lines, would more than pay
for itself, and would, at the same time, give students
practical demonstrations of different processes of infin-
itely more value to them than all the text-books that
were ever printed or lectures delivered.—E» B.]

Now announced that Amy Oastles has entered into a

A farewell “At Home" was to be tendered—“tendered”
is the right word just here—to Grace Palotta, at Melb.
Athenaeum Hall, on Tuesday afternoon of this week—
“ At Homes ” run in all shapes, but they rarely take the
form of a benefit for anyone outside. The Palottian
function, which had Canon Tucker on the Committee,
was announced as “in aid of the poor of South Yarra,”
the proceeds of a small charge for admission being
dedicated to the cause. If the poor of Souih Yarra got
the whole of the takings at the doors it was well and
good, but if the expenses were deducted—well, then the
promotresses were merely obtaining Grace for their own
glorification in the sacred name of Charity.

this time be introduced with a “welcome” at Merizies’
Hotel. The welcome happens next Tuesday afternoon
(Bth), and the Wizard of the ’Cello makes his first re-ap-
pearance at the Town Hall in the evening or next
evening. On dit that the brilliant musician has a Sydney
innamorata.
“ Melb.” : I don’t say that the worthy and musical
Father Robinson meant to drive Amy Castles on to an
operatic career, but his actions have had that effect, an
outcome which would in most cases be viewed with
abhorrence by the rest of the priesthood. Let us hope
she will never have cause to regret leaving the humble
Bendigo cottage. Even Melba’s relatives are sorry that
the theatre engages her.
Maud Grantham and Lucy Barton are giving their
vocal and pianoforte recital at Glen’s Hall, Melbourne, on
Monday, the 7th. Hattenbach and F. North will assist
the pianist in one of her numbers, and H. Cohen is to
partake of a duet from “ Carmen” with Miss Grantham.
Also, the genial Hattenbach will give a ’cello solo. The
rest of the programme will be sung or played by the
young recitalists on their respective “ owns,” and they
await the verdict with hopeful perturbation.
Slapoffski’s orchestral conducting reminds one of Wil-
son Barrett as Hamlet, only that he acts ever so much
harder, with nods, and becks, and wreathed smiles ; also
frowns and whispers, while his favorite trick to secure a
peculiarly delicate effect is laying his left forefinger beside
his Hebraic nose.
“ E.F.” : Jimmy Atholwood is so good and persevering
an actor that it were a pity to let him be overpraised in
the dailies without telling him of his one notable defect.
Originally handicapped by a weak voice, Atholwood, in
making a specialty of old-man impersonations, has de-
veloped a certain fruity fulness of tone, but his enuncia-
tion is sadly lacking in keenness. He talks so like an
ancient that one can’t always catch the words. Wilson
Barrett, who pitches the voice higher, is frequently in-
distinct because of his naturally hollow rumbling utter-
ances, and Atholwood seems to have adopted the
same sort of old-age tone instead of striving for a more
penetrative effect. A few lessons in singing, from a good
teacher, might serve to get the artistic Jimmy’s voice out
of its present groove.
A London correspondent, one of the most competent
and honest of men, writes to a Sydney friend re
Australian vocalists visiting England : “ There is an im-
pression in Australia that it is difficult to get a hearing in
London. Nothing is easier. A few pounds will enable
an aspirant to get up his or her own concert at the
Beehstein or Steinway Halls—as easily and as cheaply
as at the Y.M.O.A. Hall in Sydney. Every week,
almost every day, we have a first appearance an-
nounced—it generally being also the last. It is hard to
conceive how self - measurement can be so inaccu-
rate, but much responsibility lies with those whe
send misleading reports to Australia. If by false
report it can be made believed that a well-known
Australian mediocrity has achieved a success in London,
it is easy to understand why so many Sydney and Mel-
bourne nonentities come here to do likewise. ’ Successful
Australians, and others of means or position here, have
to put up with much begging from their stranded com-
patriots. A few weeks ago an impudent paragraph
appeared in the columns of the British Australasian,
deprecating these ill-advised visits to Europe. Now, the
B.A. is the author itself of most of the mischief. Here is
a specimen par. : ‘ Miss sang with great success at
the Orchestral Concert on Thursday last. On the
previous Saturday she appeared at , and to-day
she is engaged to sing at a reception,’ &c., <fcc. These
trumpery engagements mean absolutely nothing, but
the young lady in question has money, and can possibly
afford to delude herself into believing she is making a
career. I was present at her first appearance, three years
ago. It was a dismal failure, and I absolutely refuse to
believe she has had one genuine payable engagement
since. Again, Mr. Arthur Deane has done well enough
with the Carl Rosa Opera co. to have been spared the
following paltry puff : ‘ Indeed, for sheer vocal quality
it is doubtful if Mr. Deane has any equal in England.’
The merest tyro in musical matters knows what a wild
statement that is. Finally—well, I could give many other
instances of the same sort of thing, but have no desire to
tread on too many people’s toes.”
“ Cupid ” : Would like to know if it is usual with Sydney
women to remove their boots in places of amusement.
Have lately sat next to three women who doffed either
one or both boots in the theatre, resuming the feet-
envelopes when the play was near an end. Presumably
it is an epidemic of tight boots (if the microbe of tight
boots could be traced and extirpated the world would be
much the better). All these women had reasonably
small feet (which suggests that they took off their boots
through vanity), and in two cases they had holes in their
stockings (which suggests that they didn’t, unless the
whiteness of the foot showing through the hole was what
they were proud of).
Alfred Moul—formerly of Melbourne—is the latest
chairman of the Alhambra (London) directorate.
Unknowingly to himself, Canon Ellacombe (England)
has put a big nail in the coffin of the Shacon Bakespeare
theory. He points out, in a recently-published book of
reminiscences, that the great dramatist is entirely silent
on architecture of every description. Of the ‘stately
edifices of England, Troy, Rome and Venice he never
offers so much as the slightest sketch ; whereas, to Bacon,
stately buildings were a delight. The latter has left us
the grand essay “ On Building,” which not only shows
his practical knowledge in joining stateliness and beauty
with perfect comfort, but also shows how the greatest
intellect can deal with what many would call only petty
details. Henry Irving relies on the fact that, in his
plays, Shakespeare was a master of exits and entrances—
matters about which Bacon could not know anything.

A Hospital Patient writes:—
‘ ‘ The Military Hospital, Whittington Barracks,
“ Lichfield, May 24, 1901.
“Dear Sirs, —I have been very ill in bed three

whilst in bed, but on standing up the swelling
immediately appears in the legs ; my face is also
puffy, and, in fact, water appears to be all over
my body. I have just returned from abroad, and
believe the climate has been the cause of my ill-
ness. Seeing your advertisement, I resolved to
write you for your Powder to see if it will relieve
me.—Yours truly,
“W. H. WILTON
“ (Corporal), Army Service Corps.”
♦

the third dose entirely removed the swellings, and

Constantly Used by the Royal Family.
KUTNOW’S POWDER is doubly efficient in Rheumatism and Gout. It dissolves
uric acid and phosphatic sediments as well as other products difficult of elimination, while at the
same time it exerts a moderately stimulant effect upon the renal cells, and thereby facilitates the
swift removal of insoluble materials from the body. Without such actions insoluble sub-
stances will precipitate in the kidneys and bladder. The intense suffering produced by stone, together with
consecutive pyelitis and cystitis, are avoided by prompt elimination. Unquestionably, although the speedy
removal of uric acid and other products of faulty tissue change is of conspicuous benefit, yet to pre-
vent their formation is a service still more important. This service is performed
by KUTNOW’S POWDER when it corrects those digestive failures which are re-
sponsible for the production of deleterious materials.—Fide Medical Record, Sept., 1901.
Procrastination in applying a safe and efficient remedy may mean incom-
prehensible mischief to those who suffer from derangement of the digestive or
excretory organs. Success in business or the professions, or in any walk in life,
depends very largely upon the state of health. Men who work with the brain
value health, and men who value health take KUTNOW’S POWDER. Any
one of the ailments mentioned above may develop into serious disease unless
checked in time. A Sluggish Liver, for instance, produces mental depression,
clogs up the bile-ducts, brings on a bilious condition, and creates excessive uric
acid. Excessive uric acid contaminates the blood, and breeds Gout, Rheu-
matism, Sciatica, and congests the kidneys. Congested kidneys bring on Stone
in the Bladder, Diabetes, Bright’s Disease, and vesical irritations generally.
KUTNOW’S POWDER is simply invaluable in the treatment of these
derangements, and will restore the bloom of vigorous health to the most debili-
tated patient. It is an exact reproduction of the famous medicinal merits of
the waters of the European Spas, minus any of their drastic or lowering
properties.
Beware of Fraudulent Imitations.
KUTNOW’S POWDER may be obtained in capsuled bottles, of all Chemists and Medi-
cine Vendors. See that the Registered Trade Mark, “ Hirschensprung ” (or Deer Leap), and
the autograph facsimile signature, “S. KUTNOW AND Co. (Ld. ), are on the label and oarton.
ONLY THESE ENSURE GENUINENESS. INSIST ON “ KUTNOW’S,” AND
FIRMLY REFUSE ANY SUBSTITUTES; THEY ARE WORTHLESS AND OFTEN
INJURIOUS.

The Battler’s Love-Story.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Did I ever tell ye me love-story?” said the
Battler. “ One night as I wus travellin’ up the
Western line bound fer Bourke—l lived at Bourke
then something went wrong with the lightin’ of
the car, and, bein’ in darkness, I couldn’t see me
fellow-passengers. ”
“ I don’t suppose you could,” chipped in the
hotel jester.
“ Howld yer m tg,” said the Battler; “Tm
tell in’ the story. Well, to continue the yarn.
When the train reached Katoomba, I thought I
cud make out the dim outline of a woman seated
at t’other end of the compartment—so I does a
little cough ; she coughed back .”
“ What then ?” said the hotel jester impatiently.
“Can’t yer hold yer howlin’ox-tongue?” said the
Battler, “ After a while she gets ter coughing
very bad and confidential-like, and I coughs re-
sponsive-like. I thought if we coughed at each
other often enough it might lead up to a con-
versation. When the train reached Lithgow I
ventured on tellin’ ’er about th’ necessity of keep-
ing the winder closed with such a bad cough, and
she played right into me hands; she arsked me to
close it for ’er.”
“ So you closed it ? ” said the jester.
“ If yer don’t stop interruptin’, I’ll fling this beer
over yer. After I closed the winder I took a seat
about a yard away from ’er. And when the train
passed a few more stations I ventured to edge up
against ’er.”
“Did yer?” said the public-house jester.
‘ ‘ Go-ter-’ell and don’t come back, for interruptin’.
Then I started toyin’ with ’er ’ands and after-
wards moved one arm round ’er waist, at the same
time givin’ ’er a little squeeze.”
“ Didn’t think yer had it in yer,” said the
jester.
“Ah ! that was when I was younger, and had
the fire of love in me. She was bound for Bourke,
too, so I arsked to see’er’ome. When we reached
our destination, I wus dead square with ’er. At
Bourke, we used to meet of an evenin’ and go for
walks tergether, and make love. I suppose you’ve
all made love some time or other ? ”
“ How do you do it ?” said the jester.
“ Don’t show yer howlin’, devastatin’ igno-
rance,” said the Battler. “ Well, one evening she
proposes that I marry ’er, and, likin’ her, I fixed
the day. When the weddin’ mornin’ came round
I sent a boy to ask ’er her full name, which I for-
got to inquire before. ”
“ Did she tell yer it?” said the hotel humorist,
excitedly.
“Shut up, will yer!” answered the Battler.
“The boy came back and told me’er name was
Mulligan. Then I starts thinkin’ about havin’
seen ’er face before, an’ it struck me, her name
bein’ Mulligan, the same asmeself, she might be a
relation or somethin’. An’then I began thinkin’
about a wife I once had. ‘Damn it,’ says I, ‘ she’s
the same identical as I wus hitched to ten years
ago. I remember —she cleared out with another
fellow and took all the furniture and me shirts
and Bs. 6d.’ ”
“ And did the wedding come off as arranged ? ”
inquired the funny man in impatience.
i'he Battler said nothing. He rose up and hit
the funny man hard on the jaw.

The eruption at Martinique came so swiftly
that search parties found the sentries at the
Governor’s residence erect and with their rifles
still in their hands. This is accounted for by the
assumption that the noxious gases brought instant
asphyxiation. The beautiful legend of Pompeii
re the stern, unflinching Roman soldier who,
rather than desert his post, remained there to die,
can now be consigned to oblivion with the other
exploded fairy tales of history. Probably lie
hadn’t time to run away.
Some time ago Wellington (M.L.) City
Council determined to furnish that wet, windy
city of narrow streets and muddy roads with an
electric tram service. Levels were taken and
elaborate plans prepared, when the. work was
suddenly hung up by an “ Order-in-Council.”
Meanwhile there are weird stories about wealthy
British and Yankee syndicates offering “splendid
terms” for a concession. The municipal authori-
ties have laid it down as a fundamental rule that
in future all public services shall belong to the
municipality, so they seem to be right; but the
people are wondering if the syndicate has managed
to nobble someone higher up. That mysterious
Order-in-Council is causing honest burghers to lose
sleep o’ nights.

board case, with tuning key, and the simple but complete instructions for

on the strings. When the Chords are played upon the strings and the tune

ALONG BACK IN THE BEGINNING OF THE EIGHTIES
Herr Rassmussen introduced his now world-famed Alfaline Herbal Remedies to the Australian public at 547 George
Street, Sydney. Australia was then flooded with American importations and proprietary remedies, manufactured
by doubtful persons with assumed names and fictitious addresses, who took the people’s money without
responsibility, and very often without giving them fair value for same.
HERR RASSMUSSEN BROUGHT ABOUT A REVOLUTION
by straightforward and fair dealings with the public, offering to stake his reputation and future if his remedies
were not as efficacious as he claimed them to be, accepting all responsibility, and willing to abide by the people’s
verdict.
THE HAPPY RESULT IS KNOWN TO EVERYONE.
Over twenty years have passed away, hundreds of remedies have come and gone—many of them were forced on
the public in numerous designing ways—thousands of pounds spent on advertising same with the assistance of the
world’s grea f est experts in laying traps for the unwary ; but the public would have none of them, and they have
disappeared as suddenly as a flash of lightning.
“THE ALFALINE REMEDIES EXCELL ALL OTHERS,”
and are well known to be more popular to-day than they have ever been. They have not only obtained, but
have retained the goodwill and confidence of the Australian public, and have become famous throughout the
civilized world. The first Sydney Office not only yet exists, but offices have been opened up for their sale at
Melbourne, Bri-bane, Adelaide, Wellington, N.Z., and in West Australia at Perth, Fremantle, Kalgoorlie and
Boulder City, with Agencies throughout the world.
LET THE PUBLIC JUDGE FROM THE FOLLOWING:—
The Alfaline Remedies have held their own for over twenty years in Australia, and Herr Rassmussen has
been awarded the Parisian Acadamies’ Gold Medal and Diploma of Honour ; Gold Medal, Sydney, and several Silver
Medals; honourably mentioned in almost every newspaper in Australia; received many thousands of unsolicited
testimonials—every one of the originals open for inspection at his Offices. The Alfaline Remedies hare never been
known to fail to cure in a single instance.

Merool ”: The backyards of three-

Most of England's old soldiers die in the
workhouse— and they don’t seem to fare much
better out here. One was taken—dying— to
Meibourne Hospital recently and refused admis-
sion- No room”; thence to the Central Mis-
sion—refused again, as being too ill; thence to the
watch-house, thence to gaol, where he passed
away 30 hours later.

In the recent case of a down-South farmer,
aged 71, v. a neighbor for assault and battery, it
was shown that while the venerable plaintiff was
on the ground, having his ribs kicked in and being
chipped with a tomahawk, defendant was making
a tremendous ado about his sublime belief in
“ British fair play.”

H. WALKER, Balmain, Sydney.”

A Miscalculation.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Harky’ Tiro rnlky was a farrrjer’s son. When Ins
father died he had no farm—a bank had it. Then
an uncle, who had been “in trade,” died and left
him a business he didn’t want; so he realised it
for cash down. Leaving the northern districts
of Victoria, he looked for another farm in a
moister climate. He lighted on Gippsland, and
there found all the moisture he required, but not
a farm that he had sufficient capital to buy. All
the good land was either in the hands of the
banks, of the men who had kept out of the banks,
or of land-jobbers who held for a rise. One place
of 500 acres almost reached his ideal. It was
between two creeks, which flowed about half-a-
rnile apart, and was bordered on one side, by the foot-
hills of a Baw Baw spur, undulating gently. The
heavy timber indicated unmistakably the quality
of the soil that nourished the great trees. His.
yeoman soul thrilled at the sight. The land was
owned by Henderson. He had held the land
many years, paid the taxes —and cursed his luck.
Men came and settled all round him, and he had
smiled at his prospective unearned increment
which never came. A railway was built within
five miles of him ; still the land did not rise one
shilling above what it cost to keep. A dozen pur-
chasers came and offered 3 per cent, on his bargain
—he wanted 30 per cent. At last when Thornley
came and offered him 10 per cent., he closed on
him with a snap. When the neighbors heard of
the bargain the only one who didn’t think Thorn-
ley a fool was himself. Henderson looked for-
ward with confidence to getting his land back,
plus the deposit of £3OO.
Harry bought the land at the end of the
Autumn. In the Spring, Henderson was cal-
culating on the chance of getting it again.
He saw 300 acres of trees already yellow from
ringbarking, and a dozen men pegging away at
the scrub. Sweet visions of Harry in a lunatic-
asylum softened his cynical smile, which became
almost jolly when lie heard the comments of the
neighbors. They told him Harry was mad, for
when the timber was dry, it would be twice as
costly to cut up for burning. That made him
anxious. And the quantity ! —Trees in battalions,
some of them nearly 300 ft. high, with scrub in
some places piled 10ft. high—and all dying.
In the middle of a small cleared patch was
Harry’s dwelling. Henderson looked at it curi-
ously. It was built entirely of sheet bark, includ-
ing the c.iimney. Besting on the outside -of the
bark were saplings ; from underneath the bark
protruded the ends of parallel saplings, which
were pegged together with wooden pegs. Even
the door was bark, hung on hinges of bark. There
was not a nail in the whole structure, and it was
built from end to end with only an axe and an
auger for tools. It looked neat and cool, and,
from his experience an hour before in the iron-
roofed gridiron of the next farm, Henderson could
not help thinking it looked sensible. But com-
mon sense was the last quality he came to see in
Harry, and he looked relieved when he saw there
was not a single head of stock or a farm imple-
ment on the place ; there were only a grindstone,
some axes, half-a-dozen pickaxes, and a dray.
He looked in vain for the horse.
The next deposit was due in a week, and he
might as well find out at once that Harry had it.
I’his was surely his right. When Harry came, he
was surprised to see no trace of anxiety in him.
He went at once to the point.
“I suppose you know, Mr. Thornley, your next
deposit is due on the 17th ? ”
He said it in a manner indicating that he was
sure Harry did not possess it.
“I am quite aware of that,” said Harry, in a
manner indicating that he was nettled. Hender-
-33 l thought he was blustering.
“Look here, Mr. Thornley, how many deposits
have you paid?”
“ As many as will pay for this land.”
“I suppose you know how much it will cost
you ? ”
“ Perfectly.”
“You showed me your bank-account of £1100.”
“Well?” .
“How much is it costing you to get all that
timber rung, and that scrub cut ? ”
“£500.”
“Then, when you have paid that, and me the
next deposit, you will have nothing left.”
“ Well ?”
“There’s no well about it. It’s all wrong. It
will cost me £6OO to get all that dead timber
away.”
“ Cost you ? ”
“Yes, cost me; for, mind me, if you cannot
pay me the final £1125, the land is mine again six
months from now.”
“ Meanwhile, it is my property, isn’t it ? ”
“Well?”
“Well, just you get off it, before I kick you
off !”
Henderson looked like a vicious cat at the
brawny farmer, then went quickly. Harry
smiled as he reflected that a man might have two
1 tanking accounts, and that he was not going to
be “ stuck ” for the final payment.
Two years befo e Harry had bought Hender-
son’s property, the seasons had set in dry, a third
followed, and a fourth promised to be as dry as
the previous three. Gippsland felt the drought
less severely than the rest of the province ; but it
told even there; although the grass was nearly as
plentiful as in fatter seasons, it was dry earlier,
and the ringed timber was dead sooner.
With outraged feelings to stir his mind, Hen-
derson noted these things, till they suggested a
manner of revenge, which would ruin Harry, by
destroying all the timber, which he evidently
reckoned on selling to meet his payments, and
also give him back his land for another gullible
young man to buy. Nothing in the world was
safer. For six months he slept and dreamt
pleasantly on the idea, till he confirmed himself
in his purpose.
For three days it was cool with southerly winds ;
the fourth was calm; the fifth warm with easterly
winds; the sixth hot with northerly winds; on
the seventh and eighth it was blazing,
On the ninth a fire broke out. Henderson was
high up the valley when it started, through his
absent-mindedly dropping a lighted match. He
tried his best to put it out, but the grass was high,
the dead-leaves thick, and the wind strong. In
10 minutes 20 acres of scrub were blazing ; in 20
minutes the forest was roaring ; in three more it
had leaped the road in front of Harry’s property,
and was into his dead scrub and timber.
At the first sign of smoke Harry and his men
made for the East Greek. When they reached it
the fire was already pouring over the road. The
green scrub on the creek banks began to hiss and
shrivel in the heat; they started crawling,
chokingly, up the hill, and when they reached tne
top, lay there, gasping and panting. They were
joined in half-an-hour by Henderson and two
of his friends. All looked silently.over the blazing
valley. They were blackened and scorched
with - the heat and smoke, but forgot
their burns in the awe of the magnificent
sight at their feet. Huge tongues of flame
leaped like great yellow snakes into the sky ; falling
trees every.moment threw jets of sparks high into
the air, as they crashed to the ground, ihose
which stood were huge fire columns, with ragged
banners of flame streaming to leeward, thrashing
out streams of fie v sparks into the wind. Hour
after hour they sat on the hill, till darkness fell,
when every spark and every gleaming coal added
its light to make, the night as bright as day.
Harry’s land shone out more brightly than all the
rest by reason of the dry wood on it; it was like
one great glowing coal.
Henderson watched his mischief with something
like terror gripping his heart, wondering where it
would end. Onwards and sideways flew the fire,
spreading till nothing but fire and smoke could be
seen climbing every hill within sight. When the
sun rose, and its rays did their best to penetrate
the smoky haze above them, they showed not a
single tree, nor a stick of scrub standing on
Harry’s land ; it was as clear as if a gigantic
sickle had gone over it. Even the creek banks
were bare. On the neighboring farms that could
be seen at all, only a few of the most vigorous of
the green trees were s anding. They were leafless
columns of ebony, and all the scrub beneath them
had been swept away.
It was one of Harry’s workmen who first began
to reckon up the effects. “What about your
fences, boss ? ”
“They had to be rebuilt, anyway.
“ But the hut ? ’’
“ I can replace it with a house. It only cost a
week’s work.” ,
“ The tools though ?”
“£5 will replace them.”
“ And our clothes ? ”
“ I will fit you out again.”
One of Henderson’s friends who had been
listening, turned to Harry and said in surprise,
“But doesn’t this mean nearly ruin to you, Mr.
Thornley ? ”
“Ruin ! It’s simply made me. I gave £3 ss.
an acre for the land, and it’s worth £lO now.
Then he turned and looked at Henderson.
“I’ve got your money ready when it is due. I’d
have started the fire myself if someone else hadn’t
done it.” When he saw Henderson’s expression
he laughed.
W. BRENTA.
A Man’s Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ I thought it only fair to tell you,” the man was
saying. “ I don’t think you really do care much.”
“It was only fair,” the woman answered,
quietly. “And it was only natural that you
should grow tired. I will say ‘ good-bye 5 now.”
“Oh, well, not ‘good-bye’”! He was in an
awkward position, and unreasonably felt irritated
at her calmness. “We shall see each other some-
times, I hope. ”
“Yes, certainly—if you wish it.”
* * * *
“She does not care a scrap,” he thought. “I
am almost sorry I released her.”
“I love him! I shall always love him!” she
kept repeating to herself; “ and he is tired of me.
Ah, fool! fool that I was to lose his love—that to
me is the dearest thing in life ! But he is a man,
and I ought to have remembered it. But I will
remember it now, and now, when I regain his
love, I shall keep it while life lasts! And,” she
sighed, “ now for work ! Music instead of study ;
dressmaking instead of writing.”
* » * *
“ How strange ! ” he muttered, as he watched
the dancers. “ How strange that she should come
out like this ! She was so quiet and demure all
the time we were engaged, and now—why ! she is
flirting outrageously.” He gnawed his moustache
in anger as he watched his cast-off love—the belle
among numerous beauties—graciously receiving
the homage of many men, when he felt some-
how that she ought to be pining in some lonely
corner. “ She did not care, after all/’ he groaned.
“ May I have your programme ? ” he asked boldly,
although in his heart lie expected a refusal.
“Certainly,” she answered sweetly; and a
while afterwards they floated along in a divine
waltz. “She is beautiful,” he thought; “ I believe
I love her more than ever I did, but I know she
does not love me now—if ever she really did.”
“How can I bear it?” she thought. “His
touch almost kills. I wish I could love him less.”
“You are happy to-night,” he whispered.
“The men are crazy about you.”
“ Yes,” she answered. “And you ?”
“Just now,” he said, with an admiring glance
straight into her eyes. Then—“ Are you glad
that we parted ? ”
“Oh yes ! our engagement was all a mistake,”
with a sweet smile.
“ The parting was a mistake,” he murmured;
and wondered what meant that inscrutable smile
that played around her lips.
* * * *
“The way to win a man’s love,” said the
woman, “ is to win other men’s admiration.”
“Can you never forgive me?” he whispered to
the brilliant woman who had been charming the
company with her singing, and dazzling them
with her beauty. “You never look at me since
you have all these men at your feet. I have loved
you so long, and, even in face of your repeated
refusals, I love you more than ever ! ”
“ Because of them ! ” she murmured to herself.
But letting her eyes rest on his with all the long-
suppressed lovelight shining through them, she
said, softly : “ Hugh, Ido forgive you ; but oh !
it is too la'e—now—for both of us.”
“ Why too late ? ” he cried, eagerly.
“Because—l have promised to marry Mr.
Grey.”
“The way to keep a man’s love,” said the
woman, with a pathetic little smile, “is to put
yourself beyond hrs reach.”
ALTA CAYLEY.
From 3400 tons in 1899-1900 S. Aus.
local production of artificial manures has got to
10,500 tons in 1901-2. Imports in the same time
have moved from 21,200 to 27,000 tons ; so there
is still room for local development. In the “ seed-
ing ” season just finished the S.A. farmer has
spent £173,000 on fertiliser; 10 years ago he
knew no more about phosphates than he did
about Sanscrit. The State, whose experts boomed
the business, early passed a law requiring the
sampling of the home product, as well as every
shipment offered ; and, though last year it cost
only £B5 to administer, the Act is said to do its
work. The average “dressing” of fertiliser is
about 851 b. per acre.

Brawling.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Stir in the street and clamor in the dark ;
Swirl of hot brows and deep li ps cursing white •
Blood where the red knife left its plunging mark,’
Death on the brown cheeks, something still and
stark,
Slain for the honor of our mistress of a night.
Struth, he, a bastard of the Dago-swine,
Full from the wine-press, savage in the flush
Of a madness leaping, eyes aflame, ashine,
Leered to the Glory of the Boulevard divine;
Snatched at her red lips with a reeling rush !
Daring greatly, swift he took her close and hard,
Ere our lips could blast him or a blade might
stay.
With his arms entwining, could he see tp guard,
Death that swooned upon him, in the clamor evil-
starred,
Clutching at his heart-strings as he broke away ?
Sweet in the noon of night, full of tingling stars,
She, best of all the world, called us down the
stair;
We. froth of all the world, faces chained with scars,
Foul from the beaten deck and the clanging spars,
Followed down the streetway, playing with her
hair.
She had flashing fingers, browning in the grime
(Lord, we loved her full eyes, drowsy eyes and
deep !)
Was our kin a danger ? Was our love a crime ?
Is her scarlet story blazoned ’gainst the walls of
Time ?
If her God forsake, we at least will guard and
keep !
To a hell with such things, she is ours alone !
Keen the dagger edges when the slighting word
is said,
He, the thirsty giant, ere the kiss had colder
grown,
Felt the fine ton of an arm savagely up-thrown,
Caught the reeling stroke that split the chestnut
head.
Stir in the street, a-dying down and still—
Now for her honor dear he hath other ways,
Where the wings of Silence lean for ever, full of
ill,
Past the outer, outer sunlight to the deep in-
visible—
Ha ! to see her shrinking, and her sleeping eyes
ablaze !
Hear her laughing, laughing, with her love about
them all,
Praising his swift prowess—and his joy is fin 6
to see !
As he leans against her, straight and staunch and
tall,
Blushing to her deep eyes when the loving glances
fall.
Ah, the place of all proud places where the
bravest fain would be !
Where the bravest fain would be, he shall bask
the summer night,
In the splendour of her favor (who shall swear
’twere bitter-sweet ?)
There to list her lavish praises in a wonderful
delight;
With his beard against her forehead, golden-
brown against the white,
Where the time and place for thought of the
dead eyes in the street ?
C. L. RIVERS.
The Track-Horse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ I never travelled with a horse but once,” said
Tarkalson, “an’l don’t want-any-more-~of-him-.-
He’s a nuisance on a dry track—an’ they’re all dry
out back. Yer always askin’ about grass, an’
lookin for grass 5 an’ when you’ve got grass
there s bound to be no water. I reckon a horse-
man’s never troubled with want o’ work or tucker
—his greatest concern is finding grass. When he
strikes a patch he’s got to hobble-out whether he
likes it or not. Then, instead of feedin’ to keep
his condition up, the brute goes ramblin’ about,
takin a mouthful about every ten yards. You
think he’s makin’ off, an’ turn him back. When
you’re asleep he’ll go some other way—you don’t
know where. Best way is not to turn him back
at all. If he’s a rambler, hobble him short, or
side-line him with a bullock-chain. In the mornin’
you’ll see his track. Of course you’ll know where
to find him —at the other end of it.
“A good campih’-horse is like a good mate—
he’s precious scarce. Mostly you’re up all night
listenin for the bell, an’ it’s surprisin’ what a
variety of sounds resembles that bell o’ your’n.
It’s a burden 011 yer mind all night. In the morn-
ing, you go to ketch him—where you heard his
bell just before daylight—an’ he ain’t there. You
walk back 10 miles, to the last gate you come
through, an’ find him with his head hangin’
over the fence. You’ve tramped a day’s
journey after him—if he’s broke his hob-
bles, you run yerself blind ketchin’ him,
an you lead him a day’s journey to camp. Then
you start. Many a poor devil starts goin’ mad.
Someone’s ratted him, p’raps, while he’s been
away ; or crows an’ dogs have copped his tucker ;
or the wind’s set the fire blazin’ an’ burnt every-
thing. A horse is right enough where there’s whips
of grass, an’ water every mile. He travels more
at night-time then.
“This cuddy of mine was a pot-gutted bay.
Called him Nugget. He was a great horse —m
size. Frisky as a monkey, an’ could gallop like a
racer—if you wanted to yard him. Hidin’ him—
he was too slow for a funeral. He always had his
eye on me, though, an’ if he saw I wasn’t watchin’
he’d jump two ways at once. If that shifted me
—it mostly did—he’d down with his head an’ give
three sudden bucks. No occasion to give inore’n
three. . By that time I’d have my second seat,
an be spi'tin’ out teeth ail’ dirt an’ grass. I once
aca illar in two, spearin’ down on him
fiud le 1 ke. I had a great respect for Nugget in
them days.
‘‘Travellin’ from Nanango to Tenterfield put a
set on him. Terribly dry it was, with not as much
grass as would keep a billy-goat. Chuck a log on
that fire, mate, Gettin’ a bit chilly.
Well, Nugget got that blamed poor that he
had to stand broadside to the sun to make a
shadder. Then he’d try to get in it, an’ when he
lost it by turnin’ end-on, he’d whinny for it. He
was a bit shook-up in his intellect. An’ his head
it looked the size of a house, with a lip on the
endofitthat’dtrip a bullock-waggon. Worse’n
a « j d £°t a sore back, an’ swelled wither, an’
Puffed, legs, an’ tender feet, an’ was girth-galled
both sides. I useter have him padded, too, fit to
break him down. Tried all sorts of remedies—
from axle-grease to Stockholm tar ; but I couldn’t
effect a cure nohow. An’ darn me if I wasn’t
travellm’ as a vet'in’ry surgeon at this time, too !
‘I never went through a town without a dread
of bein’ had up. As for Nugget, he’d sidle into
every house. If a woman came out he’d whinny
at her, an’ stand. I’d ask the way to the baker’s.
If I sat there talking,- Nugget would look round
mournfully, an’ I’d fancy he was sayin’, ‘Why
j:' ie don’t you get off!’ He’d smell my boots,
fust one an’ then the other ; an’ by-’n’-bye he’d
cop one m his mouth an’ try to pull it out of the
stirrup. I’d start him again, an’ he’d sidle across
wi 10 n< i place. I’d ask for the butcher’s-shop.
When I’d asked for every blessed place in the
town, I’d get off an’ drag him out of it. I was
ashamed o’ that moke if ever a man was.
He seemed to do things a-purpose. When no one
could see him he’d go fusrate. Soon’s a cove
come along he’d slow down to a crawl. Never
met a horseman wot hadn’t to crawl round
IS ugget. Everyone would say, ‘ Yer nag looks
poor.’ Looks poor ! ’Struth, they uster have
me fairly jumpin’.
“Then We come into flat, swampy country—
plenty of green feed. I gave him a spell there—
an he got the scours. That left him so weak that
he staggered when I put the saddle on him. I led
wi* r a al1 ’ carried the pack meself.
v\ hen we got to dry country again, the clods an’
stones bruised,his feet till he couldn’t scarcely
walk. The swamps had softened ’em, an’ he
limped an’ grunted something awful. I’d nave
left him long ago only for the saddle. That was
worth a pound. I uster envy the tramps—walkin’
calmly by with bluey up. Seemed like Heaven
to have no worry about grass an’ knocked-up
horses. Take my tip for it. With a pair o’ big
feet like mine an’ your’n, a man can get along
surprisin’ well without the cuddy. Gorany tea
m that billy ?
One day I was ridin’ up to a house for bread.
JN ugget was goin’splendid. He must’ve thought
it was home, seein’ ladies outside. I’d got pretty
close up when—down goes Nugget. Turned me a
turtle spank among the petticoats 1 An’ how they
did yell ! ‘Oh dear, I hope lie’s not hurt ! ’ says
??> e ’ ' l°°k at that poor horse ! ’ says another.
Poor, poor thing ! ’ An’ everybody looked at
JN ugget. His legs was spread out, his head down,
an' his bottom lip hangin’ a yard. I asked the
way to So-and-so’s. Then I hauled Nugget round,
pulled him down the road, an’ let him go for
another spell. After you with that there fire-
stick.
“1 uster notice flasn biokes wavin’ han’kerchiefs
to girls as they went along. Seemed fine fun to
me, an’ I reckoned I’d have a joke, too. I hadn’t
as muoh sense then as a respectable man oughter
have. There was a house half-a-inile off, an’ a
girl at the door. Just the thing! I hauled out
a sweat-rag an’ waved it. She waved back, then
run inside. Presently she comes out again, with
the old woman—puttin’ on her specs.—the old
man, three more girls, an’ Loramighty knows how
many kids. I felt soared, but was jest liftin’ the
sweat-rag for another shake when—down went
Nugget! Dash his old hide, but lie shot me
summersettin’ through the dust like a tumblin’
Tommy. Dunno how they took it. Didn’t look.
Slipped on in a hurry, and stared straight ahead
for hours. Damn me ! but that did make me feel
small.
Ihe last station we passed, me an’ Nugget,
was Ellangowan. Was close to the Condamine
then. There was a hut on the bank, so I reckoned
on havin’ a week’s spell—ketchin’ cod, I fastened
the reins to the stirrup-iron, so’s he’d dodder
along in front.
“ Hell yer wot a cantankerous cuss he was ! He
actually tried to bolt with the pack ! He did get
away—into a swamp. He’s there yet. Bog! It’d
bog a duck. In two minutes all yer could see was
his head an’ the top of the pack. I got that out.
The poor brute whinnied to me, too. Thought he
was bein’ let go. But it warn’t no use. His
bones is there still not a rifle-shot from Ellan-
gowan. I’ve stuck hard and fast to Biuoher since,
and travelled strickly per boot. There’s nothing
to lick it in the dry country.”
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
Sterling Merton, the valuable crank who
had “Plant Trees ” printed on his notepaper and
who instituted Arbor Days in the U.S., died
lately. Grover Cleveland made him chief of his
Agricultural Dept., and there he worked his
beneficent fad as few men had the chance.
The poorest soil in Maoriland is found in
Canterbury. The Plains are frightfully stony;
there are more shells than loam scattered along
their shingly terraces, and it is only in spots that
the ground is good for either man or beast—com
pared, that is, with the rich soils of further north
and'south. It really grows stones. Writer has
seen a man pick his land clean of boulders, pile
them along the road side, and next year, without
any ploughing or disturbance, a fresh lot of
rocks and boulders has come from—Heaven
knows where. At present Christchurch is a
considerable manufacturing town, if it isn’t the
manufacturing centre of the islands, but again it
is handicapped by having no coal worth speaking
of, and its fuel has to be brought from the West
Coast round the north end of the island. When
the Midland railway is completed it will make
little difference; the haulage through and
over the Southern Alps will keep up the
price. Yet Canterbury industries struggle along
on the old coal basis, heedless of the source of
cheap and inexhaustible power that roars down
from a hundred solid glaciers far up among the
Alps, through great shingle cliffs, dotted over with
cabbage-trees, down to where the surf thunders
on the bow of the Ninety-Mile Beach. All the
rivers of Canterbury are swift-flowing, and they
never go dry, though in a cold, dry winter, when
there is little sun to melt the glaciers, they get
very low for a few weeks. Why Canterbury
struggles along, paying through the nose for its
coal supply, when Nature has placed the-means of
obtaining a cheap and inexhaustible supply of
electric power every few miles is a mystery. A
province with a big, swift river about every 10
miles of its length should be simply dotted over
with factories worked by electricity.
